==PAGE:5==
Abstract
In the eﬀort of developing a highly speciﬁc treatment option with minimal side eﬀects to-
wards inhibiting metastatic progression of tumours, a polymeric nanobiocatalytic system
that is capable of cleaving the pro-inﬂammatory cytokine IL-6 is designed.
By conjugation of serine protease Cathepsin G (Cat G) onto poly(D,L-lactic-co-glycolic
acid) (PLGA) polymer-based nanoparticle (NP) surfaces, a highly potent particle with
speciﬁcity towards pro-inﬂammatory cytokine Interleukin 6 (IL-6) is developed.
IL-6
plays a major role in maintaining a pro-inﬂammatory, tumour-promoting microenviron-
ment with its ability to activate the JAK-STAT3 pathway and promote NF-κB expression,
involved in tumour cell proliferation, diﬀerentiation, survival, angiogenesis and metastasis
[1, 2]. IL-6 ablation is therefore a natural target in the eﬀort to restrict metastasis of solid
tumours.
Through application of robust molecular biological approaches such as SDS-PAGE and
enzyme kinetics studies, Cat G is seen to present signiﬁcant catalytic activity in cleaving
IL-6. SDS-PAGE has been shown to present an useful technique in detection of intact
IL-6 cleavage just after 15 minutes of incubation with free Cat G. Michaelis-Menten-based
enzyme kinetics studies have indicated optimal Cat G activity with determined kinetics
parameters comparable to previously established literature values (Vmax = 0.140 ± 0.018
mM/min, KM = 0.196 ± 0.045 mM and kcat = 0.663 ± 0.086 s−1).
After proof-of-concept SDS-PAGE and enzyme kinetics studies demonstrating the eﬀec-
tive cleavage of IL-6, Cat G has been conjugated to the surface of polymeric nanoparticles
(NPs) using a variety of bioconjugation techniques. These preliminary studies have shown
that Cat G still remains active as shown with in vitro biological investigations, and fu-
ture work will involve the optimization of this process. In summary, this thesis presents
extensive proof-of-concept method development for Cat G cleavage of IL-6 and sets the
scene for further eﬃcacy studies towards in vivo cancer models.
==PAGE:6==
Resum´e
I bestræbelsen p˚a at udvikle en yderst speciﬁk behandlingsmulighed med minimale bi-
virkninger med form˚al at inhibere metastatisk udvikling af tumorer, et polymeric nanobio-
katalytisk system er blevet designet. Ved konjugering af serine protease Cathepsin G (Cat
G) p˚a PLGA polymer-baseret nanopartikel (NP) overﬂader, er en yderst potent partikel
med speciﬁcitet mod pro-inﬂammatorisk cytokine Interleukin 6 (IL-6) blevet udviklet.
IL-6 spiller en markant rolle i bevarelsen af et pro-inﬂammatorisk, tumor-promoverende
mikromiljø med dets evne til at aktivere JAK-STAT3 pathwayen og promovere NF-κB
ekspression, involveret i tumor celledeling, -diﬀerentiering, -overlevelse, angiogenese og
metastase [1, 2]. IL-6 er derfor et naturligt m˚al i forsøget p˚a at begrænse metastase af
maligne tumorer.
Efter anvendelse af robuste biomolekylærer tilgangsvinkler s˚asom SDS-PAGE og en-
zymkinetikstudier ses det at Cat G besidder bestandig katalytisk aktivitet i kløvning
af IL-6. SDS-PAGE har vist sig som en brugbar teknik i detektion af kløvning af in-
takt IL-6 efter blot 15 minutters inkubation med frit Cat G. Michaelis-Menten-baserede
enzymkinetiskstudier har indikeret fremtrædende Cat G aktivitet med bestemte kinetik
parametre sammenlignelige med tidligere etablerede værdier i literaturen (Vmax = 0.140
± 0.018 mM/min, KM = 0.196 ± 0.045 mM and kcat = 0.663 ± 0.086 s−1).
Efter proof-of-concept SDS-PAGE og enzymkinetikstudier der har demonstreret eﬀektiv
kløvning af IL-6 er Cat G blevet konjugeret til overﬂaden af polymeriske nanopartikler
(NPer) ved brug af en variet´e af biokonjugeringsteknikker. Disse foreløbige studier har
vist at Cat G forbliver aktivt som vist ved in vitro biologiske undersøgelser and fremtidigt
arbejde vil involvere optimeringen af denne proces. I resum´e, dette speciale præsenterer
vidtg˚aende proof-of-concept metode udvikling for Cat G kløvning af IL-6 and sætter
scenen for yderligere eﬀektstudier mod in vivo cancer modeller.

Preface
The following makes up the master thesis entitled ’Polymeric Nanobiocatalysts for Ab-
lation of Cancer Inﬂammatory Signalling’ by Jeppe Sejerø Holm. The project was con-
ducted at the Department of Micro- and Nanotechnology (Technical University of Den-
mark).
First and foremost, I would like express my most sincere gratitude to my supervisor Asso-
ciate Professor Nazila Kamaly, Ph.D, for providing me with great inspiration, most valu-
able scientiﬁc discussions, indispensable moral support and consistent guidance through-
out the process of this thesis. Furthermore, I would like to thank the multitude of PhD-
students and postdocs in the BioNanoMat- and CBIO groups in building 423 for allowing
me to constantly feed oﬀtheir extensive knowledge, for their indisputable assistance and
for putting up with my relentless questions and enquiries.

Chapter 1
Introduction
On a worldwide scale, cancer is the second leading cause of death and responsible for 8.8
millions deaths a year [3]. Alone in Denmark, cancer aﬀects one third of the population
[4]. With lung, liver, colorectal, stomach and breast cancers being the most prominent
cancer types, cancer metastasis is seen to cause more than 90 % of cancer-related deaths,
thus providing an enormous problem in cancer treatment [3, 5]. Due to the extent of
metastasis-related death, it thus provides a high impact target for focusing of anti-cancer
treatment [6].
Due to the impact of cancer on the human population and the size of the anti-cancer drug
market, enormous research funds and focus has been put into treating cancer. Previously,
small molecule drugs have consituted the majority of developed cancer drugs, but the vast
majority of existing drugs for cancer treatment feature signiﬁcant system toxicity, side-
and non-speciﬁc eﬀects [7, 8]. To increase eﬃcacy and limit side-eﬀects, highly speciﬁc
biologics such as monoclonal antibodies (mAbs), enzymes and proteins have been focused
on in clinical development due to their potent speciﬁcity [9].
Pro-inﬂammatory cytokines, such as Interleukin 6 (IL-6) play a major role in promot-
ing and maintaining the inﬂammatory environment involved in tumour promotion and
metastasis [1, 2]. IL-6, visualised in Figure 1.1, is naturally involved as a key component
in the innate immune response, but is seen to be immensely upregulated in tumours and
in serum blood levels during various types of cancers [1, 12]. As will be elaborated, by
persistent activation of intracellular transcription factors such as STAT3 and NF-κB in-
volved in cell proliferation, diﬀerentiaion and survival, IL-6 presents oncogenic impact by
its eﬀects as a pro-tumourigenic extracellular signalling cytokine [1, 2, 13].
Multiple approaches to reducing the carcinogenic eﬀects of IL-6 signalling exist. How-
ever, in order to fully ablate rather than merely short-term inhibiting these eﬀects of
pro-inﬂammatory IL-6, irreversible IL-6 destruction is targeted here. By development of
polymeric nanobiocatalysts for complete disintegration of IL-6, the aim of highly speciﬁc
eﬀect towards IL-6 while limiting the side-eﬀects typically seen for anti-cancer treatment
can be achieved. As one of the key advantages gained by utilising nanobiocatalysis is the
ability to ensure sustained, long-term eﬀects of enzymatic activity by administration of
low quantities of therapeutic, the developed biocatalytic nanoparticles (NPs) potentially
allow for continuous impact towards IL-6 and is therefore of major focus. Polymeric nano-
1
==PAGE:22==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Figure 1.1: Interleukin-6. Modiﬁed RSCB PDB-ﬁle 4CNI visualised by use
of PyMOL [10, 11].
materials provides a promising platform for development of spherical NPs with catalytic
eﬃciency. Integration of biodegradable polymers as core components allows for utilisation
of high surface-to-volume ratios of polymeric NPs while exploiting the enhanced perme-
ability and retention (EPR) eﬀect in passive targeting of solid tumours [14, 15]. It is
therefore hypothesised that the targeted, successful development of polymeric nanobio-
catalysts for ablation of IL-6-related cancer inﬂammatory signalling is achievable and that
these can induce prominent eﬀects towards cleaving and degrading IL-6. Development of a
such nanobiocatalytic system with the ability to destruct cancer inﬂammatory signalling
is therefore targeted in this work, as it would be with great potential in reducing and
eliminating tumour cell proliferation, diﬀerentiation and metastasis, ultimately providing
a key anti-cancer prospect with numerous applications in cancer treatment, regardless of
cancer type.
Thesis overview
The goal of this thesis and work conducted here is to develop polymeric nanobiocatalysts
with enzymatic activity towards disruption of IL-6. A schematic overview of the process
of development as well as the application of the NP-system towards cleaving IL-6 is illus-
trated in Figure 1.2. The successfully developed nanobiocatalytic NP will be comprised
of a polymeric biodegradable core prepared by nanoprecipitation. Subsequently, enzymes
will be functionalised onto the NP-surfaces. By biological assays, activity of developed
NPs towards IL-6 cleavage will be investigated. The speciﬁc steps in NP development in
addition with experiments involving NP bioactivity assessment will be described in detail
during the thesis.
2
==PAGE:23==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
In the following, ﬁrst a theoretical background is provided in order to understand the
required immunological aspects as well as the various component involved in the devel-
opment of the nanobiocatalytic system, such as state-of-the-art within existing anti-IL-6
treatment, enzymes for IL-6 cleavage, nanomedicine and polymeric, biodegradable NPs.
Proof-of-concept for detection and assessment of enzymatic cleavage of IL-6 follows. This
chapter is structured to investigate the applicability of a series of experimental tech-
niques potentially useful for determining the grade and rate of IL-6 destruction. For each
technique, the methodology behind and results yielded are covered before discussing the
potential applicability of the respective technique in this thesis.
The development of enzyme-conjugated polymeric NPs is outlined subsequently. Both the
progresses of NP core development optimisation as well as investigation into the feasibility
of utilising various enzyme bioconjugation techniques are covered and investigated thor-
oughly. By applying the techniques, for which proof-of-concepts has been determined, the
biological activity of successfully prepared NPs will be assessed. Ultimately, the catalytic
eﬃciency of developed NPs is discussed and the potential future prospects and appli-
cation of the developed nanobiocatalytic system concludes the thesis. All experimental
procedures and details are included in a separate chapter at the back of thesis and will
be referred to as appropriate throughout the thesis.
Figure 1.2: Schematic overview of the process of nanobiocatalytic NP devel-
opment and application in cleaving IL-6.

==PAGE:24==
==PAGE:25==
Chapter 2
Background
To understand the design, mechanism and eﬀects of the NP system to be developed
it is necessary to grasp the theoretical foundation behind this concept. The scientiﬁc
background is therefore presented in the following chapter.
First, an introduction to
inﬂammation in cancer and role of IL-6 as a pro-inﬂammatory cytokine is described.
Secondly, previous treatment options towards IL-6 inhibition is touched upon as well as the
possible enzymatic cleavage of IL-6 is assessed. Finally, the concepts of nanobiocatalysis,
biodegradable polymeric NPs and their advantages are covered.
2.1
Inﬂammation and cancer
Numerous extracellular factors contribute to providing a tumour-promoting microenvi-
ronment and the interplay between immune cells, growth factors, cytokines etc.
is a
complex system [2]. To maintain ideal, pro-inﬂammatory conditions for tumour devel-
opment, multiple individual factors take part [2]. Although many relationships between
these tumour-promoting factors remain not yet fully understood, a series of immunolog-
ical signalling molecules are believed to interact and play a part in cancer development.
A selection of the so-far known immunological components involved or aﬀected in cancer
development together with their impact is illustrated in Figure 2.1 [2].
It has previously been shown that an inﬂammatory microenvironment can induce and aid
numerous steps in the initiation and development of cancerous tumours [16]. Through
the recognition of pathogenic PAMPs from bacteria and vira, pattern recognition recep-
tors (PRRs) can be activated leading to an inﬂammatory, immunological response and
production of oncogenic transcription factors such as such as NF-κB [16]. However also
external factors, e.g. tobacco smoke, can initiate and lead to lung cancer, whereas inter-
nal factors such as obesity-induced chronic inﬂammation can cause liver and pancreatic
cancer. This indicates that a diverse range of factors can initiate chronic inﬂammation [1].
IL-6 is seen to be a key inﬂuencer in maintaining a tumourigenic microenvironment by
acting as a pro-inﬂammatory cytokine [1]. IL-6 is involved in activation of multiple intra-
cellular signalling pathways with oncogenic endpoints, as further elaborated on in Section
2.2. IL-6 is upregulated in numerous types of cancers and with very few exceptions, ele-
vated serum [IL-6] generally correlate with poor patient survival prognosis and advanced
stages of cancer [1]. The human cancers for which this correlation is existing include
5
==PAGE:26==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
colorectal, liver, gastric, pancreatic, prostate, kidney and bladder cancers among others
[1]. The evident inﬂuence of IL-6 in cancer development thus makes the cytokine of par-
ticular interest when assessing and investigating the impact of inﬂammation in cancer
development.
Figure 2.1: The complex interplay between numerous immune factors, cells,
cytokines and inﬂammation in initiation and development of cancer. Taken
from [2].
Besides tumour-promoting eﬀects, chronic inﬂammation is likewise believed to contribute
to genetic instability as an inﬂammatory microenvironment aids in genomic destabilisa-
tion [17]. Mutation rates in inﬂamed tissue, with mutation frequency of 4x10−8 per base
pair is increased 4-fold compared to normal tissue of 1x10−8 per base pair [17]. The com-
bination of higher mutation rates caused by inﬂamed tissue and inﬂammation-promoting
tumourigenenis, under partial inﬂuence of IL-6, creates what has been described as ”a per-
fect storm” with regards to the ability of an inﬂammatory microenvironment to promote
tumour-development and cancer [17].
6
==PAGE:27==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
2.2
The role of IL-6 in cancer inﬂammatory pathways
2.2.1
IL-6 in the immune system
In the well-functioning immune system, IL-6 plays its major part in the innate immunity
during the acute phase response [18]. Upon encounter of pathogens, subsequent phago-
cytosis, pathogen degradation and antigen presentation, activated macrophages secrete a
range of cytokines with varying local and systemic eﬀects [18]. These include e.g. the
pro-inﬂammatory cytokines IL-1β, Tumour Necrosis Factor (TNF)-α and IL-6. In the
local inﬂamed tissue, IL-6 activates lymphocytes and increases antibody (Ab) produc-
tion, whereas it induces fever and acute-phase protein production systemically [18]. More
speciﬁcally, IL-6 aids in the innate immune response together with IL-1β and TNF-α
by acting on the liver, bone marrow endothelium, fat and muscle tissue and dendritic
cells (DCs), overviewed in Figure 2.2 [18]. Additionally, IL-6 is an endogenous pyrogen
and thus induces increased body temperature and fever by acting on hypothalamus, thus
lowering the growing abilities of pathogens while increasing the eﬀects of the adaptive im-
mune response [18]. In hepatocytes, IL-6 induces synthesis of acute-phase proteins, which
are broad-speciﬁc antibody-like proteins towards pathogen associated molecular patterns
(PAMPs), involved in activation of the complement cascade as well as opsonisation of
bacteria [1, 18]. In summary, IL-6 contributes to battling and keeping infections in check
before the adaptive immune system is fully developed.
Figure 2.2: Eﬀects of cytokines IL-6, IL-1β and TNF-α in aiding the innate
immune response. Taken from [18].
IL-6 also plays a role in recruitment of neutrophils to the infected tissue. IL-6 is believed
to directly recruit neutrophils to the site of infection [19, 20]. Upon diﬀerentiation from
naive T-cells, CD4+ TH17-cells are developed when IL-6, through the below described
STAT3 pathway, and Transforming Growth Factor (TGF)-β are present [1]. TH17-cells
produce chemokines that recruit neutrophils to site of infection, thus promoting inﬂam-
mation indirectly. IL-6 and TH17-cells are therefore related to neutrophil mobilisation to
the infected tissue and maintaining a pro-inﬂammatory environment.
7
==PAGE:28==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Furthermore, in the presence of TGF-β but absence of IL-6, Treg-cells are diﬀerentiated
from naive T-cells. This demonstrates that IL-6 plays a major, natural role in the tight
regulation between a pro-inﬂammatory response and regulating and controlling the im-
mune system [1, 19].
2.2.2
IL-6 induced signalling pathways and oncogenic eﬀects
IL-6 activates transcription factors STAT3 and NF-κB intracellularly [1, 2]. Each pathway
activation and the eﬀects hereof is described below.
JAK-STAT3 pathway
Being a cytokine, IL-6 acts extracellularly with capabilities to induce an intracellular
signal through speciﬁc pathways. The overall objective and general eﬀects of cytokines,
including IL-6, is to rapidly alter expression of specﬁc genes in the nucleus [21]. IL-6 acts
on three main signalling pathways, but with the Janus Kinase (JAK)-Signal transducers
and activators of transcription (STAT) pathway as the primary pathway [1]. Gene ex-
pression controlled by STATs involve cell growth, survival and diﬀerentiation of cells and
lymphoctes. STAT3 acts as a non-classical oncogene and is activated in most cancers [16].
Upon encounter with the cell membrane IL-6 binds to the α-subunit of the membrane-
bound IL-6R, which forms a complex with two glycoprotein 130 (gp130) homodimers.
The complex consists of two IL-6 substrate molecules, two α-subunits and two gp130
molecules, known as classic IL-6 signalling [1, 22]. The IL-6R can both be membrane-
bound and in a released, soluble state (sIL-6R) [1]. The IL-6R can be shedded from the
cell surface during development of carcinoma as the sIL-6R form [1]. The binding of IL-6
to sIL-6R will prevent IL-6 from being degraded by proteases [23], although binding of
the IL-6R to the cell membrane is required for an intracellular signal. The IL-6 and sIL-
6R can perform trans-signalling when the complex is bound to membrane-bound gp130
subunits in order to initiate an intracellular signal [22]. As gp130 is expressed on most
cells, trans-signalling can be induced not only on IL-6R-expressing cells but also cells that
normally do not express IL-6 [22].
Following extracellular signalling, intracellular JAK kinases are bound to the cytoplas-
mic IL-6R domain [21]. The overall process is schematically depicted in Fiugre 2.3 [21].
Following complex association and gp130 dimerization the JAK tyrosine kinases JAK1,
JAK2 and TYK2 are brought into close vicinity. The JAK kinases on each cytoplasmic
tail are then cross-phosphorylated and activated, allowing for phosphorylation of sev-
eral tyrosine residues on the gp130 signalling chains. These include Y759, Y767, Y814,
Y905 and Y915 in human gp130 [1]. The tyrosine residue phospohyrlation allows for re-
cruitment of STAT1 and STAT3, Src-homology 2 (SH2) domain-containing transcription
factors [21]. STATs are inactive in the cytoplasm, but is activated upon phosphoryla-
tion of a specﬁc tyrosine residue in the carboxy terminus by JAK kinases [2, 21], with
higher STAT3 activation potency than STAT1 [1]. The STATs dimerise homogenously
by binding of the SH2 domains to each other due to conformational change following
phosphorylation [2, 21]. They then translocate to the nucleus, where the STATs act to
initiate gene transcription of selected genes [2, 21].
8
==PAGE:29==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Figure 2.3:
Schematic outline of the JAK-STAT pathway following IL-6
receptor binding. Taken from [21].
As touched upon in Section 2.1, IL-6 levels are increased in various types of cancers by
excretion from immune cells, epithelial and malignant cells as well as from ﬁbroblasts.
This induces a constant activation of the JAK/STAT3 pathway, prompting cell prolif-
eration, survival, invasion and metastasis of cancerous cells together with angiogenesis
and inﬂammation [1]. Figure 2.4 provides an overview of the tumour-promoting eﬀects of
IL-6.
The eﬀects of STAT3 in maintaining a procarcinogenic inﬂammatory microenvironment
are evident [2]: STAT3-deﬁcient T-cells fail to undergo IL-6 mediated cell proliferation, un-
derlining the importance of IL-6 in the proliferation of T-cells as IL-6 prevents an apoptotic
fate of T-cells [2]. Additionally, tumour-cell STAT3 activation promotes oncogenesis, sup-
presses anti-tumour immunity by limiting anti-tumour Th1-cell cytokine expression and
inhibits tumour-site Treg cell expansion. Tumour cell STAT3 activation further induces
production of cytokines, chemokines and growth factors activating STAT3 in stromal cells,
the most signiﬁcant contributor of inﬂammation mediators [2].
IL-6 induced activation of STAT3 also aﬀects proteins involved in cell cycle regulation
[1, 24]. STAT3 activation decreases the expresion of cyclin-dependent kinase (Cdk) in-
hibitor p21, a potent cell cycle inhibitor [24], whereas expression of the cyclin proteins D1,
B1 and c-Myc, important for entry into the cell cycle, are increased [1]. This allows cells
to continuously grow and proliferate, a key component in cancer development. Besides
expression of cyclins, the proteins survivin, Bcl-2, Bcl-XL, Mcl-1 and X-linked inhibitor
of apoptosis protein, all important for cell survival and chemoresistance, are increasingly
expressed [1].
In the development of metastatic cancer cells, the epithelial-mesenchymal transition (EMT)
is highly important as it allows for invasion, angiogenesis and metastatic tumour forma-
tion [1]. IL-6 is able to induce this transition in various cancers and inﬂammation is a
known launcher of EMT [1]. STAT3 activation by IL-6 is evidently involved in the tran-
9
==PAGE:30==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Figure 2.4: IL-6 and IL-11 signalling pathway and tumourigenesis in cancer.
Taken from [1].
scription of vascular endothelial growth factor-A (VEGF-A), important for blood vessel
formation in malignant tumours [1, 25]. Equally, STAT3 targets and increases expression
of matrix metalloproteinase (MMP)-2 [26], MMP-7 [27] and MMP-9 [28], important in
breaking down the extracellular matrix promoting tumour invasion [29]. Increased IL-6
levels in speciﬁc organs is thought to attract circulating tumour cells (CTCs) and thus
increase the risk of tumour cell establishment and development into metastatic lesions
[30], due to IL-6’s ability to promote angiogenesis and lymphangiogenesis [1].
In summary, IL-6 is seen to be a natural, potent activator of the JAK-STAT3 pathway to
induce an intracellular activation of genes involved in cell proliferation and diﬀerentiation
among others. In addition, it is clear that the eﬀects of persistent IL6-induced activation
of the JAK-STAT3 pathway play an important role in tumour cell development, EMT,
migration, invasion and metastasis as well as angiogenesis and cancer inﬂammation.
NF-κB pathway
Whereas the JAK-STAT3-pathway is directly activated by presence of IL-6, the NF-κB
pathway is related to IL-6 in a diﬀerent manner. NF-κβ is a key transcription factor in the
control of cellular processes such as cell development, growth and apoptosis [16]. Similarly,
NF-κβ works in the immune system to rapidly increase expression of pro-inﬂammatory
cytokines upon encountering bacterial pathogens, for instance through Toll-like Receptor
10
==PAGE:31==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
(TLR) binding [2, 18]. As with STAT3, NF-κB is also a non-classical oncogene, activated
in the majority of cancers [16].
Following antigen recognition by TLRs, NF-κβ can be activated by the pathway visu-
alised in Figure 2.5 [18]. Intracellular domains of the TLRs bind to adaptor proteins
MyD88, which recruits serine-threonine kinases IRAK1 and IRAK4 and ubiquitin E3
ligase TRAF-6 [18]. After activation by IRAK, TRAF-6 is autoubiquitinated together
with the component NEMO. The ubiquitin scaﬀold of TRAF-6 binds TAK1, the latter
being phosphorylated by IRAK. TAK1 phosphorylates IKKβ which then activates IκB
by phosphorylation. NF-κB is released following degradation of IκB, allowing NF-κB to
enter the nucleus and activate transcription of pro-inﬂammatory cytokine genes among
others [18].
Figure 2.5: NF-κβ activation following TLR-signalling. Taken from [18].
Persistent activation of NF-κB can induce continuous expression of multiple pro-inﬂammatory
cytokines, such as IL-6, IL-1, IL-8 and TNF-α [2]. Since these cytokines are strong ac-
tivators of NF-κβ a positive feedback loop is evident. It is currently unknown whether
IL-6 is able to activate and initiate NF-κβ intracellular signalling or if it merely acts to
enhance NF-κβ activity through a positive loop [31]. Nevertheless, NF-κβ is strongly ac-
tivated by basolateral IL-6 stimulation in epithelial cells although the speciﬁc mechanism
of activation is unknown [31].
As NF-κB acts as a transcription factor for genes related to cell proliferation, angiogenesis,
invasion and metastasis of cancer cells, persistent NF-κB activation is a major factor
in cancer development, while provoking cellular and DNA damage and enhancing cell
proliferation and transformation rates [2].
By controlling genes both related to cell cycle regulation, such as cyclin D1, D2, D3 and
E1, c-myc, CDK2, CDK4 and CDK6 in addition to most anti-apoptotic genes, NF-κB is
heavily involved in promotion of cell proliferation and survival, thus promoting tumouro-
genesis [2, 13]. NF-κB is also involved in cancer metastasis by regulating gene expression
of VCAM-1, MMPs and CXCR4, whereas a direct activation of ICAM-1 expression by
11
==PAGE:32==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
IL-6 signalling has been reported [31]. These latter mentioned proteins are all related to
the process of cancer metastasis, indicating the direct role of IL-6 induced NF-κB activa-
tion in cancer metastasis [2].
The activation of the JAK-STAT3 and NF-κB pathways demonstrates the tumourigenic
intracellular eﬀects of extracellular IL-6 signalling. The positive feedback loop of NF-κB-
induced IL-6, which in turn activates JAK-STAT3 upon IL-6 signalling, further signiﬁes
the close relation between IL-6, NF-κB and STAT3 in tumourogenesis. It is clear that IL-
6 is a key contributor in preserving a pro-tumourigenic inﬂammatory microenvironment
and tumour development. Additionally, IL-6 is directly correlated with cancer stage and
survival prognosis. By combining these aspects, the importance of destroying IL-6 to
irreversibly inhibit its action as a pro-inﬂammatory cytokine in tumourigenesis is a viable
novel therapeutic strategy.
2.3
Therapeutic interventions against IL-6
Since the IL-6-JAK-STAT3 signalling pathway involves numerous key components, there
are multiple possible targets for disrupting and inhibiting this pathway, including IL-6,
JAK and STAT3 inhibition and gp130 blockade among others. Multiple targeted thera-
pies towards such components have been developed or are currently undergoing clinical
trials constituting the State-of-the-Art [1, 32]. As neutralising or targeting mAbs towards
IL-6 and the IL-6R exhibit extracellular eﬀects similar to the to-be-designed NP system,
these are of primary interest here.
Conditions such as rheumatoid arthritis (RA), inﬂammatory bowl disease (IBD) and Mul-
ticentric Castleman’s Disease (MCD) are developed due to a malfunction with IL-6 and
in many ways resemble what is seen for cancer-related inﬂammatory stimulation of IL-6
[33]. Drugs towards RA, IBD and MCD are therefore also relevant and with eﬀects to-
wards cancer - and vice versa - and previous treatment options have primarily focused on
blocking IL-6’s ability to cause an intracellular signal [1]. In Appendix A, a list of biophar-
maceuticals approved or under clinical deveopment targeting IL-6 and the IL-6R is shown.
Inhibiting IL-6 signalling has been shown to signiﬁcantly decrease plasma concentrations
of CCL2 and CXCL12, chemokines promoting immune cell recruitment and angiogenesis-
promoting VEGF in a phase II trial of a chimeric Ab speciﬁc towards IL-6 in ovarian
cancer [12].
Recently, most promising results were reported for a targeting liposome-
based anti-IL-6R NP system as it was shown that tumour growth could be inhibited and
thus preventing metastasis in breast cancer mouse models, showcasing the possible abili-
ties of IL-6-signal inhibition [34].
However, targeting and neutralising mAbs against IL-6 and IL-6R can also entail the
unwanted side-eﬀect of systemic serum IL-6 accumulation as IL-6 is not degraded follow-
ing neutralisation by Abs [1]. Additionally, the eﬀects of IL-6 targeting mAbs, including
possible toxicities, will be of systemic nature and not only targeting IL-6 related to the
tumour microenvironment [1].
In particular the systemic rather than just a tumour-
targeting impact is a key drawback and shortcoming for the application of these anti-IL-6
Abs.
12
==PAGE:33==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
As described, IL-6 possesses a key role in the natural immune system and the implications
of complete, systemic inhibition of IL-6 would be a critical side-eﬀect. Clinical studies
have indicated that side-eﬀects for anti-IL-6 and anti-IL-6R therapies include increased
risk and severity of upper respiratory tract infections (RTIs), hyperuricemia, increased
weight, rash and pruritus for anti-IL-6 Siltuximab [35]; headache, mild upper RTIs and
pharyngolaryngeal pain for anti-IL-6 Sirukumab [36] and upper RTIs and urinary tract
infections for anti-IL-6R Tocilizumab [33, 37], see Appendix A. It is therefore obvious
that therapies with the side-eﬀects of systemic IL-6 inhibition constitute an inadequate
strategy for limiting IL-6 signalling.
So far only two mAbs have been approved aﬀecting either IL-6 or the IL-6R, Siltuximab
towards (MCD) [38] and Tocilizumab towards RA, MCD and Juvenile idiopathic arthritis
[1, 39, 40], but multiple mAbs are currently under development and in phase I-III with
Sarilumab expected to undergo registration in the near future [41].
With the development of this NP system, the complete ablation of IL-6 is a key parameter
of success, thereby evading IL-6 blood accumulation as well as avoiding systemic- and
side-eﬀects of IL-6 inhibition as seen for anti-IL-6 mAbs currently approved or under
development.
2.4
New interventions against IL-6
In order to inhibit eﬀects of IL-6-signalling while avoiding systemic IL-6 accumulation
and side-eﬀects, catalytic breakdown of IL-6 is an emerging approach and solution to
the issue at hand. By breaking down and changing the conformation of IL-6, ideally the
eﬀects of the cytokine are completely disrupted. As it is later elaborated on in Section 2.7,
the area of nanobiocatalysis is an upcoming ﬁeld, where the speciﬁc properties of nano-
sized platforms and the involvement of a catalytic enzyme towards a speciﬁc substrate is
utilised.
mAbs in clinics speciﬁc towards IL-6 and IL-6R are seen not to eliminate IL-6, but merely
hinder its action momentarily by being single-event therapies, binding 1-2 IL-6 molecules
per Ab. To ensure complete loss of activity of IL-6 the irreversible cleavage, conforma-
tional change and inactivation of IL-6 is crucial and an enzymatic catalytic approach
will allow for the continuous, sustainable IL-6 breakdown and, hopefully, a signiﬁcantly
increased and prolonged signal-disrupting eﬀect. For this project to be successful, assur-
ing a sustainable cleavage rate of IL-6 is therefore a key parameter. The possibility of
combining biodegradable nanomaterials with catalytic enzymes thus comprises a highly
interesting approach to ablation of cancer inﬂammatory signalling.
2.5
Neutropil-derived serine proteases
It has previously been shown that the neutrophil-derived serine proteases (NSPs) of the
chymotrypsin family Human leukocyte elastase (or Neutrophil elastase) (NE), Cathepsin
G (Cat G) and Proteinase 3 (PR 3) all have expansive speciﬁcity. Thus they present
catalytic activity towards numerous proteins, growth factors and receptors in an unspe-
ciﬁc manner, including IL-6 [42, 43]. All NSPs are capable of inactivating the cytokine
13
==PAGE:34==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Figure 2.6:
Cleavage sites of NE (Yellow; Leu19-Thr20), PR 3 (Orange;

Stabilising residue

eﬀectively and at sites of inﬂammation during normal immune responses with high [IL-6],
elevated levels of NSPs are released by invading polymorphonuclear neutrophils (PMNs)
as an anti-inﬂammatory response [23, 42].
NSPs are stored naturally in azurophilic granules of PMNs [44]. NPSs typically act in-
tracellularly within phagolysosomes to digest and break down phagocytosed pathogens
and all three proteases have bactericidal as well as antifungal activity, though acting
through diﬀerent mechanisms [45]. However, NSPs can also act extracellularly to regu-
late innate immunity and inﬂammation, to aid in killing bacteria or to destroy matrix
components, cytokines etc. upon secretion into the surrounding environment [42, 45].
NSPs are secreted from PMNs upon their encounter with chemoattractants and can be
both cell-surface bound or released entirely outside the cell for catalytic eﬀectiveness to-
wards potential substrates [45]. Multiple extracellular components can act as chemotaxins
on PMNs releasing NSPs, e.g cytokines like TNF-α, IL-8 and IL-6, growth factors such as
platelet-derived growth factor. On the other hand, cleaved fragments of PMN-stimulating
cytokines like IL-6 can act in a negative feedback loop manner, since the stimulatory eﬀect
on PMNs by IL-6 precludes subsequent to cleavage of the protein [42].
14
==PAGE:35==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
The NSPs have distinct cleavage sites of the intact IL-6 molecule, visualised in Figure
2.6. All three catalyse the hydrolysis of speciﬁc peptide bonds. NE attacks both the
Val11-Ala12 and the Leu19-Thr20 peptide bonds, yellow in Figure 2.6, located towards the
N-terminus whereas PR 3 catalyses the Ala145-Ser146-bond situated in a small peripheral
helical region facing outside towards the surrounding environment, orange in Figure 2.6.
Cat G is seen to hydrolyse the Phe78-Asn79 peptide bond located in the loop linking helix
A and B of IL-6, red in Figure 2.6, [23]. With regards to the cleavage site of Cat G,
Met184 at the end of the C-terminus of IL-6, blue in Figure 2.6, is closely situated to
Phe78-Asn79 and seem to induce highly important protein stability of IL-6. Concurrently,
Phe78 is believed to play a role in the binding of IL-6 to IL-6R gp80 chain [42]. Altogether,
cleavage of Phe78-Asn79 by Cat G signiﬁcantly impacts and results in loss of bioactivity
of IL-6. Hence, Cat G is chosen for the speciﬁc purpose of IL-6 cleavage for the to-be-
designed IL-6-ablating system [23].

2.6.1
Characteristics and speciﬁcity
Characteristics of Cat G are listed in Table 2.1 and the enzyme is visualised by use of
PyMOL in Figure 2.7, where multiple residues important for the speciﬁcity of binding

for Cat G is highlighted. Cat G is originally synthesised as a propeptide consisting of
255 amino acids with an 18-residue long signal peptide, single C-terminus extension and
a two-residue activation peptide at the N-terminus. Following activation and removal of
signal peptide and chain extensions the active Cat G is made up of 235 amino acids [45].
Cat G has similar amino acid sequence speciﬁcity as other chymotrypsin family serine
proteases. In particular, the presence of an aromatic, large hydrophobic or basic residue
at cleavage position P1; the ﬁrst residue from cleavable bond towards the N-terminus, as
indicated in Figure 2.8 [45, 47], is typical for Cat G [48]. In the cleaving of IL-6, this P1

residue Glu226 of Cat G [45]. In case of a basic residue at P1, such as Lysine, Glu226 can
form a salt bridge with the Lysine residue [45, 49]. The S2 subsite is deﬁned by His57
and a small hydrophobic residue [49]. Depending on references, either the hydrophobic
residues Leu99 [44, 45] or Ile99 [49, 50] occupy the S2 subsite. Lys192 demarcates the S3
subsite, favouring an acidic residue at position P3 allowing ionic interactions [45, 49].
Towards the C terminus of Cat G, the positively charged Arg41 in the S2’ subsite is believed

to interact with a negatively charged residue at P2’, possibly Glu80 of IL-6, although this
is not fully conﬁrmed [44, 45]. Together with Arg143, Arg41 may also take part in the S3’
pocket due to its ﬂexibility and aid in stabilising enzyme-substrate binding by interacting
with an acidic residue at P3’, Glu81 of IL-6, but again this require further clariﬁcation
[44, 45].
2.6.2
Mechanism of action
The mechanism of catalytic Cat G cleaving is highly preserved throughout evolution and
involves the so-called catalytic triad, consisting of the side chains of residues Asp102, His57
and Ser195 of Cat G, shown in Figure 2.9. The side chains of Asp102, His57 and Ser195 are
in close vicinity of each other and hydrogen bonds stabilise the system. His57 accepts the
hydrogen of Ser195 and thus contributes to the formation of a strong nucleophilic alkoxide
ion of Ser195 [48]. The potent alkoxide ion of Ser195 is what ultimately allows the peptide
hydrolysis.
The mechanism of the peptide bond hydrolysis is illustrated in Figure 2.10 [48]. The
alkoxide ion of Ser195 initially performs a nucleuophillic attack on the substrate carbonyl
carbon causing formation of an unstable tetrahedral intermediate; Figure 2.10, step 2.
The negative charge of the intermediate is stabilised through interaction with backbone
NH-groups of Gly193 and Ser195 in the so-called oxyanion hole [48]. The peptide bond
is then cleaved when the substrate amine group attacks the proton responsible for the
charge of His57. This breaks up the tetrahedral intermediate, forming an acyl-enzyme,
after which the amine component of the substrate leaves the active site of Cat G; Figure
2.10, step 3 & 4. The leaving amine component is the part of IL-6 where Asn79 constitues
the now-N terminus of the peptide chain [48].
Following amine departure, the ester bond formed by the acyl-enzyme bonding is hydrol-
ysed by water; Figure 2.10, step 5 [48]. Where His57 before acted as proton-donor, it now
attracts a hydrogen from the water. The nucleophillic hydroxyl ion of water attacks the
carbonyl carbon resulting in formation of another tetrahedral intermediate; Figure 2.10,
step 6 [48]. Ultimately, the bond to the alkoxide ion is broken upon interaction with the
17
==PAGE:38==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Figure 2.9: Residues Asp102, His57 and Ser195 constituting the catalytic triad
in the active site of Cat G together with Gly193 in the oxyanion hole. RCSB
PDB-ﬁle 1T32 visualised by use of PyMOL [10, 46].
positively charged His57 and the carboxylic acid product is released from the active site;
Figure 2.10, step 7 & 8 [48]. The departing carboxylic acid is the now-C terminus of the
cleaved IL-6 peptide containing residue Phe78.
In summary, the catalytic process of IL-6 cleavage by Cat G is reliant on the formation of
alkoxide ion of Ser195, directly facilitated by His57. Additionally, the procedure requires
the ability of His57 to ﬁrst act as a proton-attracting base and subsequnetly as a proton-
donoting acid. This stresses the importance of optimal pH, since at low pH His57 will
be protonated thus preveting alkoxide ion formation. The optimum pH level of Cat G is
∼7.5-8 [44, 45] and thus higher than the pKa of Histidine of ∼6.0 [51].
2.6.3
Turnover rate of Cat G cleavage
Little work has been done on assessing the actual kinetic catalytic rate of IL-6 cleavage by
Cat G. This is believed to partly be due to IL-6 being unﬁt for use as e.g. a colorimetric
substrate, as it does not absorp light itself. Kinetic rates of Cat G catalysis have there-
fore mostly been assessed for small peptide sequences with some IL-6-like resemblance,
conjugated as part of a small molecule with a ﬂuorescent side-group, e.g. 4-Nitroanilide
(pNA) [52, 53, 54]. With varying substrate sequences, the speciﬁcity and ability of Cat G
to catalyse cleavage of such peptides has been determined. The activity of Cat G towards
such substrates can be exploited in the comparison of Cat eﬃciency in diﬀerent settings,
e.g. as free enzyme or as immobilised on the surfaces of NPs.
To describe the activity of Cat G, enzymatic kinetic parameters can determined based on
18
==PAGE:39==

Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Figure 2.10: Peptide hydrolysis by Cat G. R1 of the substrate makes up the
N terminus whereas R2 is the C terminus. Taken from [48].
Michaelis-Menten kinetics, whose application further is elaborated in Section 3.4. Typ-
ically the parameters kcat, the ’turnover number’ describing the number of substrate
molecules converted into product per time, and the Michaelis-contant KM are useful in
comparing the activity and speciﬁcity of enzymes [55].
Unfortunately, no studies have investigated catalytic rates of Cat G towards a substrate
sequence of SGF∼REE ∼denoting the bond of cleavage, similar to the cleavable P3-P’3
amino sequence of IL-6. However, multiple studies have investigated the speciﬁcity of Cat
G towards resembling substrates. A thorough list of such studies, including determined
kinetic parameters of Cat G, is shown in Appendix B. Key points from these studies
support the substrate speciﬁcity of Cat G, in particular with signiﬁcant activity towards
substrates with bulky, aromatic or large at position P1, as is seen with Phe78 for IL-6
[54, 56, 57].
Cat G kinetic parameters towards the colorimetric substrate Succininc acid-Ala-Ala-Pro-
Phe-pNA (Suc-AAPF-pNA) have previously been determined with kcat = 2.06s−1 and
KM = 1.76mM [54].
The substrate is here utilised in Cat G kinetic experiments in
Sections 3.4 and 5.1.2.

2.7
The state of nanobiocatalysis
Nanobiocatalysis is an emerging ﬁeld within medical and biotechnology.
It is deﬁned
as the incorporation of enzymes into nanostructured materials, allowing for utilisation
of advantages of nanostructures while delivering or using the catalytic capabilities of an
enzyme [58]. Enzymes have been implemented widely commercially in the biotechno-
logical industry and advances of biocatalysis have been most prominent in the biofuel
industry among others [58]. Thus, advances in the applications of nanobiocatalysts in
bioprocessing have previously obtained higher focus. Despite the promising developments
of NP-enzyme conjugates, as introduced above, nanobiocatalysis has yet to be utilised to
any great extent in medicinal applications [58, 59]. However, the same concepts used in
the biotechnology area, and in particular the use of nanobiocatalysis, is with great poten-
tial also in the nanomedicinal world. Indeed, the idea of enzymatic catalysis of biological
substrates on nanoscale could be the cornerstone of both biotechnological and medicinal
applications of nanobiocatalysis. A schematic overview of some of the characteristics and
advantages within application of nanomaterials is seen in Figure 2.11.
The major target of nanobiocatalysis so far has been the immobilising of enzymes onto a
high surface area using structures in nanoscale. Nanostrucutres of spheres, pores, tubes
etc. allow for this increase in surface-to-volume-ratio while presenting diﬀerent properties
based on the speciﬁc shape. With uniform size distribution of manufactured nanostruc-
tures together with the ability to control the size of these, advantages of nanostructures
are apparent [58].
Previously, primarily in biotechnology, the major focus of nanobiocatalytic applications
has been on entrapping and/or delivering enzymes while ensuring enzymatic stability and
activity. Various techniques have been utilised including concepts such as nanoentrap-
ment [60, 61, 62], single enzyme nanoparticles [63], ’ship-in-a-bottle’ [64] and ’nancoscale
enzyme reactors’ [58, 65], thoroughly described in the literature. For all concepts, the util-
isation and improvement of enzymatic stability and active enzyme accumulation within a
small surface area are key advantages of the systems. These traits have previously been
utilised extensively in biotechnology but to a limited extent in medicine so far. However,
NPs have recently received extensive focus with multiple nanoparticle-based drug deliv-
ery systems currently undergoing preclinical and clinical trials [14]. The prospects and
arguments of application of NPs as drug delivery vessels and carriers are elaborated on
in the ensuing section.
2.8
Polymeric, biodegradable nanoparticles
With early nanomedicines such as liposomes, polymeric micelles and polymer-drug conju-
gates developed in the 1960’s and 70’s, the area of polymeric NPs for various uses evolved
later [14]. Polymeric NPs have numerous applications but in particular as carrier vehicles
of small molecules, enzymes etc.
One of the key advantages in using polymer-based NPs rather than any other material
is the aspect of biodegradability of the polymer core [14], further described in Section
2.8.2. Due to this biodegradability and additional advantages of polymeric NPs, these are
utilised in this work and thus described.
20
==PAGE:41==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Figure 2.11: Overview of the numerous aspects, properties and character-
istics of nanomaterials, indicating extensive utilisation for a broad variety of
applications. Taken from [14]
.
2.8.1
Advantages and applications
To obtain a prolonged and sustained cleaving rate of IL-6, NPs can be utilised as a delivery
vehicle. In general, advantages of polymeric NPs include the ability to enhance the overall
pharmacological aspects of a drug, for instance by inducing longer blood circulation time
while minimising toxicological side eﬀects as well as enhancing drug delivery across mem-
branes or barriers as are similar features for other NP-drug systems [14]. In particular for
anti-cancer drugs, severe side eﬀects follow treatment ultimately limiting the maximum
tolerated dose of drug given. By utilising and developing an NP-drug-system, targeting
of a drug or biopharmaceutical to the cancerous environment is possible, thus limiting
unwanted adverse eﬀects while increasing accumulation of therapeutic in the cancer tis-
sue. Equally, by encapsulation in NPs the rate of clearance of a therapeutic from blood
circulation as well as proteolytic degradation is signiﬁcantly reduced [14, 58].
Polymeric NPs can increase and prolong blood circulation times of e.g. enzymes com-
pared to free administration by providing a protective capping of the enzyme [14, 15].
In addition, encapsulation of enzymes by NPs also leads to increased tumour microen-
vironment (TME) accumulation, either by passive or active targeting [14, 15]. Passive
targeting of NPs allows utilisation of the leaky vasculature and EPR eﬀect seen for solid
tumours resulting in increased NP accumulation in tumour tissue [14, 15]. By prolonged
circulation times this utilisation of the EPR eﬀect is naturally also extended, ultimately
leading to extended tumour tissue accumulation of NPs. As later described, PEGylated
NPs excel in extended circulation half-life, due to the PEG polymer’s ability to act as a
21
==PAGE:42==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
shield towards degrading components in the bloodstream [14].
Passively-targeting NPs are also characterised by the lack of any active targeting ligand
attached to the surface [14]. However, by conjugating highty speciﬁc ligands to the outer
surface of NPs, the tumour site can be actively targeted, enhancing tumour accumula-
tion [14]. Numerous various targeting strategies have been investigated for targeting and
delivering anti-cancerous drugs. Polymeric NPs are particularly applicable for ligand-
conjugation due to functional endgroups (FGs) at the ends of the polymers, allowing for
covalent bioconjugation of diﬀerent targeting ligands [15]. Although no actively targeting
polymeric NP systems have made it to the market, the concept is of high interest and
with great potential [15].
Furthermore, the suitable chemically functional groups on the NP surface can be utilised
to conjugate enzymes onto the surfaces of NPs [66]. It is seen that enzymatic activity
is enhanced when enzymes are immobilised on the surface of NPs [66, 67].
This in-
creased enzymatic activity may be due to several factors: Spherical polymeric NPs have
been utilised in research due in part to their high surface-to-volume ratio. This allows
for NP-conjugation of enzymes to provide a highly dense, concentrated enzyme surface
within a small volume, increasing the chance and rate of enzyme substrate conversion
[15, 66]. Electrostatic attraction between substrate and NP has likewise seen to increase
enzyme activity as the number of substrate-enzyme binding events increases before sub-
strate migration, increasing the chance of substrate conversion [68, 69]. Furthermore,
NP conjugation allows for speciﬁc orientation of the enzymes favouring enzyme-substrate
interaction e.g. by orienting the enzyme active site towards the exterior environment [66].
2.8.2
Building-blocks and properties of polymeric NPs
Stability, characteristics and capabilities of NPs are greatly aﬀected by the speciﬁcities of
the NP, including choice of polymer and biophysicochemical properties [14], introduced
below.
Polymers
As the name implies, polymeric NPs are built up of polymers that self-assemble to form
a spherical structure following precipitation. The speciﬁcities aﬀecting the properties of
NPs are primarily determined by the choice of polymer used for the creation of the poly-
meric NP. Polymeric monomers are usually synthesised in lengths to obtain molecular
weights (MW) of 5,000-30,000 kDa, with hydrophobicity of the polymer chain increasing
with MW. Lower weight polymers also tend to degrade faster thus aﬀecting the stability-
proﬁle of the NP a well as biological properties such as biological activity and elimination
[15].
Common polymers used for polymeric NPs include poly(glycolic acid) (PGA), poly(D,L-
lactic acid) (PLA) and poly(D,L-lactic-co-glycolic acid) (PLGA), all of which are biograde-
able in vivo [15]. Typically, polymers break down due to hydrolysis of ester bonds in the
backbone of the structure [15]. In Figure 2.12 diﬀerent polymeric monomer units used for
manufacturing of polymeric NPs are shown.
22
==PAGE:43==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
PGA generally undergoes rapid degradation. PLA is more hydrophobic than PGA due
to a methyl side group, but properties of PLA can be altered by changing D- and L-
isomer racemization. PLGA is the polymer that has been utilised to the highest extent
in clinics [15]. This is to highly favourable stability, suitable polymeric properties, non-
toxicity and its classiﬁcation as Generally Regarded as Safe (GRAS) by the U.S. Food and
Drug Administration (FDA) [15, 70]. Likewise, properties of PLGA can be changed by
adjusting the ratio of L:G monomers of PLGA with degradation rate of PLGA dropping
as the L:G monomer ratio decreases [15].
PLGA copolymers have also been developed, where block copolymers of PLGA and the
polymer polyethylene glycol (PEG) are mixed [15]. PEG has been utilised in numerous
biological applications due to its ability to decrease phagocytosis of NPs by macrophages,
thus enhancing blood circulation times and making the NPs ’stealth’ [14].
PEG also
allows NPs to obtain a neutral surface change, minimise immunological responses while
allowing conjugation of various targeting ligands on the outer surface of the NPs [15].
PEG is equally classiﬁed as GRAS by the FDA [14].
Figure 2.12:
Examples of biodegradable polymers typically used for NPs.
PLGA is of highest interest in this project. Taken from [15].
Inﬂuence of biophysicochemical properties
The biophysicochemical characteristics of NPs can greatly inﬂuence the properties, pos-
sible applications and in the end successful of use of the NPs. In particular shape, size,
surface charge, hydrophobicity, rigidity and an eventual PEGylation of the NPs aﬀects
the cytotoxicity, pharmacokinetics (PK), biodistribution (BD) as well as blood circulation
times and thus level of tumour retention of NPs [14].
Shape and size
The shape and size of a NP aﬀects NP cell uptake as well as blood
circulation time.
Generally, spherical NPs tend to be taken up by cells more rapidly
than non-spherical, oblong NPs and thus have shorter blood circulation times in vivo
[14]. NPs over 1 µm are phagocytosed by macrophages (MØs), DCs or PMNs, whereas
smaller NPs undergo adsorptive pinocytosis or receptor-medicated endocytosis [14]. The
choice of NP size is a delicate balance, but 10-100 nm in diameter is currently an accepted
range. NPs in the 40-50 nm range exhibit highest cellular uptake and larger NPs of 70-200
nm usually undergo tumour accumulation through passive targeting by the EPR-eﬀect to
the highest extent [14]. ∼100 nm NPs are targeted in this project, due to extracellular
tumour targeting of IL-6. For clearance, large NPs tend to be ﬁltered out by the spleen or
phagocytosed, whereas smaller than 5.5 nm NPs usually are cleared through glomerular
ﬁltration by the kidneys [14].
23
==PAGE:44==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Surface charge
The surface charge, measured by zeta potential (ζ) in mV, is equally
important for blood circulation half-life and cellular uptake. Positively charged NPs (>
15 mV) are cleared rapidly by MØs, DCs and PMNs compared to neutral or negatively
charged NPs. Charged NPs in general have shorter half-lives in vivo as they interact
more easily with serum protein and activate the complement system. However, NPs with
positive surface charge may also be cellularly internalised to a higher extent through
endocytosis due to binding to negatively charged phospholipid head groups and proteins
on the cell surface. Negatively charged NPs also show enhanced cellular uptake compared
to neutral NPs [14]. Surface charge is easily adjustable by altering the functional end
groups of the NP. As the charged end group of the polymer chain will orient itself towards
the outside of the NP during NP preparation due to this being energetically favourable,
the properties of the end group will determine the overall surface charge of the NP. Thus,
NPs with COOH-end groups will present a negative charged surface whereas NPs from
NH2-endcapped polymers will be positively charged [14].
Hydrophobicity and PEGylation
The eﬀects of NP hydrophobicity are evident with
more hydrophobic NPs tending to be more easily internalised by cells as well bound by
serum protein and opsonising antibodies [14].
Additionally, more elastic NPs demon-
strate diﬀerent PK and BD properties than more rigid NPs, since movement through
vessels and pores in vivo is enhanced [14]. As uncoated NPs are rapidly bound by serum
proteins and opsonised by immunocomponents instantaneously when entering the blood
stream and rapidly cleared by MØs, DC and PMNs blood circulation half-life is rather
short [14]. The circulation times can be signiﬁcantly increased by surface coating with
the hydrophillic polymer PEG, termed PEGylation, where PEG acts as a shield for the
NP. PEGylated NPs experience signiﬁcantly decreased uptake by liver- and tumour cells,
decrease plasma protein binding and prolonged circulation times and thus, in today’s re-
search it is more the rule than the exception to PEGylate NPs [14]. The polymeric NPs
are not to be PEGylated here, but PEGylation will be part of the possible continuous,
future work of this project as will be discussed later.
In summary, polymeric NPs can comprise a highly suitable core for many biological appli-
cations. The build-up and biophysiochemical properties of NPs greatly inﬂuence both the
eﬃcacy, PK, BD and degradation etc. of the NP-system; properties that can be altered
and adjusted to match the speciﬁc need at hand.
As immobilisation of enzymes on NP-surfaces is seen to signiﬁcantly enhance enzyme
activity, polymeric NP systems are ideal for the purposes of sustained IL-6 signalling ab-
lation. Furthermore, utilisation of biodegradable polymeric NPs improve in vivo traits of
biologically active enzymes, limiting degradation and increasing blood circulation times
and TME accumulation. Targeting of NPs decreases systemic toxicities, thus extending,
enhancing and focusing the biological eﬀect of the enzymes compared to administration
of free enzymes.
Polymeric NPs therefore lay the foundation for the to-be developed
nanobiocatalytic system.
24
==PAGE:45==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
With IL-6 claiming a major role as pro-inﬂammatory cytokine in tumourigenesis, irre-
versible ablation is needed. The ability of Cat G to cleave IL-6 with high speciﬁcity and
activity together with the extensive advantages seen with utilisation of polymeric NP-
based delivery systems lay the foundation for the experimental development of a highly
potent nanobiocatalytic NP-system for tumour-speciﬁc IL-6 destruction.
25
==PAGE:46==
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 3
Cathepsin G and IL-6 catalysis -
Proof-of-concept
To assess and determine the eﬀectiveness of a developed polymeric NP system in cleaving
IL-6, it is ﬁrst needed to establish proof-of-concept of IL-6 catalysis by Cat G. By inves-
tigating applicable methods for detection of IL-6 cleaving, the following chapter lays the
foundation for the use of Cat G in the to-be developed polymeric NP-system in the con-
text of IL-6 catalysis and describes diﬀerent potential methods and techniques applicable
for assessment of IL-6 cleavage by Cat G. Multiple approaches to assess IL-6 integrity, size
etc. exist including methods such as ELISA, SDS-PAGE and MALDI-TOF, investigated
here. The methodology behind and the results yielded from experiments using the tech-
nique is described for each of the methods. The results lay the foundation for discussion
of the applicability and eﬀectiveness of each technique in measuring cleaved IL-6 by Cat
G. Additionally, the catalytic eﬃciency of Cat G is studied for later comparison with the
fully developed NP system.
3.1
ELISA
Enzyme-Linked Immunosorbent Assay (ELISA) is a biochemical assay for detecting and
quantifying antigens, cytokines, protein levels, serological components etc. ELISA has
been developed and utilised extensively in research and development of numerous drug
candidates. The method has been favoured due to its high speciﬁcity towards either an
epitope or antibody of interest. This means that only presence of a speciﬁc antigen or
cytokine will induce a measurable signal in the assay [71]. In this project, ELISA is utilised
for quantiﬁcation of IL-6 levels. It is equally investigated if the assay can diﬀerentiate
between intact and cleaved IL-6 in order to determine the applicability of the assay in
assessing the enzymatic cleavage of IL-6.
3.1.1
Methodology
ELISA is based on the concept of the ability of Abs to bind with incomparable speciﬁcity
towards a given antigen. Due to this speciﬁcity, only the antigen, or epitope, for which the
antibody is speciﬁc towards will be bound to the antigen-binding site in the hypervariable
region at the interface between the heavy- and light chain of the Ab [71]. As a result, a
27
==PAGE:48==
CHAPTER 3. IL-6 CATALYSIS - PROOF-OF-CONCEPT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
biological complex will form that is available for further secondary Ab-binding and signal
induction. However, if the Ab-speciﬁc antigen is not present in a sample, a secondary Ab
cannot adhere to the complex and thus no signal is induced.
Being a well-known and well-developed diagnostic method, there are multiple variations
of ELISA. Direct, indirect and sandwich ELISAs have been developed. Here, an indi-
rect ELISA is utilised for the detection and quantiﬁcation of recombinant human (rh)
IL-6. Typically, 96-well surface-coated such as Nunc MaxiSorpTM microplates are used,
allowing a capture Ab to bind to and coat the surface at the bottom of the well. The
capture Ab, designated for capturing the antigen, binds with its Fc region to the well
bottom, presenting the IL-6-binding sites towards the exterior environment. Upon addi-
tion of sample, only IL-6 will bind to the capture Ab and remain in the well when the
excess sample is washed away. Following IL-6 capture, a biotinylated secondary, detection
Ab is allowed binding to IL-6 [72]. Both capture and detection Abs are anti-IL-6 Abs,
typically mammal, but with each originating from a diﬀerent species e.g.
mouse and
goat. By utilising Abs from distinct species, any unspeciﬁc binding of the detection Ab
to the capture Ab is precluded, thus not providing a false positive signal. Biotinylation of
the secondary antibody allows streptavidin to be linked to the complex through a strong
streptavidin-Biotin non-covalent bond [73]. Streptavidin is conjugated to horse radish
peroxidase (HRP), which facilitates substrate conversion and oxidation of tetramethyl-
benzidine (TMB) to oxTMB, inducing development of a turquoise colour. As the colour
is developed, the reaction is stopped by adding an acidic solution of pH < 1, reducing
TMB and resulting in a yellow solution. The colorimetric product absorbs at 450 nm [72].
In Figure 3.1, a schematic overview of the indirect ELISA described is depicted.
The concentration quantiﬁcation from sample absorbance at 450 nm is conducted based
on a logarithmic standard curve generated based on the absorbance of standard sam-
ples with known concentrations [72]. From average duplicate absorbance measurements
and subtraction of blank absorbances, alleged concentration of each known-concentration
sample is calculated. For the logarithmic standard curve ﬁt, Log10 is calculated for both
the known concentration and the measured absorbance. Determining a linear regression
between the logarithmic absorbance and concentration, the correlation is given as
Log10(Abs) = a · Log10(Conc.) + b
(3.1)
where a is the slope of the curve and b the intersection on the Y-axis.
The concentration is thus determinable as
Conc. = 10
Log10(Abs) −b
a
(3.2)
Based on Equation 3.2, the [IL-6] in samples can then be determined from the Log10
of the sample absorbances. Any possible dilution factors during sample preparation is
accounted for here.
Due to the maximum sensitivity of this type of ELISA, standard IL-6 samples are typically
prepared in the concentration range of 32.5-2000 pg/ml IL-6 and are analysed in duplicates
[72]. The maximum [IL-6] is limited by the maximum sensitivity before saturation of the
28
==PAGE:49==
CHAPTER 3. IL-6 CATALYSIS - PROOF-OF-CONCEPT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Figure 3.1:
Schematic overview of ELISA. Blue/orange Ab bound to the
well-bottom; capture Ab, red miniature protein; IL-6, green/purple Ab; biotiny-
lated detection Ab, HRP; Horseradish Peroxidase conjugated to Streptavidin,
S; TMB substrate, P; oxTMB product emitting a turquoise light.
ELISA, as reported in Appendix C, whereas the lower limit is demarcated by the lower
limit of detection. In case of over-saturation, no useful correlation between increasing
absorbance and [IL-6] can be generated.
Cat G inhibition and unit activity
Incubated samples with Cat G and IL-6 are measured for [IL-6] by ELISA and at the
end of reaction Cat G activity must be inhibited at an appropriate time. Cat G can be
inhibited by use of multiple serine protease inhibitors such as diisopropylﬂuorophosphate
(DFP) and 4-(2-aminoethyl)benzenesulfonyl ﬂuoride hydrochloride (AEBSF). Both com-
pounds act by binding to the serine-residue in the active site, thus irreversibly inhibiting
the eﬀects of the enzyme [74, 75]. However, DFP is a highly potent neurotoxin with oral
LD50 in rats of 3.3 mg/kg [76] and is unstable in aqueous solution [77]. Commercially
available AEBSF, such as Pefabloc® SC (Pefabloc), is less toxic than DFP while retaining
inhibitory eﬀect of Cat G and being directly soluble and stable in water [78]. Pefabloc is
therefore highly useful for experimental inhibition of Cat G. See Appendix D.1 for applied
concentrations of Pefabloc.
Commercially available Cat G from diﬀerent vendors was used in the experiments with
varying enzymatic strengths of Cat G, denoted in Units. Units of Cat G strength is based
on the rate of conversion and cleavage of the peptide substrate Suc-Ala-Ala-Pro-Phe-
pNA, see Section 2.6.3: 1 Unit of Cat G from Sigma Aldrich, here denoted as UnitSigma,
corresponds to the conversion of 1 nanomol Suc-AAPF-pNA substrate per second at pH
29
==PAGE:50==
CHAPTER 3. IL-6 CATALYSIS - PROOF-OF-CONCEPT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
= 7.5 at 37 .
1 Unit of Cat G from Athens Research, here denoted as UnitAthens,
corresponds to the conversion of 1 µmol Suc-AAPF-pNA substrate per minute, equal to
16.67 nanomol substrate per second at pH = 7.5 at 37 .
With [IL-6] being 14-15 ng/ml, Sigma Aldrich [Cat G] of 2.8-3 ng/ml (0.278-0.298 ·10−3
UnitsSigma/ml) were used for initial experiments.
Cat G from Athens Research with
[Cat G] of 15 ng/ml were utilised in one experiment following unsatisfactory results and
to ensure suﬃcient enzyme concentration and activity. Strength of Cat G from Athens
Research is given in range of 0.2-0.4 ·10−3UnitsAthens/ml and is onward assumed to 0.4
·10−3UnitsAthens/ml. 15 ng/ml Athens Research’ Cat G equals 0.06 ·10−3UnitsAthens/ml.
3.1.2
Results
Multiple studies have been setup and conducted in order to investigate the sensitivity and
applicability of ELISA to detect IL-6 and IL-6 fragments following Cat G cleavage. In all
studies, ELISA was performed as is described in Section 8.1.2.
ELISA has been utilised for quantifying [IL-6] up to 140,000 pg/ml [79], however standard
concentration up to 2000 pg/ml was suggested from the commercial supplier for the type
of ELISA. The applicable [IL-6] range and maximum ELISA sensitivity were therefore in-
vestigated in a separate high concentration ELISA study, reported in Appendix C. Here,
it was determined that 2000 pg/ml comprise the maximum applicable [IL-6] for use in
ELISA, independent of use of standard ELISA kit IL-6 or rh IL-6. Linear correlations
between absorbance and concentration were seen below 2000 pg/ml IL-6. Rh IL-6 was
used in incubations with Cat G. Hence, rh IL-6 in the range of 32.5-2000 pg/ml for IL-6
standard cuves was used going forward.
As essential to investigating the applicability of ELISA for quantifying IL-6 cleavage,
samples following incubations with IL-6 and Cat G were measured using the assay. Incu-
bations investigating Cat G activity were conducted for 15, 30 and 60 minutes, based on
previously conducted similar experiments [23, 80, 81, 82]. Incubations were performed as
described in Section 8.1.3. Incubations with IL-6 and Cat G, reference IL-6 and reference
Cat G incubations, all N = 6, were conducted and measured by ELISA in a 96-well plate
layout similar to the one overviewed as Table D.1 in Appendix D.
A 1:5 Cat G:IL-6 concentration ratio was applied based on previous studies [23]. For
incubations, initial [IL-6] of 14-15,000 pg/ml were used and subsequently diluted three-
fold, yielding expected reference [IL-6] of 4667-5000 pg/ml. Although these levels were
well above the suitable [IL-6] for ELISA of 2000 pg/ml, the concentrations were utilised
since at least some drop in IL-6 signalling was expected both for reference samples but in
particular samples incubated both with IL-6 and Cat G.
In Table 3.1, an overview of incubations and the subsequent ELISAs is visualised. In-
cubation conditions, reagent concentrations and speciﬁcations for each experiment are
summarised.

setup. The incubations were performed successfully, but upon analysis by ELISA it was
evident that almost no signal was measured by the assay. Mean [IL-6] for IL-6+/Cat

However, with measured [IL-6] still way below the expected 14,000 pg/ml, at least for IL-

as well as the vigorous shaking during incubation in the Tecan microplate reader had a
negative eﬀect on the proteins. Additionally, as Sigma Aldrich performs Cat G activity
assays using 100 mM 4-(2-hydroxyethyl)-1-piperazineethanesulfonic acid) (HEPES) buﬀer
with no shaking, a similar approach was taken. Subsequent to ELISA studies 1.a and 1.b
it was learned that the catalytic activity of Cat G from Sigma Aldrich was less than
optimal under the suggested conditions, as is further described in Section 3.4. The study
was repeated with active Cat G from Athens Research. With this study 2, incubations
both at 4 , Room Temperature (RT) and 37  were conducted to investigate the
eﬀects of temperature during incubations using 15,000 pg/ml IL-6 samples. To ensure
visible eﬀect of Cat G on IL-6 cleavage, [Cat G] was raised to the equivalent of [IL-6]
of 15,000 pg/ml in a 1:1 ratio. Upon transfer from incubation to ELISA plates, samples
with diluted 12-fold to bring [IL-6] within < 2000 pg/ml for the ELISA. Figure 3.2 shows

the determined mean [IL-6] for all samples obtained from the study, based on the data
