The assay was only developed for 2-4 minutes as rapid colour development occurred.
Previous, non-reported ELISA experiments had shown rapid oversaturation of ELISA
plates, thus precautious approach of development stoppage was taken, likely stopping the
development before it was too soon. Hence, this probably inadequate development time
likely aﬀected the observed [IL-6] and thus explains the magnitude and lack of expected
[IL-6]. The implications of the results are discussed below.
3.1.3
Discussion
Based on the performed experiments involving ELISA, it is evident that IL-6 can be
detected and quantiﬁed by ELISA, as has previously been utilised in research. However,
with the observed results from Figure 3.2, it is equally clear that the ELISA set up
here is incapable of quantitatively distinguishing between [IL-6] in IL-6+/Cat G+ samples
and reference IL-6+/Cat G−samples. At all time points, mean [IL-6] in IL-6+/Cat G+
samples were measured to similar or higher values compared to mean [IL-6] of reference
IL-6 samples, within the standard deviation (SD). The trend is seen at both 4 , RT
and 37 . Additionally, at 4  after 30 min, RT after 60 min and 37  after 30 min
incubation [IL-6] in IL-6+ reference samples is comparably lower than measured [IL-6] in
respective IL-6+/Cat G+ samples. This indicates that the presence of Cat G in incubation
samples may cleave rh IL-6, but the eﬀect is not measurable and quantiﬁable by ELISA
under this setup and the conditions given here.
A concern might be that Cat G is only added with a 1:5 ratio to IL-6 and therefore
may not have been able to induce a measurable decrease in intact [IL-6]. However, it is
reasonable to assume that some catalytic eﬀect of Cat G would be expected at least after
60 minutes incubation, resulting in a decrease in [IL-6]. This is not seen here. Therefore,
the results indicate that quantities of intact IL-6 between reference and Cat G-containing
samples cannot be distinguished by the use of the above ELISA setup, despite expected
decrease in [IL-6] following Cat G incubation. Thus, the assay cannot be used to diﬀer-
entiate between cleaved and non-cleaved IL-6.
The fact that ELISA evidently is not able to quantitatively diﬀerentiate between cleaved
and non-cleaved IL-6 might be due to the setup and design of the assay. Both the captur-
ing and detecting Ab supposedly bind to speciﬁc epitopes on the IL-6 protein. Whilst the
speciﬁc epitope is unknown, it is unlikely that the detection Ab is binding to the exact
peptide sequence of IL-6 containing the cleavage site of the protein. Hence, binding of the
detection Ab to the IL-6 epitope is not restricted by the intactness nor fragmentation of
IL-6. This explains why detected IL-6 and thus measured [IL-6] is within similar quanti-
ties between IL-6+/Cat+ and IL-6+/Cat G−samples. Even though some conformational
change in the IL-6 protein must be expected following cleavage, the detection Ab will
with all likeliness still bind to the intact IL-6 epitope, inducing the destined signal of IL-6
presence - cleaved or not. The optimal solution hereto would be the designing of a detec-
tion Ab with speciﬁcity towards the peptide sequence of IL-6, wherein the cleavage site
by Cat G is present. By doing so, the detection Ab would exclusively bind to intact IL-6
and no or lowered signal would be observed for samples with fragmented IL-6, compared
33
==PAGE:54==
CHAPTER 3. IL-6 CATALYSIS - PROOF-OF-CONCEPT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
to samples with intact IL-6.
A tendency of decreased [IL-6] for both IL-6+/Cat G−and IL-6+/Cat G+ samples with
increased incubation time is observed, in particular for samples incubated at 37  but
to lesser extent at RT. As the trend is observed for both types of samples, it is deemed
unrelated to the presence of Cat G during incubation. Although initially determined for
IL-18, it has previously been shown that increasing incubation temperature might neg-
atively aﬀect the detectability of the IL-18 epitope by ELISA, visualised by decreased
[IL-18] measured by the assay [83]. However, despite decreased epitope recognition of
[IL-18] by ELISA, the biological function and activity of IL-18 is preserved in other bioac-
tivity assays. Hence, protein integrity and activity is therefore still maintained, even if
immunochemical recognition may be impaired with increasing incubation time. A similar
aspect is contemplated upon with regards to IL-6 as with increasing incubation temper-
ature and durations, [IL-6] determined by ELISA decreases. Measured [IL-6] for IL-6+
reference samples incubated at 37  constitutes 34.2 % of [IL-6] after 15 minutes, whereas
[IL-6] after 60 min incubation at RT is reduced to 64.3 % of [IL-6] after 15 min incuba-
tion, notably less than seen for incubation at 37 . This indicates a similar trend as has
been seen for IL-18 and may suggest that the IL-6 epitope is decreasingly recognised by
ELISA with increasing incubation temperatures. However, despite this reduced epitope
recognition, IL-6 might still be active and functional - or maybe even be cleaved in Cat
G+ samples.
Adjustments of experimental conditions such as temperature, reaction buﬀer, pH etc.
in the ELISA could have been performed in order to optimise the measured signalling
and determined [IL-6]. However, the observed results indicate that the use of the set up
ELISA here is not useful to measure and quantify a diﬀerence in [IL-6] between reference
IL-6 samples and IL-6 samples incubated with Cat G. It was therefore determined to
be invaluable to this project to further optimise the assay and deemed that ELISA was
unsuitable for the quantiﬁcation of cleaved IL-6 compared to intact IL-6 as per observed
results. Based on this, focus was shifted from ELISA to other techniques in the attempt
to identify suitable methods for detection and quantiﬁcation of IL-6 cleavage following
incubation with Cat G.
34
==PAGE:55==
CHAPTER 3. IL-6 CATALYSIS - PROOF-OF-CONCEPT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
3.2
SDS-PAGE
Sodium Docecyl Sulfate-PolyAcrylamide Gel Electrophoresis (SDS-PAGE) is a useful tool
and technique to analyse and separate proteins and enzymes from a sample based on size.
While the method has no real upper and lower limitations in protein size, it works well
within the 1-200 kDa range. Although the method is qualitative rather than quantitative,
the technique provides clear evidence of the presence of protein or -fragments of particular
sizes in a protein sample, regardless of protein function, origin, charge, integrity etc.
3.2.1
Methodology
While the technique is very robust and is extensively used in research, it provides some
ﬂexibility with regards to buﬀers, gels etc. to be used. However, despite being easy and
quick to conduct, SDS-PAGE has limited applicability e.g. towards detecting multiple
proteins separately of similar sizes. Equally, higher protein concentration is required for
each experiment in comparision with e.g. ELISAs.
The concept behind the analysis is migration of protein upon an electric force, known
as electrophoresis [84]. In a protein mixture sample, smaller protein will migrate faster
through a gel compared to larger ones due to the sheer size of the protein compared to
the gel pore sizes [84]. Gels such as Bis-Tris polyacrylamide gels are typically used with
varying Bis-Tris content, thickness etc. dependent on the desired experiment conditions.
Gels are composed of a stacking and a running gel with 2-20 wells carved out in the top
stacking gel part. In 1mm 10-well 10-12 % Bis-Tris gels, used here, wells can contain 25 µl
sample [85]. Samples of interest, e.g. pure protein samples or incubation samples with IL-
6 and Cat G, are prepared using Sample Buﬀer (SB), being a reducing agent containing
β-mercaptoethanol and SDS. β-mercaptoethanol denatures proteins by disulﬁde bridges
whereas SDS denatures secondary and tertiary protein structures [86]. SDS also applies
a negative charge to the protein, allowing the proteins to migrate electrophoretically
through the gel upon application of electric voltage [86]. Following SB addition, protein
samples are heated at 70  for 10 min to further ensure protein denaturation. Denatured
samples are then loaded into the respective wells of the gel, situated in a gel cassette
and electric voltage is applied to the system. A running buﬀer, typically MOPS or MES,
allows the running of the gel electrophoresis. The choice of buﬀer, here MES, also has
eﬀect on the migration pattern and separation of proteins as does the gel of choice.
As the positive anode is located at the bottom of the gel, the electrical current causes
the negatively charged proteins to migrate downwards. Each protein will migrate and
be visualised as a transverse band. The SB dye will run ahead of the sample protein,
allowing visualisation of when the assay should be stopped. Following electrophoresis, a
staining agent is utilised to visualise the protein bands. Although multiple options exist
as staining agent Coomassie blue is applied here, in the form of SimplyBlueTM SafeStain.
Coommassie blue acts as staining agent of the protein by binding to positively charged
protein residues and through hydrophobic Van der Waals-interactions [87].
Based on
the migrated distance, sample protein sizes are determined by comparison with a protein
ladder also run on the PAGE together with the samples. The ladder contains a protein
mixture with pre-determined protein sizes ranging from e.g. 1.7-40 kDa or 10-180 kDa.
35
==PAGE:56==
CHAPTER 3. IL-6 CATALYSIS - PROOF-OF-CONCEPT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Sizes of sample protein can therefore be assessed by comparing migration distances with
the ladder proteins.
3.2.2
Results
A series of studies involving SDS-PAGE has been conducted, overviewed in Table 3.3. As
the concentration sensitivity as well as applicability of the assay were investigated, varying
reagent concentrations and enzymes were utilised. The studies were conducted using the
procedure described in Section 8.2, except for studies for concentration sensitivity where
no incubations were conducted.
Table 3.3: Overview of SDS-PAGE proof-of-concepts experiment. For all
experiments, Cat G was used as enzyme for cleaving IL-6, except in study 3
where NE also was applied in separate incubation samples.
Study

formed to investigate the sensitivity of the assay and at what [IL-6] and [Cat G] visible
bands on the gels would appear. The ran gels are seen in Figure 3.3. From the studies it
is evident that intact IL-6 is detectable with [IL-6] > 5µg/ml with a band around 25 kDa
whereas the sensitivity of Cat G acquired from Sigma Aldrich is higher with sensitivity
around >37.5 µg/ml. Cat G is seen as a band around 29 kDa, corresponding to previously
listed in Table 2.1. It can be argued that the sensitivity should be similar as the measured
proteins present similar size, although the observed results indicate otherwise for Cat G
from Sigma Aldrich.

3.2.3
Discussion
It is clear that SDS-PAGE constitutes a useful technique for detection of decreased pres-
ence of intact IL-6 following incubations at 15, 30, 60 and 120 min with Cat G in compari-
son with reference IL-6 incubation based on the observed trends. Following incubations, a
time-dependent loss of intact IL-6 in samples with Cat G acquired from Athens Research
is visible and measurable. A diminishing presence of IL-6 is observed following extended
incubations with Cat G, indicating a catalytic cleaving activity of Cat G towards IL-6 and
that this cleaving is prolonged with increased incubation times. This trend is particularly
evident from signal strength quantiﬁcations shown in Figure 3.8, where the %-wise drop
in IL-6 band strength is seen. After 15 min incubation with Cat G, IL-6 band integrity is
reduced by a signiﬁcant ∼60 % in both study 5.a and 5.b. Although signal reduction is
enhanced with incubation times, to a maximum of ∼95 % reduction and thus approach-
ing completely removal of IL-6, the increase in signal drop is slowed. Hence, it is proposed
that the catalytic reaction velocity of IL-6-cleavage by Cat G drops as [IL-6] decreases
during incubations. This trend of slowed catalytic activity is similar to the trend seen
41
==PAGE:62==
CHAPTER 3. IL-6 CATALYSIS - PROOF-OF-CONCEPT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Figure 3.8: Signal strengths of IL-6 bands around ∼25 kDa in SDS-PAGE
experiments 5.a and 5.b.
The strengths were measured using the LI-COR
Odyssey® FC gel imager software. ’IL-6 Signal % decrease’ is percentage de-
crease in IL-6 signalling between Cat G+ and Cat G−samples. The underlying
data is shown in Appendix E.1.
in enzyme kinetics study, such as conducted in Section 3.4. It therefore showcases the
catalytic eﬀect and ability of Cat G towards IL-6 and that with decreasing substrate IL-6
presence over time, the corresponding decrease in [IL-6] is correspondingly slowed. The
IL-6 band strength drop seen for free Cat G incubations is compared to similar experi-
ments for NP-conjugated Cat G in Section 5.1.1.
In the reported results, IL-6 is seen as a clear band with the approximate size of 25 kDa.
Both IL-6 and Athens Research Cat G are detectable in concentrations of > 5µg/ml. Rh
IL-6 is listed with the size 20.3 kDa from the commercial supplier [88], but this size diﬀer-
ence is most likely due to diﬀerences in experimental setup [89]. Although the apparent
size IL-6 of of 25 kDa might suggest possible O-glycosylation of rh IL-6 [90], this is not the
case as rh IL-6 has been expressed in E. coli without any post-translational modiﬁcations
[89]. Similarly, bands of rh IL-6 with molecular weight of >22 kDa have previously been
detected by Western Blotting [23].
As mentioned, a series of bands at ∼10 kDa in study 5.a and at ∼13.5 kDa in study 5.b
can be seen vaguely in Cat G+/IL-6+ samples. It is believed, that these bands might
be fragmented IL-6, although the band strength is weak. This corresponds well with
previously reported results where an intact IL-6 protein weighed an apparent 20.1 kDa
42
==PAGE:63==
CHAPTER 3. IL-6 CATALYSIS - PROOF-OF-CONCEPT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
and where IL-6 fragments were estimated to be approximately 9 and 12 kDa following
cleavage by Cat G [23]. As the here determined weight of the commercial rh IL-6 is around
25 kDa an increased apparent weight of intact IL-6, compared to previously reported IL-6,
would similarly increase the weight of IL-6 fragments here. Hence, the visible fragments
of around 10 and 13.5 kDa may correspond to the previously reported IL-6 fragments of
9 and 12 kDa [23].
Following this presumption of IL-6 fragmentation, IL-6 fragments should be visualised
vaguely in studies with 40 µg/ml IL-6+/Cat G+ samples, similar to study 5.b. Yet, no
increased 13.5 kDa fragment signalling is seen over time, proportionally to the increase
loss in intact IL-6. Thus, despite disruption of intact IL-6, an increased IL-6 fragmention
is not seen as would else be expected.
The method set up here with the conditions
applied in the above experiments has therefore not showed absolute, clear applicability
in detecting and quantifying cleaved IL-6 fragments. However, it is able to clearly show
a time-dependent decrease in intact IL-6 after incubations with Cat G and increasing
incubation times. Despite detectable fragmentation of IL-6 being of major interest, SDS-
PAGE and the set up method is nevertheless deemed a highly functional technique for
IL-6 cleaving detection.
The observed results here are comparable with what has else been reported in the litera-
ture using Western Blotting with IL-6 being cleavable by enzymes and in particular Cat G
[23, 91]. SDS-PAGE is therefore concluded to be highly useful in detecting IL-6 cleavage
by Cat G and experiments with SDS-PAGE is therefore included in the investigation of
the ability of developed NPs to cleave IL-6.
3.3
MALDI-TOF
Matrix Assisted Laser Desorption Ionization (MALDI)-Time-of-Flight (TOF) is a tech-
nique used to measure and quantify presence of chemical compounds, peptides, proteins
etc. in a given sample. Based on knowledge about the sample composition, MALDI-TOF
can be utilised to verify or gain information about the presence of speciﬁc compounds,
potential biproducts and impurities.
In this thesis, MALDI-TOF and mass spectrometry (MS) is used to quantify IL-6 and
-fragments as well as determining the exact mass of these fragments. Previously, MS
has been used to investigate and determine cleavage site of IL-6 following enzymatic
cleavage with Meprin A and B [91] as well as with Cat G [23]. The technique is therefore
investigated for applicability in determining and quantifying Cat G cleavage of IL-6 here.
3.3.1
Methodology
The concept of MALDI-TOF is based on the ability of compounds, proteins etc. - the an-
alyte - to be ionised and ﬂy through a ﬂight tube with the aid of an aromatic, laser energy
absorbing compound - the matrix compound - and reach a detector [92]. Matrices for
use in MALDI-TOF are required to be stable in vacuum, absorb laser wavelength, able to
co-crystallise with the analyte, soluble in solvents compatible with the analyte and to pro-
mote analyte ionisation [93]. Typically matrices such as 3,5-dimethoxy-4-hydroxycinnamic
acid, also known as sinapinic acid (SA), and α-cyano-4-hydroxycinnamic acid (CHCA)
43
==PAGE:64==
CHAPTER 3. IL-6 CATALYSIS - PROOF-OF-CONCEPT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
are used [93].
Upon loading of matrix-analyte samples into the mass spectrometer, solvents are evapo-
rated in the resulting vacuum leading to drying and matrix-analyte crystallisation. Al-
though the speciﬁc mechanism remains uncertain, it is proposed that upon laser beam
shooting onto the crystallised sample, the laser beam energy is absorbed by the matrix
and then transferred to the analyte [92]. Diﬀerent approaches are used, but the nitrogen
laser used here shoots laser pulses at 337 nm in 3 ns wide pulses with 20 Hz [92]. The
energy transfer causes the analyte to ionise into gas phase with the analyte ions ﬂying
through a ﬂight tube subject to a high electrical ﬁeld. Depending on the time of ﬂight,
the ion size can be determined, with smaller ions reaching the TOF-detector faster than
larger ones [92]. The resulting spectrum generated from the TOF-measurements provide
information on the ion sizes and intensities hereof. MALDI-TOF is both applicable for
detection of small molecules as well as larger peptides and proteins [92, 93].
3.3.2
Results
The samples measured using MALDI-TOF for detection of IL-6 are overviewed in Table
3.4. As seen in the table, no incubated samples having undergone enzymatic cleavage have
been measured. All measured samples were samples containing intact IL-6, as the ability
to detect intact IL-6 by MALDI-TOF needed to be assessed at ﬁrst. All measurements
were conducted as according to the procedures described in Section 8.3.
Results of MALDI-TOF measurements of rh 100 µg/ml IL-6, reconstituted in Phosphate-
buﬀered saline (PBS), are shown in Figure 3.9(a), comprising study 1 in Table 3.4. The
sample solution is a 1:25 mix with SA matrix and is representative for the results yielded
using other sample:matrix ratios. Decreasing sample:matrix ratio samples was looked into
to ensure the ability of IL-6 to be ionised and ﬂy through the ﬂight tube. A high noise-
to-sample-signal ratio is evident from the ﬁgure. No peaks are observed around 20-25
kDa, as would else be expected for IL-6 per previous results in Section 3.2, indicating no
detection of IL-6.
Salts present in sample solution are generally seen to quench sample signal [94] and it
was thus suggested that the salts in PBS, in which IL-6 was reconstituted, had a negative
eﬀect on the measurements and resulting spectrum. To investigate if sample desalting
would improve any IL-6 signal while limiting noise, IL-6 samples were desalted before
MALDI-TOF measurements. First, desalting by ﬁltration and buﬀer exchange was con-
ducted, resulting in MALDI-TOF study 2. Samples with increasing sample:matrix ratios
were investigated. A representative spectrum of IL-6 samples from MALDI-TOF study 2
with a 1:50 sample:matrix ratio is seen in Figure 3.9(b). Evidently, no peak is observed
that can be related to the presence and detection of IL-6. Further, no decrease in back-
ground noise is observed. Additionally, desalting was conducted utilising Milipore ZipTip
C18 pipette tips, allowing for desalting and puriﬁcation of sample proteins, constituting
MALDI-TOF study 3. Figure 3.9(c) shows a representative MALDI-TOF spectrum for
samples puriﬁed using ZipTip pipette tips. Comparing the intensity scale between the
spectra from studies 1, 2 and 3, a signiﬁcant decrease in background noise is observed
after desalting with ZipTip pipette tips. This indicates that the ZipTip pipette tips suc-

As MALDI-TOF has previously been used to investigate cleavage sites of IL-6 follow-

as per SDS-PAGE band procedure described in Section 8.3.2. The resulting MALDI-TOF
spectra are seen in Figures 3.9(d) and 3.9(e). Contrary to MALDI-TOF studies 1-3, a
peak at 20-25 kDa is not expected here, as the samples have been treated with Trypsin.
However, following Trypsin cleavage, smaller fragments of IL-6 would be expected to
appear on the spectra, although this is not seen here for neither spectrum.

3.3.3
Discussion
From Figure 3.9, it is evident that IL-6 has not been detected in quantiﬁable levels in any
of the analysed samples with no signal peaks in the size ranges of 20-25 kDa. The studies
conducted using MALDI-TOF included a wide range of both [IL-6] spotted for MALDI-
TOF analysis as well as sample:matrix ratios. The decreased sample:matrix-ratio should
have allowed the sample proteins to be ionised and ﬂy through the ﬂight tube and with
[IL-6] ranging from ∼1-800 femtomol, some signal related to IL-6 presence would have
been expected. Nevertheless, no improved peaks and signalling from IL-6 was observed
in neither of the analysed samples.
46
==PAGE:67==
CHAPTER 3. IL-6 CATALYSIS - PROOF-OF-CONCEPT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Measures were taken to desalt IL-6 samples, either by sample wash and centrifugation
or by use of Millipore ZipTip pipette tips. In particular desalting by use of Millipore
ZipTip pipette tips were seen to lower the spectrum background noise compared to spec-
trum from non-desalted samples. The lowered background noise would give basis for a
more clear IL-6 signal, but no improved signal related to IL-6 presence was observed.
Desalting samples before analysis by MALDI-TOF was therefore seen not to enhance
any protein-related signalling. Similar to the spectra from studies 1-3, the MALDI-TOF
spectra of IL-6 samples cut from SDS-PAGE gel in study 4 did not show any increase in
peak signalling other than the already observed background noise in the 2-3 kDa range.
Thus, the resulting spectra indicate no improvement in IL-6 or IL-6 fragment detection
following Trypsin treatment. On the other hand, it is also worth considering if the protein
extraction treatment was suﬃcient for IL-6 extraction from the gel. If the SDS-PAGE
gel had not been properly disintegrated for IL-6 extraction, it would explain the lack of
signal from potential IL-6 fragments.
The results from the four conducted studies using MALDI-TOF indicate that the tech-
nique is not applicable for detecting presence of IL-6 using the setup applied here with
the calibrated method. However, MALDI-TOF has previously been used extensively to
detect peptides and proteins in various samples [23, 91]. It is therefore highly likely that
the setup, approach and calibrated method utilised here is the limiting factor for IL-6
detection, rather than MALDI-TOF itself not being a suitable technique for protein de-
tection. In order to further develop a functional MALDI-TOF method for IL-6 detection,
optimisation of the methods applied here would be a required step forward in the attempt
to utilise MALDI-TOF for the purpose at hand.
As example of method optimisation, investigation of the eﬀects of other matrices than
SA such as CHCA to induce ionisation of IL-6 would be an ideal step. Multiple possible
matrices suitable for MALDI-TOF exist and although SA is utilised to great extent and
should have been able to present a detectable signal for IL-6, it might not be most suitable
for IL-6 ionisation.
Additionally, the application and involvement of trypsin in protein extraction and prepa-
ration of proteins from SDS-PAGE gel samples could further be exploited by peptide mass
ﬁngerprinting (PMF). Incubating protein samples with certain enzymes such as Trypsin
allows for speciﬁc cleavage of proteins into peptides. As Trypsin will cleave a protein
resulting in presence of speciﬁc peptides, these peptides of exact masses can be used to
identify the intact protein that has undergone Trypsin-treatment and -cleavage. The sizes
of these peptides can then be determined by MALDI-TOF and by using the mass of four
to six peptides, a speciﬁc originator-protein can be determined with <0.05 % accuracy [92].
Although the approach of PMF is with great potential and that both PMF as well as
the use of other matrices could have been investigated, no further optimisations than the
applied in the conducted studies were performed here due to the limited time available
and focus of this project. In addition, since no successful results in detecting rh IL-6 using
MALDI-TOF were yielded, no eﬀorts were made to investigate incubated samples with
IL-6 and Cat G as not even intact IL-6 was detectable by MALDI-TOF. Further use of
MALDI-TOF here was discontinued.
47
==PAGE:68==
CHAPTER 3. IL-6 CATALYSIS - PROOF-OF-CONCEPT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
3.4
Enzyme kinetics study
As opposed to the techniques described above for assessment of IL-6 cleaving, enzyme
kinetic studies of Cat G is initially unrelated to the speciﬁc cleaving of IL-6. This is due
to the fact that no commercially available IL-6-based substrates for Cat G are available.
Hence, the activity of Cat G towards ﬂourescent or absorbant substrates resembling the
cleavage site amino acid sequence of IL-6 is assessed to get an understanding of the rate
of substrate conversion - and IL-6 cleaving.
3.4.1
Methodology
The determination of an enzyme’s rate of activity and speciﬁcity towards a certain sub-
strate (S) is what is known as enzyme kinetics studies. Kinetic studies are performed
to investigate and quantify with what rate an enzyme performs its speciﬁc action - here,
convert a substrate into product (P). Enzyme kinetics are useful both for investigating
catalytic rate of free enzymes as well as enzyme-incorporated systems. Equally, kinetics
studies can also provide information about an enzyme’s speciﬁcity towards diﬀerent sub-
strates.
The overall concept behind this is the investigation and determination of the enzymatic
rate activity of Cat G based on its activity towards a speciﬁc substrate over time. Based
on the measurable change in substrate or product concentration, [S] and [P] respectively,
the kinetic parameters of the enzyme can be established. As brieﬂy touched upon in
Section 2.6.3, enzymatic parameters of interest include Vmax, KM and kcat. Vmax is given
as the maximum reaction rate of the enzyme and KM, the Michaelis constant, is the [S]
at 1
2Vmax. The turnover number kcat is the catalytic rate of conversion from substrate to
product by the enzyme [95]. In general, a smaller KM value indicates high enzyme aﬃnity
towards a substrate, whereas higher kcat/KM points to higher substrate speciﬁcity and
higher enzyme eﬃciency in converting a substrate into product [95].
Multiple angles to assess and determine the enzyme kinetic parameters exist, although
the study of colorimetric-based substrates has been widely utilised approach in the lit-
erature [52, 54, 56, 57, 96]. Therefore, for the study of Cat G in this work, a similar
colorimetric-based approach is applied.
The activity and speciﬁcity of Cat G towards multiple enzymatic substrates have previ-
ously been assessed, overviewed in Appendix B. As described in Section 2.6.1, the cleavage
site of IL-6 is the peptide bond between Phe78-Asn79, with Phe78 in the P1-position of
IL-6. Therefore, for investigation of Cat G activity the previously used substrate Suc-
Ala-Ala-Pro-Phe-pNA [54], is used in order to resemble cleavable sequence of IL-6 to the
highest extent. Upon enzymatic cleavage the reaction yields the product pNA, which
absorbs light around 410 nm. The reaction scheme of the reaction is seen in Figure 3.10.
Over incubation time, this increased absorbance at 410 nm following incubation with Cat
G is utilised to quantify the product [pNA] following enzymatic cleavage of the substrate.
[pNA] is determined from absorbance based on a standard concentration curve of pure
pNA. From [pNA] development, enzymatic kinetic parameters of Cat G can be determined
by Michaelis-Menten kinetics [95].

where kcat is the reaction rate constant for the conversion step of the ES-complex to E +

At conditions where [S] >> KM, KM can be neglected and the reaction rate becomes
independent of [S]. During this ’steady state’ scenario, typically seen early during the
reaction, all enzymes are bound to substrate and the reaction rate v approaches Vmax,
with zero-order kinetics, and Equation (3.5) applies as
Vmax = kcat[E]0
(3.5)
where [E]0 is the free enzyme concentration at time-zero [95].
Based on experimental values of the reaction velocity, v, and [S] in addition the assump-
tions and equations above, the kinetic parameters Vmax, KM and kcat of Cat G can be
determined. Using a non-linear regression approach, Vmax and KM can be calculated by
applying equation (3.3), where a calculated vcalc. is determined from Vmax and KM. By
minimising the sum of the squared errors (SSE) between the experimentally determined
vexp. and calculated vcalc., values of Vmax and KM are optimised to ﬁt the data set. SSE
is given as Equation (3.6)
SSE =

3.4.2
Results
Four experiments with decreasing [S] have been conducted concurrently together with
reference Cat G, reference substrate and a 10:10:80 dH2O:DMSO:HEPES buﬀer control.
For all experiments, n = 2. The experimental overview seen in Table 3.5 applies for the
experiments conducted with Cat G acquired from Athens Research. Experiments were
performed as described in Section 8.4.
[S] and [Cat G] for the studies involving Athens Research Cat G were based on literature
and company instructions. Previous kinetic studies of Cat G with Suc-AAPF-pNA as
substrate had utilised [S] between 0.1-3 KM for Cat G, determining Cat G KM to 1.76
mM [54]. Additionally, Sigma Aldrich assesses Cat G activity with [S] = 2.192 mM. Max-
imum [S] was therefore set at 2.5 mM, corresponding to 1.42 KM of Cat G in previous
studies [54]. A range of four diﬀerent [S0] were used in the experiment based on a 2-fold
dilution-series descending from 2.5 mM. Maximum stock [Cat G] of 100 µg/ml was ap-
plied.
From the measured absorbance, product [pNA] at each time point was calculated from
absorbance at 410 nm based on 2-fold dilution standard curve, applicable in the range of
0.04 - 1.25 mM pNA. Using [S] = [S]0−[P], [S] for each time point was calculated for each
replicate. Mean and uncertainty of [S] and [P] at each time point was then determined.

inﬂuences from Cat G-only and blank samples were observed.

3.4.3
Discussion
Evident from the conducted experiments Cat G originating from Athens Research is highly
active and eﬃcient in cleaving the substrate Suc-AAPF-pNA. Over time, an increasing [P]
has been observed, showing the cleaving of substrate into product. Naturally, with higher
[S]0 the faster [P] increases, indicated by a steeper [pNA] curve over time in particular seen
in Figure 3.12(b), as the likelihood of enzyme-substrate complex formation is increased
upon higher [S]. Additionally, as the rate of substrate conversion decreases over time,
indicated by a decreasing steepness in the curve, the observed suggests a [S]-dependent
decrease in reaction velocity over time.
The cleaving eﬃciency is seen to be quantiﬁable by the determination of kinetic parame-
ters for Cat G using Michaelis-Menten enzyme kinetics. By comparing the experimentally
established values here with values from the literature, both sets shown in Table 3.7, KM,
kcat as well as kcat/KM determined here are in similar range as values reported in the lit-
erature [54]. The required [S] to reach 1
2vmax, KM, is signiﬁcantly lower than the reported
KM, indicating higher aﬃnity of the Cat G at hand than the applied in the literature.
Although the turnover number kcat is likewise lower than reported kcat, kcat/KM is sig-
niﬁcantly higher in this experiment, indicating higher speciﬁcity of Cat G utilised here
towards Suc-AAPF-pNA than in the literature [54]. After all, Cat G utilised here has
been shown to have eﬃciency to the same extent and beyond what has previously been
reported by others.
From Figure 3.12(a), the plateaus of ﬁnal [P] reached deviates slightly compared to the
expected end [P]. As the substrate-to-product cleaving reaction is 1→1, [P]final would be
expected to reach same levels as [S]0 at the end of each experiment. Whereas the study
with [S]0 = 1.25 mM satisﬁes this expectation, [P] with [S]0 = 2.5 mM fails to increase
beyond 1.6 mM. This is due to absorbance oversaturation, where the maximum detectable
absorbance by the Tecan Microplate Reader is reached. The end [P] of this study is there-
fore expected to be higher than indicated from the experiment. Furthermore, [P]final for
experiments with [S]0 = 0.3125 mM and 0.625 mM are seen to increase beyond the ex-
pected, which may be due to experimental variations in standard concentration curve or
measurements by the microplate reader. In any case, the late-stage absorbance oversat-
uration and inaccurate [P]final compared to the expected is not believed to inﬂuence the
rate of increasing absorbance, and thus [P], during the initial part of the experiment,
which is ultimately utilised for determination of kinetic parameters.
53
==PAGE:74==
CHAPTER 3. IL-6 CATALYSIS - PROOF-OF-CONCEPT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
A similar experiment, as the one described utilising Athens Research Cat G was set up
using Cat G acquired from Sigma Aldrich, where at time zero [S]0 was 1.25 µg/ml and
0.625 µg/ml and [Cat G] = 6.201 µg/ml. Although the experiment was only performed
with n = 1, minimal increase in absorbance at 410 nm was observed over 180 minutes,
in particular in comparison with the observed results from the experiment using Cat G
acquired from Athens Research. The experiment indicated no activity of Sigma Aldrich
Cat G. It thus veriﬁed the previously observed results for SDS-PAGE experiments in Sec-
tion 3.2, where minimal cleavage of IL-6 following incubations with Sigma Aldrich Cat
G had been observed. Based on these combined observations, it was determined that
Cat G from Sigma Aldrich failed to show an eﬀect ﬁt for the experiments and intended
design here. Hence, all incubations from this point on involving Cat G was conducted
using Cat G from Athens Research, as this evidently showed high eﬃciency in cleaving
Suc-AAPF-pNA.
Although the set up method and experiments did not involve Cat G cleavage of IL-6
the approach behind enzyme kinetics studies of Suc-AAPF-pNA cleavage is nevertheless
deemed highly useful in investigating the catalytic ability of Cat G. Furthermore, the
cleavage site of the substrate resembles the presumed cleavage site of IL-6, also pointing
to the fact that Cat G is useful in cleaving IL-6. A similar experiment can and will there-
fore be set up using developed Cat G-conjugated NPs for testing the activity of these.
Ultimately, this allows for comparing the catalytic eﬃciency of NP-surface coupled Cat
G with free Cat G. A similar comparison of enzyme activity between free and scaﬀolded
enzyme has previously been performed to show a 5-fold increase in kcat following enzyme
immobilisation [67], showcasing the potential great improvement and eﬀect on catalytic
activity using nanobiocatalytic approach.
By utilising the approaches of ELISA, SDS-PAGE, MALDI-TOF and enzyme kinetics, ex-
tensive insight into the catalytic activity of Cat G towards IL-6 has been yielded. Whereas
the results from ELISA and MALDI-TOF experiment with the set up methodology here
indicate limited applicability of these techniques for assessment of IL-6 cleavage by Cat
G, SDS-PAGE is shown to be highly feasible in assessing time-dependent cleavage of IL-
6 following incubations with Cat G. Additionally, enzyme kinetic parameters of Cat G
have been established. SDS-PAGE and kinetic studies are therefore seen to be useful in
measuring IL-6 cleavage and investigating the catalytic activity of Cat G, respectively.
Both techniques will therefore be used accordingly in assessing the activity of the fully
developed NP system.
54
==PAGE:75==
Chapter 4
Polymeric NP development
The successful development of a polymeric nanobiocatalytic system for IL-6 ablation is
dependent on multiple diﬀerent aspects. In particular, the choices of speciﬁc polymer
exhibiting the NP core as well as the strategy for how Cat G is conjugated to the surface
of the core NPs being examples of areas of development with crucial importance. First,
the following chapter addresses the aspects of polymer core optimisation, including the
choice of polymer, as well as preparation of suitable polymeric NP cores. The applicability
of NP cores as basis for further NP-system development is then discussed. Secondly, based
on successful precipitation of core NPs, these are utilised in the work and optimisations
in bioconjugation of Cat G onto the NP surfaces. Several diﬀerent angles to this coupling
exist with the results from each approach being reported. Ultimately, the potential further
use of Cat G-conjugated NPs as nanobiocatalysts towards IL-6 cleavage is evaluated and
discussed.
4.1
Polymer core optimisation
There are multiple potential polymers for use as polymeric NP core in the development
of a nanobiocatalytic system. The choice of speciﬁc polymer to act as polymeric NP core
depends on multiple aspects including organic solvent solubility, ability to be prepared
successfully while presenting suitable and useful characteristics in size, charge, polydisper-
sity etc. As previously touched upon in Section 2.8.2, these speciﬁc characteristics greatly
inﬂuence the properties of nanoparticle stability, function and potential targeting of the
NP system. In the following section, the development and preparation of polymer-based
NPs is optimised in order to provide a suitable polymeric NP core for enzyme bioconju-
gation.
The methodology behind polymeric NP precipitation is covered ﬁrst, followed by the
results yielded using the methods and ultimately the suitable polymers for polymeric NP
core preparation are discussed and determined.
4.1.1
Methodology
Multiple ways to manufacture NPs exist and most of these methods utilise the ability
of polymers to self-assemble under the right conditions. Techniques for NP preparation
55
==PAGE:76==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
include emulsiﬁcation methods, the salting out method, electro-spraying and nanoprecip-
itation with the latter being applied in this project and thus elaborated on here. The
remaining techniques are described extensively in the literature [14, 15].
Nanoprecipitation utilises the principles of the Marangoni eﬀect, allowing hydrophobic
part of the polymers to self-assemble instantaneously into spherical particles upon contact
with an aqueous solution [15, 97, 98]. The Marangoni eﬀect occurs in the interface be-
tween two phases or solvents [98]. Upon drop-wise addition of polymer-containing organic
solution, presenting a low surface tension, into water with a comparably higher surface
tension, a surface-tension gradient is created [99]. The polymers then form nanoparticles
based on interfacial turbulence between the water and organic solvent [99]. This turbu-
lence or instability is in part due to this unequilibrated surface-tension gradient between
the two phases, in the shape of the Marangoni eﬀect [98, 99]. In this solvent:water inter-
face, spheric polymeric particles thus precipitate as the organic solvent diﬀuses into the
water phase [99]. Generally, increase in solvent:water ratio and applied polymer concen-
tration is seen to impact the success of nanodroplet and nanoparticle formation [97].
In this way, polymers will form a spherical core denoted as the polymeric NP. In this
project, the polymer of interest is dissolved in an organic solvent, e.g. acetonitrile (ACN),
dimethylformamide (DMF) or tetrahydrofuran (THF). The organic solution is added
drop-wise to water under stirring causing the NP formation. A schematic overview of the
nanoprecipitation process is shown in Figure 4.1. The organic solvent is then evaporated
through continuous stirring ultimately yielding spheric polymeric NPs [14]. Puriﬁcation of
the precipitated NPs follows by centrifugation using size-dependent 50 kDa cutoﬀﬁlters,
allowing ﬁltering out unprecipitated polymer typically with sizes of 5-10 kDa [100, 101].
If the polymer contains an end-group, such as -NH2 or -COOH, these charged end-groups
will face outwards from the polymeric NP core towards the aqueous surroundings due
to their hydrophilicity [14]. The NPs can both be precipitated as a core-shell, as here,
or to include a drug with hydrophobic drugs being entrapped with greater success than
hydrophillic drugs. PEG diblock polymers can also be utilised, with the more hydrophilic
PEG-polymer facing out towards the outside of the NP [15]. The additional, possible
future modiﬁcations of NPs are further touched upon in Section 6.4. Synthetically, nano-
precipitation is a very eﬀortless way of producing NPs and is available to scale-up if need
be. Generally, ∼100 nm NPs are easily produced with nanoprecipitation using polymers
with MW <50 kDa, as is the case here [15].
To characterise the precipitated polymeric NPs, the approach of Dynamic Light Scattering
(DLS) and measurement of ζ, the zeta potential, provides information about the size and
surface charge of NPs in a solution.
DLS is based on the concept of the scattering of light from a laser beam by particles in a
solution, assessable by photon correlation spectroscopy [102]. Based on the intensity and
the angle of scattered light, the hydrodynamic radius of a spherical particle in solution is
measured. The radius is determined through intensity correlation functions, determining
the difussion coeﬀecient of the particles, which is then related to the radius of the particle
[102].
In addition to the hydrodynamic radius, DLS is applied to determine the size
distribution proﬁle of particles in a solution.
From DLS measurements the eﬀective,
hydrodynamic diameter and the polydispersity index (PDI) are of particular interest. Due
56
==PAGE:77==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Figure 4.1: Schematic overview of the nanoprecipitation process utilised here.
The ﬁgure constitutes the nanoprecipitation step of the overall Figure 1.2.
to the desire for using NPs with similar sizes in order to have identical properties, the PDI
therefore provides a measure on the size distribution between particles of varying sizes
in a solution. With lower PDI, particles are less polydisperse and more homogeneously
distributed towards a given NP size [102]. A PDI tending towards zero is an indication
of a homogeneous NP sample [15].
The ζ determines the electric potential at the so-called slipping plane, being the spherical
distance surrounding a particle at which ions of opposite charge to the particle do not
move and migrate around with the particle [103]. In practical terms, ζ is used here to
characterise the electric charge of NPs in solution, measured in mV. ζ can be used e.g.
to gain insight into how the particle surface composition has changed by comparing the
change in particle ζ following surface conjugation.
4.1.2
Results
Screening of polymers
Prior to the work performed during this thesis, a screening of useful polymers for NP
precipitation was conducted. Generally, it was seen that diﬀerent polymers presented
varying solubilities in ACN, DMF and THF. Based on these solubility experiments, use-
ful polymer-solvent combinations were nanoprecipitated according to the described pro-
cedure in Section 8.5. Nanoprecipitated NPs were characterised by DLS and ζ. Of the
prepared NPs, NH2−PLGA−NH2- and PLGA−COOH-based NPs were determined to be
most optimal due to measurements of low polydispersity, minimal polymer crash-out dur-
ing nanoprecipitation, low standard errors in particle size, with particle diameters around
70-80 nm and negative ζ. Thus, all factors indicated stable, suitable particles for use as
polymeric cores. Based on the observations, all experiments in the development of a bio-
catalytic NP-system during the course of this thesis involved use of PLGA−COOH- and
NH2−PLGA−NH2-based polymers in addition to PLGA−CH2−CH2−NH2. The struc-
57
==PAGE:78==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
ture of PLGA-based polymers is depicted in Figure 2.12.
NP core development
Results and data of a selection of NP nanoprecipitations are seen in Table 4.1. The data
originates from DLS and ζ measurements following nanoprecipitation of polymeric NPs
as described in Section 8.5, with measurements of sample particle count rate, diameter,
PDI and ζ listed. The results represent both examples of successful, semi-successful and
unsuccessful nanoprecipitations as well as batches yielded following optimisation of the
nanoprecipitation protocol and approach. Additionally, results of PLGA−COOH- and
NH2−PLGA−NH2-based NPs utilised for the further bioconjugation of endpoint Cat G-
conjugated NPs are shown. As will be explained in detail in Section 4.2, the strategy for
using diﬀerent polymers with diﬀerent endgroups in NP preparation is due to multiple
approaches available for conjugation of Cat G to the NP surfaces.
In addition to the shown results, the outcome of each precipitated batch describes the
degree of nanoprecipitation success for each respective batch. A successful batch, grade
A, is characterised by a high count rate, low PDI and low Standard Error (SE) on mea-
surements of NP diameter and ζ whereas an unsuccessful batch, grade C, is characterised
by low count rate, a highly polydisperse solution and inaccurate diameter and ζ measure-
ments. Grade B is for intermediate batches.
For the dissolution of polymers, being PLGA−COOH, PLGA−CH2−CH2−NH2 and
NH2−PLGA−NH2, either DMF or ACN was used. The concentration of polymer so-
lution utilised for the nanoprecipitation, denoted [Polymer], was adjusted throughout
experiments. The volume of H2O in which the polymer solution was dripped into was
also changed as was the concentration and pH of the HEPES buﬀer, the NPs were recon-
stituted in. The reasoning behind these changes in the experimental approach together
with the implications of each optimisation will be discussed in the following section.
Numerous other batches than the listed in Table 4.1 have been prepared. However, in order
to showcase the types of results that can be yielded from DLS and ζ measurements, in
addition to how the results are changed following optimisation and changed experimental
approach, the data from the indicated precipitated NP batches is included to give a
representative image of the results yielded throughout the NP core optimisation step.
From Table 4.1, it is seen that studies 1.A-1.C represent nanoprecipitated batches ranging
from classiﬁcation of A to C. Batches in study 1.A presents high count rates of ∼300-500
kcps indicating a large NP volume in each sample. Average NP diameters are between
84.5-101.5 nm whereas PDI is low, between 0.098-0.153, both measures with minimal
standard error. Higher absolute values of ζ indicates a more stable particle suspension
compared to low absolute values [103], which is observed for batches in study 1.A. Between
the batches, an increase in count rate is observed with increasing [polymer]. A similar
trend is seen for the batches in study 1.B with increase in count rate from 47.8 to 308.5
kcps upon [polymer] doubling from 5 to 10 mg/ml, although average values and SE of
diameters, PDI and ζ are similar between batches, entailing poorer batch quality for the
ﬁrst batch. In study 1.C, two batches are observed both with low count rates.
Prepared batches in studies 1.A-1.C thus demonstrate the variety of yielded batch results

==PAGE:79==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
and the quality of these NP batches. The results also exemplify signiﬁcant ’batch-to-batch’
variation that is seen following NP preparation having a high inﬂuence on the precipitated
NPs. Despite no changes in protocol, NP properties was altered signiﬁcantly, indicating
a great variation in NP characteristics between batches.
Batches 2.A-2.C are included as part of an increased [polymer] optimisation for use for
nanoprecipitations. Study 2.A was performed utilising a 2 mg/ml polymer solution for
nanoprecipitation, yielding poor results with a low count rate of 7.4 kcps, higher PDI of
0.429 ± 0.093 and ζ of -2.31 ± 2.70 mV. By increase of [polymer] to 5 mg/ml while de-
creasing the volume of H2O used for nanoprecipitation from 10 to 5 ml, sample count rate
increased slightly to 12.9 kcps, whereas PDI dropped to 0.322 ± 0.022 and ζ indicated
more stable particles with increased ζ to -15.58 ± 2.30 mV, indicating improved NP prop-
erties. Following additional increase in [polymer] to 10 mg/ml, a further enhancement in
NP batch quality was observed with increased count rate to 124.3 kcps, signiﬁcantly low-
ered PDI of 0.121 ± 0.031 and ζ of -30.67 ± 2.82 mV. The SE on diameter measurement
of NPs was reduced slightly with NP diameters of 66.7 ± 0.9.
Studies 3.A, 3.B and 4 utilised ACN for dissolution of NH2−PLGA−NH2 polymer for
nanoprecipitation. Although the NP batch quality varies between studies 3.A, 3.B and
4, suﬃciently high count rates, low PDIs and SE on PDI and diameter measurements, in
addition to particles being adequately charged, are seen for both studies but in particular
for study 3.A. Although average diameter is increased slightly, successful NPs are equally
nanoprecipitated using ACN as polymer solvent.
In study 5, PLGA-COOH-based NPs were nanoprecipitated with great success in the
form of a high count rate of 463.3 kcps, suitable NP diameter, PDI and ζ. Whereas
nanoprecipitation of PLGA-COOH NPs was successful, further preparation and use of
PLGA−CH2−CH2−NH2-based NPs was discontinued due to yielded batches in study 6
with low count rates of 5.3 kcps, irregular NP diameters of 34.0 ± 14.5 nm, high polydis-
persity of 1.945 ± 0.804 and inadequate ζ of 1.26 ± 1.58 mV.
The observed impacts on NP characteristics of polymer dissolution solvents, adjustments
in [polymer], volume of H2O for nanoprecipitation as well as the further application of the
diﬀerent polymers in use for NP nanoprecipitations are discussed in the following section.

4.1.3
Discussion
The data from Table 4.1 demonstrates the multitude of results that can be yielded from
measurements of NP sizes and charges following nanoprecipitation. In particular with
studies 1.A-1.C the range of NP characteristics is evident as the NP batches have been
prepared using the same approach but NP properties vary greatly between batches. Even
though these shown studies are only a selection of prepared batches conducted throughout
the work of this thesis, it exempliﬁes the possible results that can be obtained from NP
nanoprecipitations and what is meant with high and poor quality NP batch properties.
However, the results also indicate that NP characteristics are susceptible to signiﬁcant
batch-to-batch variation and that the reproducibility of nanoprecipitations yielding similar
NP properties is somewhat inadequate. In particular nanoprecipitations using [polymer]
of 5 mg/ml in studies 1.A-1.C, where especially sample count rate and ζ varies signiﬁ-
cantly between batches, demonstrate the point of batch-to-batch variation.
To limit batch-to-batch variation and ensure nanoprecipitation of suitable NPs on a con-
sistent basis, measures for protocol optimisations and improvements in the experimental
approach were taken. As will be elaborated on in Section 4.2, in eﬀort to keep amounts of
used Cat G for enzyme bioconjugations low, as Cat G stock was limited, the concentration
of prepared NPs was attempted to be lowered by decreasing [polymer] applied. Despite
initial nanoprecipitations utilising [polymer] of 5-10 mg/ml, a [polymer] of 2 mg/ml was
applied at ﬁrst. Indicated by the results of study 2.A, it is clear that use of 2 mg/ml [poly-
mer] did not yield high quality NPs. The low [polymer] may simply not be suﬃciently high
to allow for proper precipitation in solvent:aqueous solution mix, causing results of low
NP count rates of a polydisperse solution. Based on these results, [polymer] was increased
to 5 mg/ml in attempt to improve precipitated NP properties. Additionally, to increase
the organic solvent:H2O ratio in eﬀort to further facilitate successful nanoprecipitation,
the volume of stirring H2O used during the nanoprecipitation step was decreased to 5
ml. The collective contribution of increased [polymer] and decreased VH2O led to a slight
improvement in NP sample quality, although not suﬃciently for the batch precipitation
to be successful. Thus, [polymer] was further increased in attempt to yield results and
NP batch quality as was seen in studies 1.A and 1.B. Following increased [polymer] to 10
mg/ml while maintaining VH2O at 5 ml, a signiﬁcant improvement in NP batch quality
was observed. Although NPs were reconstituted in 50 mM HEPES pH 8.5 rather than 100
mM HEPES pH 7.4, this is not believed to have noticeable impact on the observed results.
The results indicate that increased [polymer] as well as solvent:H2O-ratio have positive
impacts on the success of NP preparation and characteristics. This is in concordance with
the known physicochemical aspects of nanopreparation described earlier [97].
Although NP diameters does not seem to be inﬂuenced by an increasing [polymer], in-
creased sample count rates along with decrease in PDI and ζ were observed for NP batches
upon increase in [polymer]. This signiﬁes that the ultimate [polymer] applicable for NP
nanoprecipitation was 10 mg/ml, whereas VH2O were to be kept at 5 ml for successful and
high quality NP batch preparations. This approach was therefore utilised in the nanopre-
cipitations of NPs to be used for Cat G-bioconjugations going forward.
Whereas increased [polymer] did not seem to impact NP diameter in a predictable man-
ner, the use of ACN as polymer dissolution solvent rather than DMF does. In studies
61
==PAGE:82==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
3-6, ACN was utilised as solvent over DMF, due to considerations regarding the possible
reactivity of the −NH2 endgroup on the PLGA−NH2 polymer with DMF, elaborated on
in Section 4.2.3. Concurrently to the mentioned work on polymer core nanoprecipitations,
crosslinker reaction was attempted using NPs from study 2.A. With this initial test of
crosslinking, utilising approaches similar to those described in Section 4.2, limited suc-
cess was seen. The lacking results was ascribed to the DMF solvent, as it was suggested
that DMF might react with and thus interfere with the −NH2 polymer endgroups, thus
inducing a negative eﬀect on the crosslinking.
Utilising ACN as solvent, the resulting measurements in studies 3-5 indicate successful
nanoprecipitations and preparation of NPs with promising properties. However, overall
slight increase in average NP diameter is observed for all NP batches following use of
ACN compared to DMF. The increase in NP diameters from ∼70-80 nm to >100 nm
is not of crucial importance and NPs were still highly applicable for further use for Cat
G bioconjugations as count rates generally remained high with low PDIs and distinctive
negative particle charges.
With no observed diﬀerence in polymer solubility in ACN compared to DMF, no essential
impact between the use of ACN as polymer solvent compared to DMF were seen as NP
properties following ACN use were of equal high quality. To evade the concern of DMF
potentially aﬀecting −NH2 endgroup reactivity, ACN was utilised as polymer solvent go-
ing forward.
Comparing the results from studies 1-4 with studies 5, there is no signiﬁcant change in NP
properties following preparation of PLGA−COOH-based NPs compared to PLGA−NH2-
based NPs. Prepared PLGA−NH2- and PLGA−COOH-based NPs in studies 3-5 were of
medium to high quality with suitable properties. Hence, these NPs were approved for fur-
ther use as NPs for coupling of suitable crosslinkers for Cat G NP-surface conjugation in
the work described in Section 4.2. As opposed to PLGA−NH2- and PLGA−COOH-based
NPs, NPs nanoprecipitated using PLGA−CH2−CH2−NH2 polymers demonstrated NPs
unﬁt for further use as NPs for enzyme bioconjugation, as the prepared NP samples pre-
sented poor quality NP characteristics. Any further use of the PLGA−CH2−CH2−NH2
was therefore discontinued based on these unsatisfactory results.
In conclusion, the results accentuated here demonstrate the applicable use of both PLGA-
NH2- and PLGA−COOH-based polymers as substituents in the preparation of useful NPs
with characteristics suitable for use as polymeric cores in the NP system to be developed.
The favourable NPs exhibit properties of low PDI, particle diameters in the range of
∼100-135 nm with ζ around ∼-25-55 mV together with high particle count rates. This
diameter range is particularly suitable as it allows for passive targeting of tumours in
vivo by exploitation of the EPR eﬀect. These applicable NPs for Cat G bioconjugation
are best prepared by applying ACN for dissolution of polymers using a [polymer] of 10
mg/ml dripped in 5 ml H2O for the nanoprecipitation step.
Both PLGA−NH2- and
PLGA−COOH-based NPs are utilised in future surface bioconjugations of Cat G. The
diﬀerent polymer endgroups allow for application of diverse coupling strategies for Cat G
conjugation, covered in detail in Section 4.2, as is the further development of a functional
nanobiocatalytic NP system.
62
==PAGE:83==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
4.2
Enzyme-polymer core bioconjugation
As with targeting ligands such as mAbs and other biologically active components, it is
possible to couple catalytic enzymes, including Cat G, onto the outer surface of NPs. The
loading of NPs with surface-enzymes, known as bioconjugation, allows for the combination
of catalytic, repetitive, increased enzymatic activity rather than using a single-event drug
or mAb, while exploiting the properties of NPs such as half-life extension, EPR-eﬀect
etc., as described in detail previously in Sections 2.7 and 2.8.
To develop a fully functioning biocatalytic NP system, the successful coupling of Cat G
to the surface of polymeric core NPs is vital. There are numerous approaches to this step
of Cat G bioconjugation, multiple of which have been investigated and optimised here.
The following section describes the approaches utilised here, this optimisation and the re-
sulting enzyme-conjugation yields from the coupling of Cat G to NP surfaces. Ultimately,
the applicability and further use of Cat G-conjugated NPs are discussed and assessed.
The strategy for bioconjugation has been to apply various approaches for coupling NPs
and Cat G. Additionally, multiple techniques are utilised to determine the rate of conju-
gation success applicability of the nanobiocatalytic system developed. Both during and
after surface-conjugation of Cat G, quantiﬁcation steps of reaction success are included
to investigate the extent of successful coupling. The quantiﬁcations provide the basis
for decision of further use and testing of the developed Cat G-integrated NP-system.
Besides these quantiﬁcations, DLS and ζ measurements are used for characterisation of
intermediately-crosslinked- and conjugated NPs similar to the previously described in
Section 4.1.
4.2.1
Methodology
The principles behind enzyme conjugation and the experimental approaches of NHS-,
maleimide- and EDC-NHS-based crosslinkings utilised here for surface enzyme conjuga-
tion are described below in detail. Additionally, the techniques utilised for assessment of
successful conjugation are covered.
Enzyme loading on nanoparticle surface
The coupling of Cat G and NPs is conducted by bioconjugation, covalently coupling a
polymeric NP, crosslinker and enzyme, known as crosslinking [104].
The crosslinking
reagent can be bound to either the NP core or Cat G and by addition of the second
reagent, Cat G enzymes are linked to the NPs. The process is schematically overviewed
in Figure 4.2. Bioconjugation is an extensive ﬁeld and numerous diﬀerent approaches for
conducting this exist [105]. All approaches come with advantages and ﬂaws making these
suitable for speciﬁc situations, but the techniques relevant for NP-Cat G bioconjugation
are described here.
The choice of bioconjugation strategy depends, among other things, on the FGs at the end
of both the polymer and enzyme to-be linked. As mentioned, following NP precipitation
the polymer FG will ideally face outwards into the surrounding, hydrophilic environment
presenting itself for potential coupling reactions.
Typical classes of polymer-/enzyme
63
==PAGE:84==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Figure 4.2: Schematic overview of the bioconjugation process applied here
in the eﬀort to coupled core NPs (beige) and Cat G (red). As example, the
−NH2- and thiol-reactive SPDP is used as crosslinking reagent here, see Figure
4.6(b). Step 1 is denoted as the linking of NPs with crosslinker and step 2 is
the conjugation of Cat G onto NP surfaces. The ﬁgure constitutes the enzyme
functionalisation step of the overall Figure 1.2.
FGs utilised for bioconjugation are primary amines (R−NH2), carboxyls (R−COOH)
and thiols (R−SH) [104].
Based on the NP or enzyme FG, a series of diﬀerent crosslinking reagents, crosslinkers,
can be utilised. Most crosslinkers are designed to include reactive endgroups towards a
speciﬁc FG. For instance, a crosslinker can be designed to include an amine-reactive end-
group in one end and possess speciﬁcity towards a thiol-, carboxyl- or another amine FG
in the other. Additionally, incorporation of spacer arms to separate functional endgroups
gives added crosslinker ﬂexibility, molecular weights, alternative water solubility, reaction
properties etc. [106].
Towards each type of FG, multiple potential reactive endgroups exist.
As examples,
typically N- Hydroxysuccinimide (NHS) esters and imidoesters are used to react with
primary amines. As thiol-reactive groups maleimide and pyridyl disulﬁde can be used.
Additionally, carbodiimides are typically used to form crosslinks between carboxyl and
amine FGs [106].
Primary amines are nucleophilic and for bioconjugation purposes NHS esters is often used
to react with primary amines. An NHS ester bioconjugation results in a stable amide bond
under slightly alkaline conditions, pH 8-8.5, following substitution of the NHS ester, see
Figure 4.3. The reaction rate is increased with buﬀer pH and temperature, although pH
>8-8.5 is required for deprotonating of the primary amines [67]. However, natural hydrol-
ysis of the NHS ester is equally escalated with increased pH [107]. The reaction is quick
and can be performed in as little as 15 minutes at RT [108].
64
==PAGE:85==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Figure 4.3: NHS ester reacion mechanism scheme with primary amine to
produce a amide-bonded conjugate between NP and linker. Adapted from [107].
Thiols are an equally utilised functional endgroup of crosslinkers for bioconjugations.
Crosslinking with thiols is more selective than primary amines, as thiols are typically
not present on enzymes to as high extent as primary amines. On enzymes, thiols can
be prepared from cysteine residues by reducing the disulﬁde bonds using reagents such
as 1,4-Dithiothreitol (DTT) or Tris(2-carboxyethyl)phosphine (T-CEP), revealing a free
thiol, although enzyme integrity and activity can be aﬀected by the disrupted disulﬁde
bridges [67].
Maleimides are utilised as thiol reactive endgroups. In pH of 6.5-7.5, the reaction creates
a stable, irreversible thioether bond, seen in Figure 4.4. The rate of success decreases
with pH > 8.5, as the reaction then favours primary amines, rather than thiols, and the
reactive maleimide is reduced to non-reactive maleamic acid by hydrolysis. Naturally,
buﬀers used for the reaction should not include thiol-containing compounds as they will
compete for the maleimide reagent [109].
Figure 4.4: Reaction mechanism scheme of maleimide-thiol coupling to pro-
duce a thioether bond between linker and enzyme. Adapted from [109].
In addition to maleimides, crosslinkers cotaining pyridyl disulﬁdes are similarly used to re-
act with thiols. Examples of use of pyridyl disulﬁde is with a 2-pyridyldithio-linker. With
65
==PAGE:86==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
the addition of a thiol-containing enzyme in thiol-free buﬀers around pH 7-8 the free thiol
will displace pyridine-2-thiol, yielding the release of a pyridine-2-thione and formation of
a new disulﬁde-bond between the crosslinker and enzyme. The reaction mechanism is
seen in Figure 4.5 [109].
Figure 4.5: Reaction mechanism scheme of pyridyldithio-thiol displacement
to produce a disfuldide bond between linker and enzyme.
Pyridine-2-thione
absorbs light at 343 nm. Adapted from [109, 110].
Heterobifunctional crosslinkers are linkers with reactive groups towards two diﬀerent
classes of FGs, of which NHS-ester-PEG4-Mal and Succinimidyl 3-(2-pyridyldithio) pro-
pionate (SPDP) are examples and used here, both shown in Figure 4.6. The crosslinkers
can be used in one-step reactions due the respective endgroup FG-speciﬁcity although
two step reaction are commonly utilised to ensure proper conjugation, by removing ex-
cess unreacted reagents between steps. Generally for all bioconjugations, it is favourable
to perform the reaction in near-physiological conditions as it will maintain the enzyme in
its native, folded state [106].
(a) NHS-ester-PEG4-Maleimide crosslinker
(b) SPDP crosslinker
Figure 4.6: Examples of heterobifunctional linkers [111, 112]. Both linkers
are utilised experimentally here and are both subject to reactions with amines
and thiols.
Although the number of speciﬁc carboxyl-reactive crosslinkers are not as extensive as for
primary amines and thiols, carbodiimide still provides a valuable reagent for crosslinking
carboxylic acid- with primary amines-FGs. The reaction forms an amide bond and the
66
==PAGE:87==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
most utilised crosslinker is 1-ethyl-3-(3-dimethylaminopropyl)carbodiimide hydrochloride
(EDC), shown in Figure 4.7 [113].
EDC can react with a carboxylic acid-FG to form an unstable O-acylisourea. If NHS is
added simultaneously to the reaction, an amine-reactive NHS complex will be formed.
Addition of enzyme will lead to reaction of the NHS-ester with primary amines on the
enzyme, e.g. from deprotonated sidechains of lysine (Lys) residues under slightly alkaline
conditions, pH 8-8.5, where the primary amine reacts with nucleophilic properties [114].
Generally, outward-facing Lys residues are accessible for conjugations without aﬀecting
the stability and activity of the enzyme [106]. Arginine (Arg) residues do likewise contain
primary amines, but do so as part of a guanidino group that can function as a strong
nucleophile when unprotonated [115, 116]. But with pKA > 12 of the guadinino group,
Arg is protonated at the conditions utilised here with pH 8-8.5, thus not presenting
nucleophilic characteristics [115].
The reaction scheme of EDC-NHS-based linking is seen in Figure 4.7. The ﬁrst step of the
reaction is best performed in buﬀers with pH around 4.5, without carboxyls or primary
amine-containing compounds, whereas the NHS-linking is conduct in pH 8-8.5 [107, 117].
Figure 4.7: The EDC crosslinking reaction mechanism scheme used to pro-
duce an amide bond from carboxylic acid- and primary amine FGs. Adapted
from [117, 118].
SPDP linking quantiﬁcation
The SPDP crosslinker, seen in Figure 4.6(b), contains a pyridyldithio-group and upon
cleavage of the disulﬁde bond in SPDP, either by the reaction outlined in Figure 4.5 or by
addition of disulﬁde-bond reducing agents such as DTT or T-CEP, pyridine-2-thione is
released. As pyridine-2-thione aborbs light at and around 343 nm, it is possible to quantify
the release of pyridine-2-thione based on the increased absorbance at 343 nm and thus
the amount of SPDP that have been coupled to the NP by reaction of the amine-reactive
67
==PAGE:88==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
NHS-ester endgroup [67, 119]. This provides a valuable, intermediate quantiﬁcation step
and assessment of success of crosslinker-reaction onto NPs.
SPDP-coupling quantiﬁcation can both be conducted as a sample-invasive procedure fol-
lowing SPDP-linking onto the NP surface, following the addition of DTT or T-CEP or
during the ﬁnal conjugation of enzyme af Cat G coupling. In any case, pyridine-2-thione
is released. The labelling ratio of SPDP-coupled NP versus non-coupled NPs, given in mol
SPDP linked to the NP-surface per mol polymer in solution, is used to indicate the extent
of SPDP-coupling based on the increased absorbance at 343 nm. The ratio is determined
by Equation (4.1)
Ratio =
∆Abs343
ϵ343
[Polymer]
(4.1)
where ∆Abs343 is the diﬀerence in absorbance at 343 nm from NP-SPDP particles reacted
either with reducing agent or thiol-containing Cat G versus untreated NP-SPDP particles.
ϵ343 is the extinction coeﬃcient of pyridine-2-thionen of 8080 M−1· cm−1 [67]. [Polymer] is
the concentration of the NPs in solution on the assumption of that all potential polymers
have been precipitated into polymeric NPs.
Micro BCA
Assessment on surface-conjugated Cat G is performed by Micro BCA assay. The assess-
ment is based on the ability of Cu2+ to be reduced to Cu+ by the presence of protein in a
sample in a basic environment. Colour development due to Cu+ allows sample light ab-
sorption at and around 562 nm and increase in absorbance correlates linearly with protein
concentration increase [120]. Both samples in question as well as reference samples, e.g.
Cat G-conjugated NPs and linked NPs, respectively, are measured, as polymeric NPs are
seen to exhibit a false positive signal indicating presence of protein. Hence, alleged [Cat
G] contribution of linked, non-conjugated NPs are subtracted from the measured [Cat G]
of NP-Cat G conjugates.
4.2.2
Results
As already introduced in Section 4.1, batches of NPs without any surface modiﬁcation,
termed naked NPs, were nanoprecipitated for the application as polymeric NP cores in the
development of the desired biocatalytic NP-system. Due to NP properties, the naked NP
cores prepared from studies 3.A, 3.B, 4 and 5 were used in the ensuing nanobiocatalytic
NP development.
A series of studies on the potential bioconjugation of Cat G to PLGA-based NP-surfaces
were carried out utilising these prepared NP cores. For convenience, the bioconjugation
studies are referred to as the the individual study number already denominated during the
NP core development stage. The results of these NP-Cat G development studies 3.A, 3.B,
4 and 5 are overviewed in Table 4.2, where measurements of DLS and ζ are listed together
with linking quantiﬁcations following SPDP-linking as well as results of sample enzyme
quantiﬁcation by use of Micro BCA. The studies have utilised a variety of linking ap-
proaches from use of SPDP- and NHS-PEG4-Mal-linkers to EDC-NHS-coupling. Besides
linker substitution, the approach to Cat G conjugation has been optimised throughout the
68
==PAGE:89==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
experiments by modifying the volumes and molar amounts of reagents as well as the dif-
ferent reaction incubation times. The reasoning behind these optimisation are discussed
in the following Section 4.2.3.
Applying the bioconjugation procedure described in Section 8.6, NH2−PLGA−NH2 NPs
were linked using SPDP in bioconjugation study 3.A. SPDP was added with a 5-molar
excess to free −NH2 NP FGs, but despite the excess amount of SPDP, SPDP-linking quan-
tiﬁcation indicated limited SPDP-coupling with a linking ratio of 2.48 · 10−3 ± 2.04 · 10−4
mol SPDP per mol polymer.
Additionally, poor quality NPs for bioconjugation were
observed with NP sample count rate of 5.5 kcps and poor PDI of 0.597, indicating a
polydisperse sample solution. Signiﬁcant drops in diameter and negative surface charge
between naked- and SPDP-modiﬁed NPs were also seen, with diameter decreasing from
135.9 ± 1.1 nm to 105.1 ± 5.4 nm and ζ dropping from -58.03 ± 2.57 mV to -3.22 ± 1.91
mV. These results indicated limited SPDP-linking. Thus the experiment was optimised to
include an increase molar excess of SPDP-linker, raised from 5 to 37.87, while extending
the reaction time at RT from 1 to 2 h in study 3.B. As seen in Table 4.2, a ∼1.5 times
increase in the SPDP-labelling ratio to 1.63·10−3±4.19·10−4 mol SPDP per mol polymer,
compared to the ratio in study 3.A. Additionally, sustained NP-SPDP sample qualities
compared to naked NPs were observed. A fairly high count rate of 72.3 compared to
40.9 of naked NPs, low PDI of NP-SPDP complexes with 0.169 ± 0.019 in comparison to
0.260 ± 0.009 for non-linked NPs together with steady ζ around ∼-25 mV for both types
of NPs followed from the optimisations, which were seen to improve the properties of
the resulting SPDP-linked NPs. Despite the lack of increase in labelling-ratio, increased
molar excess of linker and extended reaction time was adapted based on the observed re-
sults. Linked NPs prepared in studies in 3.A and 3.B were utilised for Cat G-conjugations.
In studies 3.A and 3.B, respectively, 5 and 15 µl of NP-SPDP solution were used for
NP-conjugation of 0.25 and 0.1 molar excess of Cat G. Considerations behind volumetric
and molar quantities are discussed in the ensuing Section 4.2.3. Following conjugation,
extremely low NP count rates of 1.9 kcps were observed. Further, DLS and ζ measure-
ments indicated no presence of particles in solution and ζ measures of merely 0.22 ± 1.45
mV and -1.15 ± 1.30 mV were observed for Cat G-conjugated NPs in study 3.A and 3.B,
respectively. Additionally for both studies, SPDP-labelling ratio did not provide useful
quantiﬁcation of Cat G-coupling, as the absorbance at 343 nm of SPDP-linked NPs, that
had not been incubated with activated Cat G was higher than the absorbance of Cat
G-coupled NP. Hence, this indicated no further release of pyridine-2-thione following re-
action with activated Cat G.
Micro BCA assay was utilised for assessment of sample [Cat G]. However, in study 3.A,
[Cat G] was determined to higher quantities for NP-SPDP samples compared to NP-Cat
G conjugates. In study 3.B, the absorbance at 560 nm of Cat G-containing samples did
not surpass the absorbance of blank reference samples. No indication of NP-surface cou-
pled Cat G was therefore observed.
Upon application of NHS-PEG4-Mal linker as crosslinking reagent in study 4, utilising
a high, 50-fold molar excess of linker compared to free amines on polymeric core and
extended reaction time, semi-useful linked NPs were seen.
Compared to naked NPs,
69
==PAGE:90==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
NHS-PEG4-Mal linked NPs presented a low count rate of 16.8 kcps and PDI of 0.442 ±
0.013. Increased diameter to 212.0 ± 2.0 nm and ζ to -12.22 ± 2.61 were observed in
comparison with naked NPs. Following conjugation of Cat G using a similar approach
as in study 3.B in relation to VNPs and Cat G quantity, NP-Cat G batch composition
was not comprised of high quality Cat G-conjugated NPs. low count rate of 1.4 and ζ of
-8.00 ± 1.69 mV together with non-existent diameter and PDI measurements indicated
minimal sample concentration of conjugated NPs. Furthermore, as was seen for study
3.B, absorbances at 560nm for blank reference samples were seen to exceed NP-Cat G
sample absorbance, showing no indication of NP-conjugated Cat G in the samples.
Naked PLGA−COOH-based NPs with great NP characteristics were utilised in study
5, where Cat G-conjugation following EDC-NHS-based linking was carried out. As is
seen in Table 4.2, quite signiﬁcant molar excesses of NHS and EDC reagents were used
for the linking. An equivalent quantity of Cat G was used for the experiments, but as
the PLGA−COOH polymer only contains one endgroup per polymer chain the molar
excess of Cat G is therefore seen to increase to 0.2. Incubation times were unchanged
from the applied in studies 3.B and 4. Opposite studies 3.A, 3.B and 4, results from
individual prepared NP batches are shown for study 5 in Table 4.2. Initially, poor NP
sample properties were observed after linking using EDC and NHS as aggregates of NP
complexes were seen to constitute batch a) with ∼14,000 nm sized particles, self-evident
unﬁt for use. On the other hand, batch b) was made up of small-sized NPs of 6.2 ± 5.0
nm. High polydispersities of 0.566 ± 0.354 and 0.30 ± 0.151 and ζ of -16.80 ± 3.07 mV
and -12.85 ± 2.20 mV, for batches a) and b) respectively, were observed concurrently.
Following Cat G-conjugation, quality of NP characteristics for both batches were not
seen to improve. Count rates were largely unaﬀected with values of 3.2 and 3.0 kcps for
batch a) and b), while measurements of NP diameters, PDIs and ζ failed to indicate any
presence of suitable Cat G-conjugated NPs. However, results obtained from Micro BCA
showed [Cat G] of 0.49 µg/ml for batch a), n = 1, and 1.89 ± 0.30 µg/ml for batch b),
n = 2.
The impact of the optimisations to the bioconjugation approach, the observed results and
potential further use of any of the developed Cat G-conjugated NPs are discussed in detail
in the following Section 4.2.3.
