4.2.3
Discussion
The approach to bioconjugation of Cat G has been optimised during the course of the
underlying experimental work conducted here in an attempt to increase NP-linker and
-Cat G conjugates sample qualities, labelling ratios of SPDP linking and ultimately con-
centration of NP-surface conjugated Cat G.
Crosslinking
Throughout the studies, signiﬁcant molar excess of crosslinker reagent have been utilised
to ensure suﬃcient reaction with naked NPs as well as allowing all potential −NH2 end-
groups to be crosslinked. The required molar quantities of the respective crosslinkers
were determined based on the polymer solution concentration applied for polymeric core
nanoprecipitation under the assumption that all potential polymer had been precipitated
into core NPs, of which 400-500 µl solution was applied for crosslinking. In study 3.A,
the quantity of used SPDP linker account for a 5-fold molar excess of SPDP, compared
to free −NH2 endgroups on the polymeric NP surfaces. However, as is shown in Table
4.2, following SPDP-crosslinking an unsatisfactory SPDP-labelling ratio was observed.
A signiﬁcantly more neutral NP ζ was observed for crosslinked NPs, yet this apparent
surface alteration might be due to the overall low quality of the NP sample with high PDI
and low count rates. As poor NP sample quality was seen, limited SPDP-crosslinking was
therefore deduced rather than a change in surface composition being the case. To increase
in particular the labelling ratio, obtaining a higher population of SPDP-crosslinkers on
NPs, the quantities of SPDP were increased signiﬁcantly to comprise a 37.87-fold molar
excess together with reaction time being increased from 1 to 2 h. As previously men-
tioned, the NHS-amine reaction occurs quickly, but reaction time was still increased to
further facilitate binding. This improvement in reaction conditions should have yielded a
signiﬁcant increase in SPDP-labelling ratio, indicating a higher crosslinking success, how-
ever this was not observed with merely a ∼1.5-fold increase in labelling ratio, comparing
studies 3.A and 3.B.
Although the labelling ratio was not seen to increase, the general decent quality of SPDP-
crosslinked NPs in study 3.B was upheld with maintained low PDI and ζ levels of -23.90 ±
1.50 mV for crosslinked NPs, compared to naked NPs. However, even though the increased
molar excess of linker aﬀects the quantity of crosslinked SPDP, the NP characteristics
should not directly be aﬀected by increased linker amount, although the seen results
suggest so. But this observation is likely more due to the recurrent aspect of batch-to-
batch variation, originally introduced in regards to the naked polymeric NP core in Section
4.1. Across all studies, no evident relation between increased crosslinker quantities and
NP properties were observed, see Table 4.2, hence the improved characteristics of SPDP-
crosslinked NPs in study 3.B are not believed to be inﬂuenced by the amount of added
crosslinker.
It is clear that only limited crosslinking success was yielded following preparation of both
batches. Nevertheless, despite only slight increase in labelling ratio and limited surface
alteration, in particular evident from the preserved ζ between naked and crosslinked NPs,
the combined signiﬁcant molar excess of crosslinker and extended reaction time were main-
tained in the procedure to ensure further facilitation of the subsequent bioconjugations.
72
==PAGE:93==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Increased molar excess and extended reaction time was likewise exploited in study 4. As
NHS-PEG4-Mal-crosslinking is not quantiﬁable by itself here, the crosslinking success was
only investigated by NP properties assessment. Although sample count rate was seen to
drop to 10 % of what was observed for naked NPs, the more positive ζ could indicate that
the surface composition might have been altered following crosslinking of NHS-PEG4-
Mal. Sample PDI was however increased following crosslinking, a trend observed for all
conducted NP studies, as well as NP diameter roughly doubled compared to naked NPs.
The results are somewhat inconsistent at best, indicating a mediocre crosslinking success
using NHS-PEG4-Mal.
Even less crosslinking success was observed in study 5 utilising the approach of EDC-
NHS-coupling of Cat G. Aggregates and minimally sized crosslinked NPs permeate the
prepared batches, generally indicating poor sample quality with NP unﬁt for further use.
Molar excess were exaggerated, based on previously reported literature [121], to ensure
suﬃcient reagent overﬂow and provide basis for successful coupling of linker. Additionally,
crosslinking was allowed for 2 h. Despite the results of DLS measures and ζ indicating
signiﬁcantly poor NP sample qualities, the prepared batches were still utilised for Cat
G-conjugation, as were the crosslinked NP batches from studies 3.A, 3.B and 4.
Cat G conjugation
The ultimate goal of the bioconjugation experiments was to conjugate Cat G on NP-
surfaces. Ideally, all crosslinked NP endgroups would be conjugated with Cat G for the
most potential NP system, however volume of Cat G in stock was limited. Due to the high
volume of Cat G required for 1:1 linker:Cat G-conjugation and the cost of Cat G, a 1:4-
and even 1:10-linker:Cat G-molar ratio approach was taken. Successful conjugation with
this approach would then lead to a NP surface composition of 1:10 between Cat G and
respective crosslinker of all available conjugation sites. Allegedly, this surface composition
would increase the stability of the NP-conjugate by presenting a more hydrophilic surface
compared to unconjugated NPs with free endgroups.
Initially for NP-surface conjugation of Cat G in study 3.A, 5 µl crosslinked NPs was
used, see Table 4.2. The low volume of NPs was applied in relation to the aspect of
only limited volume of Cat G being available for experiments.
As is obvious across
the bioconjugation studies conducted, DLS does not provide a suitable measure for NP
assessment when NP concentration are at these minimal levels. With low count rates, non-
existing measurements of diameter and PDI and although ζ might provide useful results,
DLS is generally unﬁt for characterisation purposes at these low NP concentrations as
results indicate no presence of NPs. Besides non-applicable results from DLS, Cat G-
conjugation quantiﬁcations using SPDP-labelling ratio assessment as well as Micro BCA
assay provide insight into conjugation success. SPDP-labelling ratio and Micro BCA did
however not indicate any conjugation of Cat G to the NP surface. The absorbance at 343
nm for non-reacted SPDP-linked NP was higher than the absorbance for NPs coupled with
Cat G, indicating no further release of pyridine-2-thione caused by successful conjugation
of Cat G, compared to untreated SPDP-linked NPs.
Similarly, the apparent protein
concentration in non-conjugated samples were also determined to higher levels than for
the Cat G-coupled NPs. This shows that polymeric NPs give a false positive protein
indication, possibly by similarly catalysing the reduction of Cu2+ to Cu+ as is done by
73
==PAGE:94==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
proteins, introduced in Section 4.2.1 Micro BCA.
All in all, observed results did not indicate presence of surface-conjugated Cat G in study
3.A. However, it is worth considering if the used quantities were simply below what
was required for the sensitivity of the applied quantiﬁcation techniques. To investigate
this aspect activity of both prepared, Cat G-conjugated NP batches were measured by
SDS-PAGE to validate the catalytic activity towards IL-6. The study is described and
discussed in details in Sections 5.1.1 and 6.1, but as it is clear from the study the catalytic
activity of the prepared NPs is very low with minuscule degradation of intact IL-6 follow-
ing incubations. Hence, the conducted bioassay conﬁrms the observed, non-existent Cat
G-surface conjugation on polymeric NPs in study 3.A and concludes that no functional
Cat G-coupled NP had been prepared during the study.
To improve NP-surface conjugation of Cat G, the approach was optimised by increase of
utilised crosslinked NP volumes from 5 to 15 µl. Furthermore, to match the available
Cat G volume, molar amounts of Cat G were lowered from 0.25 to 0.1 of available linker-
coupled poylmer endgroups. By increasing the volume of crosslinked NH2−PLGA−NH2
NPs in studies 3.B and 4, it was presumed that [NP] could be enhanced to allow im-
proved measurements using both DLS, ζ, SPDP-labelling ratio in study 3.B as well as
Micro BCA, as the previously prepared batch of Cat G-conjugated NPs evidently was
too dilute. However, as is indicated for both studies 3.B and 4 in Table 4.2, no improve-
ment in results were observed. DLS and ζ measurements were similar to results for study
3.A while SPDP-labelling ratio in study 3.B and Micro BCA in both studies 3.B and 4
equally indicated no Cat G-conjugation onto NP-surfaces. Here, the blank backgrounds
in the Micro BCA assays absorbed to higher extent at 560 nm than what was seen for the
Cat G-incubated crosslinked NPs, showing no sample Cat G presence. Hence, in spite
of increased NP volume, Cat G-conjugation was not successfully achieved and despite of
applying two diﬀerent crosslinking approaches in SPDP- and NHS-PEG4-Mal-linking, nei-
ther studies 3.B nor 4 yielded feasible NP-Cat G conjugates. Measurements of DLS and
ζ were largely unaﬀected by the raised NP volume, with NP samples likely still being too
dilute for proper measurements of size and charge. No further assessments of bioactivity
were conducted for the prepared batches in studies 3.A and 4 due to results similarities
with study 3.A.
Whereas studies 3.A, 3.B and 4, utilising the approach of PLGA−NH2−NHS-linking
followed by SPDP−/Mal−SH-Cat G conjugation, evidently were without much suc-
cess in coupling Cat G to NP surfaces, slightly more useful results were seen by use
of PLGA−COOH-based NPs coupled with the approach of EDC/NHS-crosslinking in
study 5. As described above, poor quality NPs were obtained following crosslinking us-
ing EDC and NHS for both conducted batches and the apparent sample qualities were
not seen to improve following Cat G-conjugation. However, following NP-linked [Cat G]
assessment by Micro BCA it is seen that some Cat G might have been conjugated to
the surfaces of NPs, with batch b) in study 5 being the most prominent prepared batch.
With 220.3 µl 100 µg/ml Cat G added to the reaction mix of 1 ml per batch and with
an average, ﬁnal NP-[Cat G] of 1.89 µg/ml, the conjugation yield amounts to a mere
8.6 % of the theoretical yield. This measure is clearly inadequate but is nevertheless the
highest that has been yielded throughout all the conducted studies. Furthermore, during
74
==PAGE:95==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
the conducted studies, NP-Cat G complexes were washed ﬁrst with a 1.5 M NaCl HEPES
buﬀer to ensure breaking of any non-covalent bonds between Cat G and crosslinked NPs
[67]. Therefore it is assumed that sample-Cat G is conjugated covalently to the surfaces
of NPs, as desired.
The results from study 5 indicate that the covalent bioconjugation of Cat G to surfaces
of polymeric NPs using a crosslinker-based approach is in fact possible. Although the
conjugation occurs to very little extent with low reaction yields of maximum 8.6 %,
the positive result provides indication that use of EDC-NHS-based coupling of Cat G is
feasible and likely useful to a much greater extent if the procedure is further optimised. In
any case, Cat G-conjugated NPs have been prepared by the applied setup and approach
here. To further validate the presence of Cat G on NPs and to test the biocatalytic activity
of the NP-conjugated Cat G enzymes, the prepared batch b) was used in SDS-PAGE and
enzyme kinetics studies similar to the previously conducted for study 3.A and in Sections
3.2 and 3.4. The results and impact of these experiments are covered in Chapter 5.
Overall considerations
Limited reactivity using NH2−PLGA−NH2-based NPs has generally been seen.
But
these observations might be explained by the structure of the provided polymer, seen in
Figure 4.8 [100]. As is evident from the ﬁgure, the endgroups are in reality comprised
of succinic hydrazide endgroups rather than −NH2. Naturally, reactivity of the diﬀerent
endgroups varies and hydrazides are generally used and seen to react with aldehyde- and
ketone-based carbonyls [106]. The presence of a succinic hydrazide endgroup rather than
−NH2 thus potentially explain the decreased reactivity of the naked NP towards the NHS-
part of the utilised linkers. This ultimately accounts for the insuﬃcient crosslinking and
unsatisfactory Cat G-conjugation onto NP surfaces. If the approach was to be optimised,
a natural step on the way would be to incorporate the use of a −CH2−CH2−NH2-like
polymer, as was attempted in Section 4.1 but without immediate success, or use aldehyde-
or ketone-carbonyl-based linkers for improved reaction with a crosslinker suitable for
further Cat G bioconjugation success.
Besides potential altered reactivity of polymer endgroup, potential shielding of the end-
group might also aﬀect the limited crosslinking-success. Ideally during nanoprecipitation
the NPs would conform to present the polar, hydrophilic endgroups on the outer spherical
surface with the NP core being made of solely of the hydrophobic glycolic-lactic-acid part
of the polymer chain. However, this perception might be idealised and endgroups might
be burried on the NP surface and thus not as easily accessible for crosslinker reaction as
desired. In this case of limited conformational availability of the NP surface endgroups,
endgroup reactivity is therefore also restricted. This may provide a further explanation
for the observed limited success in NP-linker reaction and succeeding Cat G conjugation.
Despite the fair results of NP-Cat G conjugation yielded from study 5, a concern about
utilising EDC-NHS-based coupling to Lys residues is the position of the Lys residues on
Cat G. As covered, solely the primary amine of Lys residues react with NHS-activated
constructs [106]. The positions of Lys residues are therefore of interest, as binding to Lys
in close promixmity to the active site of Cat G can greatly aﬀect the catalytic activity of
the enzyme. Three Lys residues are present in Cat G, visualised in Figure 4.9. In partic-
ular Lys192 and Lys217 are in somewhat close proximity of the active site, whereas Lys188
75
==PAGE:96==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Figure 4.8: Structure of NH2−PLGA−NH2-polymer with succinic hydrazide
endgroups. Taken from [100].
is not considered in the active site vicinity. Lys217 does not seem to be directly involved
in preservation of the active site, but potential conjugation of Cat G via linking of these
residues will likely immobolise the enzyme on the NP surface with the active site facing
towards the NP. Lys192 demarcates the S3 subsite of Cat G, as described in Section 2.6.1
[45, 49], thus being heavily involved in substrate binding. It is therefore suggested, that
Cat G-conjugation by Lys192 and Lys217 would entail a signiﬁcant drop in Cat G activity,
as the active site would be burried from IL-6 accessibility. On the other hand, based on
the position of Lys188, NP-Cat G-conjugation exploiting Lys188-linking would likely not
induce the same restriction in availability of the active site for IL-6 cleavage. Hence, suc-
cessful enzyme conjugation following the approach of EDC-NHS-based coupling to Cat
G Lys residues may be dependent on the speciﬁc coupling to Lys188 rather than Lys192
or Lys217. This criterion for success might inﬂuence potential observed catalytic activity
of the developed NP-Cat G system, both here and in the future. This potential eﬀect is
discussed further in Chapter 6.
Despite some success in study 5 in conjugating Cat G to the surface of polymeric NPs,
generally minimal success in both crosslinking and Cat G-conjugation has been obtained,
in particular for studies 3.A, 3.B and 4. It is clear that batch-to-batch variations af-
fect the prepared NP batches and that the successful preparation of linker coupled- and
Cat G-conjugated batches are inconsistent at best. Nevertheless, batches with maximum
NP-[Cat G] concentrations of 1.89 ± 0.30 µg/ml have been successfully obtained. The
yielded results therefore suggests that EDC-NHS coupling comprise the most appropriate
approach to obtain applicable NP-Cat G batches, although signiﬁcant procedure optimi-
sations are required in order to achieve higher conjugation yields. The nanobiocatalytic
eﬀect of these prepared conjugates is investigated by use of SDS-PAGE and enzyme kinetic
studies in Chapter 5.
The repetitive, limited achievements argue that the applied techniques here might not
be suitable for crosslinkage and conjugation of Cat G. However, as the approaches are
general methods that have been and are used extensively for similar purposes, it points
towards that the setup utilised here and the adjustments made to the procedure have
not been suﬃcient to achieve the desired results and functional nanobiocatalytic NPs.
Evidently, modiﬁcations to the ﬁnalised approach here are required for further optimisa-
tion. This could potentially be achieved by focusing on other polymers with improved,
suitable endgroups or changing the linking strategy to ﬁt the endgroup reactivity of the
already utilised NH2−PLGA−NH2 polymer, as already touched upon above. Further ad-
justments related to the work of increasing molar excesses of reagents as well as optimising
experiment conditions such as reaction time, temperature etc. are natural steps in the
76
==PAGE:97==
CHAPTER 4. POLYMERIC NP DEVELOPMENT
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Figure 4.9: Cathepsin G with active site residues His57, Asp102 and Ser195
and the three Lys188, Lys192 and Lys217 residues shown. RCSB PDB-ﬁle 1T32
visualised by use of PyMOL [10, 46].
investigation of how to enhance the success of crosslinker coupling and Cat G conjuga-
tion. These adjustments are potentially to be carried out in future work, as the limited
time provided during this work called a natural halt to further optimisations of the Cat
G-conjugation procedures, setup and experiments.
Development of a polmyeric, Cat G-conjugated nanobiocatalytic system has been at-
tempted and carried out with varying success. Whereas polymeric NP cores with suitable
biophysical characteristics are attainable and have been prepared with decent consistency,
the subsequent crosslinker coupling and conjugation of Cat G are seen to impose mul-
tiple challenges with limited successful results hereof. Nevertheless, single NP batches
with some veriﬁable Cat G-conjugation have been prepared and the potential catalytic
bioactivity of these derived NPs are tested in the following chapter.
77
==PAGE:98==
==PAGE:99==
Chapter 5
Nanobiocatalysis of IL-6 with
Cathepsin G Polymeric NPs
In Chapter 4, multiple NP batches with varying success on Cat G-conjugation have been
prepared. In order to assess the biocatalytic activity and applicability of these developed
NPs in cleaving IL-6 as well as inducing a biological eﬀect, activity analyses are conducted
for a series of prepared NPs. Nanobiocatalytic eﬀect towards IL-6 are assessed applying
previously established techniques and procedures in Chapter 3. In the wake of potential
catalytic activity of NPs, either NP-conjugated or free Cat G is utilised for assessing
the further biological eﬀect of Cat G on raw macrophages by investigating the levels
of cell-secreted IL-6 and intracellular STAT3 activation.
Ideally, catalytic activity of
developed NPs is seen as well as a Cat G-induced eﬀect on cell-secreted IL-6 in the eﬀort
to establishing proof of impact of the polymeric nanobiocatalyst towards eliminating IL-6.
5.1
Assessment of NP-Cat G catalytic activity
Proof-of-concept of a series of methods for assessing and quantifying catalytic breakdown
of IL-6 and enzymatic activity has previously been established in Chapter 3. SDS-PAGE
provides a suitable technique for investigating degradation of intact IL-6 upon incubation
with free Cat G enzyme, where enzyme kinetics studies yield information on the catalytic
activity of Cat G towards a speciﬁc, IL-6 resembling substrate.
The approaches of SDS-PAGE and enzyme kinetics can equally be exploited in the charac-
terisation of the bioactivity towards rh IL-6 of NPs developed as part of NP development
studies 3.A and 5 in Chapter 4. The experimental approaches to each experiment type
are largely unchanged from the established procedures, with similar rh IL-6 and substrate
concentrations, incubation times, reaction conditions etc. as used previously. Results
of the catalytic activity studies are introduced in the following and further discussed in
consolidation with additional experiments in Chapter 6.
5.1.1
SDS-PAGE
The methodology and procedure behind SDS-PAGE are described in Sections 3.2 and
8.2, respectively. The study conducted here applied the same approach as previously only
instead of addition of free Cat G, respective NP-Cat G sample was added in Cat G+

Jeppe Sejerø Holm
incubations. Of respective NP-Cat G sample, 5 µl was added to incubations regardless of

As previously reported, no indications of successful Cat G-conjugation for NPs, prepared
as part of NP development study 3.A in Chapter 4, were seen for neither prepared batch.
Coupled [Cat G] on NPs from NP development study 5 batch b) was quantiﬁed to 1.89
± 0.30 µg/ml.
Despite the non-existent NP-[Cat G] from NP development study 3.A, SDS-PAGE analysis
of NP incubation with IL-6 was still conducted. Both prepared NP batches, a) and b),
from study 3.A were incubated with rh IL-6 for 30, 60 and 120 min. The resulting SDS-
PAGE gel is shown in Figure 5.1(a). Rh IL-6 appears on the gel as a clear band with
apparent molecular weight of ∼22 kDa, slightly lower than what was seen in previous
SDS-PAGE studies in Section 3.2. Although this diﬀerence is somewhat puzzling, it is
likely just due to experimental variations rather than IL-6 degradation. In any case, no
time-dependent decrease in intact rh IL-6 following NP-Cat G incubations for 30, 60 and
120 min was observed for neither of the analysed batches, as well seen for incubation with
free Cat G in Section 3.2. The negative result is consistent with the previously observed
indications of limited successful Cat G-conjugation to the surface of the polymeric NPs
for these batches. It was therefore determined that the developed NPs were not seen
to exhibit catalytic activity towards IL-6 and that no Cat G has been conjugated to
NP-surfaces. The NPs from study 3.A were not used further.

30, 60, 120 and 180 min based on the results shown in Figure 5.1(a) in the hope that posi-

as sample NP-[Cat G] amassed to only 1.89 ± 0.30 µg/ml during NP development. Al-
though IL-6 failed to be added to 1 h incubations, explaining the lack of respective bands
on the gel, a time-dependent decrease in intact IL-6 is observed after incubations with
Cat G-coupled NPs, comparing NP-Cat+- to NP-Cat G−samples. The percentage-wise
decrease in IL-6 signalling of each band around ∼22 kDa representing IL-6 at each time
point is shown in Figure 5.2. As a ∼27 % and ∼20 % decrease in IL-6 signalling at 30 min
and 2 h, respectively, compared to ∼44 % signal decrease after 3 h incubation, a notably
enhanced drop in intact [IL-6] is observed with extended incubation time up to 3 h. Ad-
ditionally, for all incubations in the experiment, a band at approximately ∼13.5 kDa is
seen vaguely across all IL-6+ incubations. Although very weak, the bands are presumably
IL-6 fragments as has been seen and discussed earlier for SDS-PAGE studies in Section
3.2. It is, however, noteworthy that the band strength is not increased with incubation
time, similar to the previous SDS-PAGE studies. Nevertheless, time-dependent intact
IL-6 breakdown following NP-Cat G incubation is observed, although the results are only
obtained after 3 h incubation.

5.1.2
Kinetics
In Section 3.4, the methodology and approach behind conducted enzyme kinetics ex-
periments are introduced.
In order to investigate and compare enzymatic activity of
NP-conjugated Cat G to free Cat G, a similar enzyme kinetics study to the previously
conducted is therefore set up and performed for NP-Cat G particles.
As described above, NPs from NP development study 3.A did not present any catalytic
activity and further use of these NPs was discontinued. Hence, no enzyme kinetics exper-
iments have been conducted with the use of these NPs.
On the other hand, NPs prepared as part of NP development study 5 presented some
time-dependent eﬀect in IL-6 degradation, potentially from IL-6 cleavage. As a result,
the bioactivity of the NPs was therefore studied further by enzyme kinetics investigation.
The procedure applied was identical to the previously utilised and described in Section
8.4, apart from the addition of 10 µl NP-Cat G solution rather than free Cat G and the
addition of 10 µl 50 mM HEPES buﬀer 7.4 instead of 10 µl H2O in reference buﬀer samples.
(a) [P] during 0-150 min.
(b) [P] during 0-40 min.
Figure 5.3:

of Suc-AAPF-pNA yielding colorimetric product of pNA absorbing at 410 nm.

seen, in particular (b).
In Figure 5.3, developments in product [pNA] from varying [S]0 over time are visualised.
Large error bars of each N = 2 measurements for each time point are seen. Especially
between t = 0 to ∼40 min., these error bars overlap and include the mean values of mea-
surements for the concurrent concentrations indicating large measurement errors. Nev-

mM, showing catalytic activity of the conjugated Cat G. No increase in [P] in reference,

previously seen activities for free Cat G in Sections 3.2 and 3.4. However, it is already
clear that bioactivites of developed Cat G-conjugated NPs are inadequate, both evident
from SDS-PAGE and the enzyme kinetics experiment. It was therefore decided not to
utilise these NPs in further experiments, as [Cat G] and catalytic activity were insuﬃcient.
Instead, free Cat G, rather than NP-Cat G, was used in the subsequent experiment with
the purpose of investigating catalytic activity of Cat G towards IL-6 secreted from cells.

Jeppe Sejerø Holm
5.2
Cell study
The original objective of this work was to study the proteolytic eﬀects of NPs in an vitro
setup involving live cells, as in vivo studies of developed NPs were not to be conducted
during this thesis. Instead, the study of loss of IL-6 bioactivity on viable cells following
NP-Cat G treatment was therefore the ultimate aim here.
Ideally, investigation into the biocatalytic eﬀect of NP-Cat G conjugates on the expres-
sion of IL-6 from LPS-stimulated raw mouse macrophages would have been conducted.
However, with the ineﬀectual catalytic activity of the developed NPs, the eﬀect of Cat G,
rather than NP-Cat G, on cell-expressed levels of IL-6 as well as intracellular expression
of STAT3 were measured instead. To fully investigate the cell-related eﬀect of Cat G,
measures of ELISA and Western Blotting were applied for this purpose. The method-
ology and procedures behind the cell-involving experiment as well as western blotting is
brieﬂy introduced, whereas ELISA towards mouse IL-6 was performed as previous with
few alterations. Results are reported below but discussed in the ensuing Chapter 6.
5.2.1
Methodology
Expression of IL-6 following LPS stimulation
Raw 264.7 macrophages are naive, unstimulated, immortalised monocytes originating
from mice [122].
Upon stimulation by lipopolysaccharides (LPS), naturally found in
the outer membrane of Gram-negative bacteria, through TLR-4 [123], these monocytes
diﬀerentiate into M1 macrophages by intracellular STAT3 activation and secrete pro-
inﬂammatory cytokines such as TNF-α, IL-1β and IL-6 [123, 124, 125, 126]. That STAT3
and IL-6 expressed following LPS stimulation can be exploited here as it allows for the
measure of stimulation and pro-inﬂammatory diﬀerentiation of the naive cells.
By treatment with Cat G, preferably as part of a NP-Cat G conjugate but here instead
free Cat G, a drop in extracellular, cell-secreted [IL-6] compared to non-Cat G-treated
cells is expected. Additionally, it is investigated whether initially raised levels of STAT3
following LPS-stimulation is aﬀected following decreased [IL-6] as part of a potential
feedback mechanism, since IL-6 is a potent activator of STAT3, see Section 2.2. [IL-
6] in extracellular supernatant following incubation with Cat G is assessed by ELISA,
although it has previously been established that ELISA is incapable of diﬀerentiating
between cleaved and intact IL-6 in Section 3.1. Expression of STAT3 is investigated by
Western Blot following protein extraction from incubated cells.
Western Blotting
Western blotting is a further development of the previously described SDS-PAGE, see
Section 3.2. But as SDS-PAGE utilise staining agents such as coommassie blue to un-
speciﬁcally stain protein bands in samples, this approach is particular unﬁt for use for
cytoplasm-originating samples, as innumerable sample proteins would be stained. This
would make size-based protein diﬀerentiation impossible. But whereas western blotting
utilises the same approach of protein separation by gel electrophoresis, the detection and
diﬀerentiation of speciﬁc sample proteins is conducted by highly speciﬁc binding of mAbs
85
==PAGE:106==
CHAPTER 5. NANOBIOCATALYSIS WITH POLYMERIC NPS
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
to the protein epitope in question. The approach of western blotting allows for signiﬁcant
improvement in the speciﬁcity of detection.
Following separation, sample proteins can migrate from the electrophoresis gel onto e.g. a
polyvinylidene ﬂuoride (PVDF)-membrane upon electric charge, towards the positive pole
due to the negative charge of proteins [127]. The PVDF-membrane presents the ability to
adsorb the transferred proteins and by incubation with a highly speciﬁc primary mAb to-
wards the protein of interest and subsequently by a secondary Ab with speciﬁcity towards
the Fc region of the primary mAb, protein speciﬁc detection is achievable. HRP is con-
jugated to the secondary Ab and based on conversion of the chemiluminescent substrate
ECL, a ﬂourescent signal will only be provided where the complex of protein:primary
mAb:secondary Ab is present, thus exclusively highlighting the protein of which the pri-
mary mAb is speciﬁc towards [128]. In this experiment, primary rabbit anti-mouse STAT3
and anti-β-Actin mAbs were utilised together with secondary goat anti-rabbit polyclon-
cal Abs. The secondary Ab requires speciﬁcity towards Abs of the species in which the
primary Ab was raised to avoid crossreactivity. The membrane is imaged using the 600
and 700 nm as well as chemiluminescence channels of LI-COR Odyssey® FC gel imager,
where ladders will appear in the 600 and 700 nm channel whereas bands for the protein
of interest are visualised by chemiluminescence. Expression of cell β-actin is used for
normalisation of STAT3 expression data.
5.2.2
Results
The study was set up to investigate the eﬀects of LPS-stimulation upon raw 264.7 macrophages
in their ability to be diﬀerentiated into pro-inﬂammatory M1 macrophages and secrete
IL-6. Subsequent to LPS-stimulation, the eﬀects of Cat G cleavage of cell-secreted IL-6
were studied by incubation of varying [Cat G] to study the levels of IL-6 by ELISA as
well as intracellular activation of STAT3 by Western Blot.
To conduct a comparative study of the eﬀects of LPS and Cat G, the following experiment
layout was set up in two 6-well plates, as according to the setup shown in Table 5.3. N = 2
for each sample type. The cells were treated based on the procedure described in Section
8.7. The results from each aspect of the study including cell morphology changes, [IL-6]
analysis by ELISA and STAT3 expression assessed by western blot are covered below.

Cell morphology changes
During the course of the conducted experiment, signiﬁcant changes were observed in cell
morphology of the stimulated macrophages upon stimulation with 100 ng/ml LPS and
subsequent treatment with [Cat G]. In Figure 5.5, some representative images captured
of the cell types during the study is shown. The remaining captured images are shown in
Appendix F.
Amongst the samples, cells were generally seen to be alive, in good condition and of good
quality. Throughout the study, cell cultures were sticking to the well-sufaces with few
dead cells ﬂoating around. The shown images are representative of the cell morphologies
and conditions at the given time.
Non-treated- and LPS-treated cells are compared between Figures 5.5(a) and 5.5(b). Non-
treated ’Cells only’ were generally seen as round cells with minimal elongation, repre-
senting unstimulated cells, whereas LPS-treated cells were signiﬁcantly more elongated.
LPS has previously been reported to induce ﬂattening and elongation of M1-stimulated
macrophages [129]. The elongation is deemed due to the LPS-stimulation, causing the
raw macrophages to diﬀerentiate into M1 marophages and intercommunicate due to the
presence of bacterial LPS.
The cell cultures were treated with 1 and 5 µg/ml Cat G for 24 h in respective samples.
LPS+/Cat G−-treated cells were generally seen to exhibit some continuous elongation,
but not to the same extent as seen prior to removal of LPS-containing media, evident
from Figures 5.5(b) and 5.5(d). Hence, following the removal of LPS-stimulant, cells are
evidently less prone to intercommunication.
However, by comparing non-Cat G- with Cat G-treated samples, it was clear that Cat G-
treated cells shown in Figures 5.5(e) and 5.5(f) exhibited a more round morphology, similar
to the observed for cells-only samples in Figure 5.5(c), compared to the LPS+/Cat G−-
treated cells in Figure 5.5(d). This indicated that the LPS-triggered intercommunication
between cells had decreased following Cat G-treatment, but that cell-to-cell interactions
still appeared to be occurring. It may be argued that a slightly more circular morpholog-
ical structures were observed for cells treated with the higher Cat G dose, compared to
lower dose-treated cells, but this diﬀerence was not signiﬁcant thus a tendency could not
be deduced.
In addition to the observed ﬁgures captured with 40x magniﬁcation, 20x magniﬁcation
sample images of the referred to samples together with images of pre-LPS treated and 1
µg/ml and 5 µg/ml Cat G-only cell samples are included in Appendix F. Cat G-only cells
treated with 1 µg/ml and 5 µg/ml Cat G were included as reference samples, visualised in
Figures F.2(d) and F.2(f), and were generally seen to present similar shapes as cells-only
samples indicating no cell morphology-inﬂuencing factors of Cat G treatment.
Mouse IL-6 ELISA
Mouse [IL-6] in each double-determined cell sample supernatant following 24 h incubation
with Cat G was measured by ELISA as described in Section 8.1, only that a 1000 pg/ml
2-fold IL-6 standard curve was made here, using 100 µl standard or sample in each well.

Interestingly, [IL-6] was observed to notably decrease following Cat G-treatment for 24 h.
The drop is [IL-6] was seen to be Cat G-dose dependent; further enhanced with increas-
ing [Cat G] as 1 µg/ml Cat G treatment yielded [IL-6] = 3579.97 ± 368.20 pg/ml and
cell incubation with 5 µg/ml Cat G resulted in [IL-6] = 2926.07 ± 151.00 pg/ml. It is
therefore obvious that Cat G-incubation inﬂicts decreased [IL-6], verifying the previously
observed results in Chapter 3 of IL-6 cleavage by Cat G.
The mouse IL-6 ELISA results in relation to the observed changed morphology of cells
upon LPS-stimulation and Cat G-treatment is discussed in Chapter 6.

From Figure 5.7(b), an uniform expression of β-actin is observed across all samples with
no signiﬁcant diﬀerences across the various sample types. β-actin is seen as a protein
band at approximately ∼37 kDa, slightly lower than the expected 45 kDa [131]. It is
nevertheless determined that β-actin is successfully visualised by the conducted western
blot.
The signalling strengths of both STAT3 and β-actin band expressions were determined
by use of the LI-COR gel imager software. For cells-only expression of STAT3, the signal
strength of the areas where STAT3-bands would have been expected based on bands in
adjacent lanes were measured and constituted the signal values, despite no evident bands
here. Based on the measured signal strengths, the expression of STAT3 was normalised
by β-actin band strengths by calculation of an expression ratio. The signal strengths are
shown in Appendix G.2 together with the calculated ratio of each replicate. Mean ratio
values and deviations are plotted in Figure 5.8.
90
==PAGE:111==

Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Figure 5.8:
Western blot signalling strength ratio of STAT3 vs.
β-actin
expression in cell samples. The underlying data is listed in Appendix G.2.
In particular apparent from the expression ratios in Figure 5.8, an increased expression
in STAT3 was seen in LPS-stimulated cells compared to cells-only cells. Some increased
STAT3 expression was also seen in Cat G-only cells, but with expression measures more
similar to cells-only than LPS-stimulated. Although inﬂuenced by great variation between
replicate measurements, no clear diﬀerence between Cat G-treated cells and LPS-only cells
is observed. It could be argued that treating cells with 5 µg/ml Cat G compared to 1
µg/ml Cat G-treatment could lead to a more pronounced STAT3 expression in cells, but
signal strength deviations overshadow this potential deduction. Hence, STAT3 expression
is increased in LPS-stimulated cells but not seen to decrease in Cat G-treated cells.
During the cell study, an observable decrease in oblong cell morphology following treat-
ment with Cat G was seen compared to LPS-only cells. Additionally, signiﬁcant Cat
G-dose dependent decrease in supernatant [IL-6] was observed for cell samples incubated
with Cat G. Although western blots failed to directly show any decreased STAT3 expres-
sion in cells subject to Cat G-treatment, increased expression of STAT3 was nevertheless
seen in LPS-stimulated cells compared to unstimulated and non-treated cells.
In the above chapter, the biocatalytic activities of NP-Cat G conjugates have been in-
vestigated. Proteolytic activity towards cleaving IL-6 assessed by SDS-PAGE as well as
enzyme kinetics measurements have been conducted. The observed results overall indi-
cated unsatisfactory results with limited IL-6 cleavage and slow enzymatic activity. Based

==PAGE:112==

Jeppe Sejerø Holm
on these results, the eﬀects of free Cat G, rather than NP-Cat G, on LPS-stimulated raw
macrophages were investigated by visual cell examination, measurements of [IL-6] by
ELISA and STAT3 expression by Western Blot. The biocatalytic activities and eﬃcien-
cies of developed NPs are compared to the activities of free Cat G enzyme in the ensuing
Chapter 6. Furthermore, the results from the conducted cell study and eﬀects of Cat G
are equally discussed in the following.

Chapter 6
Discussion & Future outlook
In the previous chapters the development of nanobiocatalytic NPs have been described
with the activities hereof investigated. The following chapter discusses the observed activ-
ity results of developed NPs in relation to the NP development. Subsequently, the activity
of Cat G in cellular setups are discussed. The prospects of successful NP development,
based on the combined observations of NP-Cat G- and Cat G-activities are covered and
ultimately the future outlook and goals of the ongoing project are described.
6.1
Nanobiocatalytic activity of NPs
Described in Chapter 5, SDS-PAGE and kinetics experiments have been conducted using
developed Cat G-conjugated NPs from NP development study 5 in Chapter 4. Generally,
only a slight catalytic activity is observed for the prepared NPs, detailed below.
6.1.1
IL-6 cleavage
From SDS-PAGE, activity of NP-Cat G NPs is indicated by a time-dependent decrease in
IL-6. This IL-6 breakdown is likely due to a cleaving of IL-6 by Cat G, as is seen for pre-
vious SDS-PAGE experiments in Section 3.2. It is however notable that the IL-6 cleavage
by NP-Cat G occurs signiﬁcantly slower than in incubations with free Cat G. The compa-
rable reduction in IL-6 integrity is obtained at prolonged time-frames following NP-Cat
G and IL-6 incubations with decrease in IL-6 integrity after 3 h incubation, Figure 5.1,
being comparable to the loss of IL-6 after 15 min IL-6 incubation with 1:5 ratio of free
Cat G, see Figure 3.7(b). Comparing the measured % IL-6 signal strength decreases from
Cat G and NP-Cat G SDS-PAGE studies in Figures 3.8 and 5.2, respectively, the delayed
eﬀect in cleaving IL-6 is evident. With IL-6 signal %-decrease after 15 min incubation
with Cat G resembling the % decrease after 3 h incubation with NP-Cat G, activity of
NP-Cat G towards cleaving is obviously signiﬁcantly reduced. This is clear as the similar
quantity of cleaved IL-6 after 15 min with free Cat G requires 3 h incubation to achieve
with NP-Cat G. Hence, a signiﬁcant decrease in catalytic activity towards IL-6 is seen
for NP-Cat G compared to free Cat G, further conﬁrming the insuﬃcient activity of the
prepared NPs towards IL-6.
93
==PAGE:114==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION & FUTURE OUTLOOK
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
On the other hand, [Cat G] on NP-Cat G particles is equally considerably lower than
[Cat G] in free-Cat G incubations. With [IL-6] being identical between the SDS-PAGE
experiments yielding Figures 3.7(b) and 5.1, free-[Cat G] utilised in proof-of-concept SDS-
PAGE study 5.b equals 0.16 µg per sample, whereas by use of 5 µl NP-Cat G from NP
development study 5 [Cat G] amount to 0.00945 µg per sample, thus merely ∼6 % of free
[Cat G]. The lower NP-[Cat G] is indisputably a key factor in the delayed IL-6 cleaving and
increased IL-6 cleavage following NP-Cat G incubations would undoubtedly be expected
had NP-[Cat G] been higher, potentially at levels seen for free Cat G. But with NP-surface
Cat G conjugation posing a serious challenge, optimisations and improvements in Cat G-
coupling yield would be required for increased NP-[Cat G] in order to further investigate
and compare the catalytic activity of NP-Cat G towards IL-6 with free Cat G.
6.1.2
Enzyme kinetic activity
Despite the discussed observations of limited NP-Cat G activity, the eﬀect on IL-6 cleavage
caused by comparably lower NP-[Cat G] cannot be precisely determined by SDS-PAGE,
only estimated. Enzyme kinetics experiments were therefore utilised to investigate the
rate of catalytic activity of NP-Cat G despite the substrate not being IL-6. From Table
5.2, derived enzymatic parameters for NP-Cat G can be compared to parameters of free
Cat G, determined in Section 3.4. As the experiments were conducted identically, the
activities of Cat G in each study can therefore be equitably compared.
The enzymatic activity and reaction velocity v of NP-Cat G towards the Suc-AAPF-pNA
substrate is undeniably lower than free Cat G, in particular with a much slower increase
in [P]. Hence, steady state was also assumed for an extended time-frame compared to
the previous kinetics study. This extended time-frame for steady state is self-evident due
to the remarkably lower number of enzymes available for the same amount of substrate.
Hence, it is clear that the given NP-Cat G enzymes can be occupied by substrate for a
longer period of time, explaining the prolonged steady-state scenario.
Vmax, being the maximum reaction velocity [95], is signiﬁcantly reduced for NP-Cat G
compared to free Cat G, also evident from the decreased v between the studies. Concur-
rently, the required [S] to reach 1
2Vmax, KM, is also increased ∼3-fold compared to for free
Cat G. Although Vmax and KM thus in combination show a reduced catalytic activity of
NP-Cat G, the turnover number [95], kcat is similar to kcat of free Cat G. This indicates
that the number of substrate molecules converted by Cat G per second is close to equal
between free and NP-conjugated Cat G. The maintained kcat suggests that by Cat G is
unconcerned by possible enzyme conjugation in the ability to perform the ﬁnal conversion
of ES→E + P, see Figure 3.11 [95]. It thus illustrates the preserved and similar activity
of NP-coupled Cat G compared to free enzyme. Additionally, it shows that feasible en-
zymatic activity can be obtained despite particle conjugation.
Similar to SDS-PAGE studies, applied incubation NP-[Cat G] was lower than for free
Cat G incubations with NP-[Cat G] only comprising 1.89 % of applied free [Cat G]. As
Vmax is dependent of [E]0, see Equation 3.5, the maintained kcat thus implies that it is the
lower NP-[Cat G], compared to free [Cat G] in Section 3.4, that inﬂuences the observed
decreased maximum reaction velocity of NP-Cat G, rather than a reduced ability to con-
vert ES-complex into product.
94
==PAGE:115==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION & FUTURE OUTLOOK
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Despite preserved kcat the enzymatic eﬃciency, denoted by kcat/KM, is comparably lower
for NP-Cat G than for free Cat G. The eﬃciency drop for NP-Cat G is prompted by the
increasing KM as kcat is almost constant between the two studies. As KM is a measure of
aﬃnity for substrate towards enzyme, explained in Section 3.4, the change in enzymatic
eﬃciency is related to a decreased ability of substrate to bind eﬀectively to the enzyme.
Touched upon in Section 4.2.3, Cat G Lys-residues are exploited in NP-enzyme conju-
gation, when utilising the EDC-NHS-based coupling approach. The Lys-residues Lys192
and Lys217 are positioned close to the active site, visualised by Figure 4.9. It may be
argued that conjugation of Cat G to NP-surfaces through these Lys-residues will induce
at least slight conformational eﬀect on the enzyme and some drop in Cat G activity. This
coupling could therefore entail a reduction in substrate aﬃnity - thus enzymatic eﬃciency
towards the cleaving of substrate - as the substrate may either be hindered in accessing
the active site or in another way being susceptible to a decreased binding aﬃnity to Cat
G. An enzyme will generally function to its maximum capability in a setting closest to
its natural conditions, in the case of Cat G; freely, not bound to particle surfaces.This
decreased aﬃnity may therefore be due to a possible negative impact on Cat G following
NP-coupling.
As the measure of kcat is largely unchanged compared to free Cat G, it is suggested that
the similar, potent activity of the enzyme in converting ES-complex to product is pre-
served for NP-Cat G. However, enzymatic eﬃciency is decreased as kcat/KM is lower for
NP-Cat G than free Cat G. This eﬃciency drop is suggested to be due to a similar decrease
in substrate aﬃnity based on increased KM. Therefore, the overall process of substrate
to product conversion, characterised as kcat/KM, is here restricted by the binding of the
substrate with lower aﬃnity than previously observed for free Cat G. This decreased sub-
strate aﬃnity, and thus reduction in overall enzyme eﬃciency, is suggested to be due to
the utilisation of Lys-residues close to the active site for enzyme coupling for some of the
Cat G enzymes. In combination, due to lower applied [Cat G] as well as aﬃnity drop
and weakened binding between enzyme and substrate, product accumulation and general
enzyme activity is evidently slower during NP-Cat G incubation compared to free Cat
G. Hence, the eﬃciency of NP-Cat G in substrate cleavage is then likely experiencing
reduction due to NP immobilisation, probable slight conformational adaptation and con-
cealing of the active site, while the slow increase in [P] is also greatly aﬀected by the low
experimental NP-[Cat G].
Signiﬁcant and promising enzymatic activity is nevertheless still seen for Cat G upon
immobilisation onto NP-surfaces. This shows that Cat G activity yield is feasible after
NP-conjugation. It is additionally proposed that by an improved success in NP-coupling of
Cat G than the observed, this increased enzyme NP-surface immobilisation will potentially
overshadow any restriction in Cat G activity caused by the conformational aspects. The
increased Cat G quantity on NP surfaces will thus potentially surpass the reduced quality
in enzyme eﬃciency. This is likely, as immobilising enzymes onto NP-surfaces have been
seen to enhance enzymatic activity substantially up to a 5-fold activity increase across
a broad variety of enzymes [66, 67]. As introduced in Section 2.8.1, the improvement in
enzyme activity as a result of NP immobilisation is primarily substantiated by increased
enzyme-density on NP-surfaces compared to free enzyme, enhanced substrate attraction
95
==PAGE:116==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION & FUTURE OUTLOOK
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
to NP surface, improved curvature of NPs and favourable enzyme orientation towards
the substrate following enzyme immobilisation [66, 67]. Although the Cat G orientation
is here suggested not to improve substrate interactions, in particular increased Cat G
density would lead to an overall improved catalytic eﬃciency towards IL-6, if the NP-Cat
G surface conjugation is achieved with higher yields and greater success.
To further optimise the conjugation, reaction volumes may be a further key focus points.
As is described in Chapter 4, the general volumes of NP solution used for preparation of
biocatalytic NPs are considerably low throughout the preparation process. As example,
15 µl linked NPs are used for Cat G-conjugation performed in a 1 ml reaction solution.
It is suggested that by increasing the volume of linked-NP solution or by generally de-
creasing reaction volumes, a more successful reaction and Cat G-conjugation could occur.
By decreasing reaction volumes and conducting the reactions on a microlitre scale, the
likelihood of successful reaction would thus be signiﬁcantly increased.
In conclusion, enzymatic activity of the developed Cat G-conjugated NPs are seen to
be very low. The successful development of nanobiocatalytic polymeric NPs have been
signiﬁcantly troublesome and conjugation of NP-surface Cat G has not been performed
with particular high yields. The limited successful Cat G-conjugation and thus minimal
NP-[Cat G] naturally has an impact on the insuﬃcient biocatalytic activity of the de-
veloped NPs. However, prepared Cat G-coupled NPs still exhibit a measurable eﬀect
despite the low [Cat G] successfully immobilised to the NP-surface, although not with the
same eﬃciency as for free Cat G enzymes. In particular, the turnover number of substrate
molecules given as kcat present similarly high measures for NP-Cat G in comparison to free
Cat G. This means that for each system, the same number of substrate molecules are con-
verted per time. kcat is therefore not aﬀected by Cat G immobilisation onto NP-surfaces.
Furthermore, despite IL-6 cleavage being observed to the same extent for NP-Cat G after
3 h incubation as for Cat G after 15 min, some delayed activity of Cat G-conjugated NPs
is still seen. Slight activity of the developed NPs is therefore clear but not to the same
extent as has been observed for free Cat G. It is therefore proposed that if and when
conjugation of Cat G is achieved with higher yields, producing enhanced bioactive NPs,
the catalytic activity of NPs towards cleaving IL-6 will be remarkably improved. This
activity may go beyond the catalytic eﬃciency seen for free Cat G and potentially to
greater levels than previously observed in research for NP-scaﬀolded enzymes.
96
==PAGE:117==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION & FUTURE OUTLOOK
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
6.2
Eﬀect of Cat G on cell secreted IL-6
In Section 5.2.2, changes in cell morphology at various stages during the cell study, cell
supernatants [IL-6] from ELISA and western blots of intracellular STAT3 expression are
shown. These results are discussed in the below section followed by the combined impacts
covered subsequently.
6.2.1
Cell morphology, [IL-6] and STAT3 expression
Naive macrophages are in the conducted experiment seen to change to more oblong mor-
phology upon LPS-stimulation and diﬀerentiation into M1 macrophages compared to
unstimulated cells, as described in Section 5.2.2 [129]. This elongation of cells here upon
stimulation of the bacterial PAMP LPS is suggested to be due to increased intercommu-
nication between cells in the eﬀort to raise a natural immune response or provoked by the
LPS-induced cell apoptosis, causing cells to communicate with neighbouring cells [132].
Following Cat G-treatment, the observed cells are seen to adapt to a more unstimulated-
cell-like morphology with more circular traits. This change proposes a decrease in cell-
to-cell intercommunication, likely due to clearing of LPS-stimuli and potentially due to
decreased [IL-6] and thus less pro-inﬂammatory signalling between cells. The link between
morphology and [IL-6] is investigated further below in Section 6.2.2.
From ELISA, both LPS-stimulation and Cat G-treatment has great eﬀects on extracel-
lular [IL-6]. Stimulating cells with LPS causes an immense production and secretion of
IL-6 compared to non-stimulated cells and LPS prompts a substantial pro-inﬂammatory
response in cells that secretes pro-inﬂammatory IL-6 to high extents. Subsequent treat-
ment of M1 diﬀerentiated macrophages with Cat G entails signiﬁcant reduction in [IL-6],
showing that Cat G has a remarkable impact on cleaving cell-secreted IL-6 and thus re-
moving the extracellular eﬀects caused by IL-6. The impact of Cat G towards cleaving
cell-secreted IL-6 here conﬁrms the previously described trends for simplistic setups while
showing the potential of Cat G catalytic activity towards pro-inﬂammatory IL-6 in more
biologically true environments.
The ability of ELISA to quantify [IL-6] and in particular the diﬀerence and decrease in
[IL-6] following incubation and cleavage by Cat G is observed here. This is however con-
tradicting to the previously observed and inferred in Section 3.1. Although the in Section
3.1 deduced results are still deemed valid, it is somewhat uncertain why ELISA is able to
quantiﬁably diﬀerentiate between cleaved and uncleaved IL-6 in this experiment, with the
decreased measurement of [IL-6], compared to the previous studies. However, despite the
results being conﬂicting with the previous, it is important to understand the diﬀerence
in that previous studies were conducted in a simplistic setup with only Cat G and IL-6.
On the other hand, the layout of cell-involving experiments provides a diﬀerent, more
complex biological system, increasingly similar to physiological conditions. It might be
suggested that the fragmented IL-6 is either further cleaved by other proteases or in some
way taken up by the macrophages, although this currently remains uncertain.
The results from western blotting of STAT3 indicate limited inﬂuence of Cat G in lowering
intracellular STAT3 expression following treatment of LPS-stimulated macrophages with
97
==PAGE:118==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION & FUTURE OUTLOOK
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Cat G. Western blot is clearly a suitable technique for quantiﬁcation of LPS-induced
STAT3 expression as trends between non- and LPS-stimulated cells indicate increased
levels of STAT3 expression in the latter, but the shown results dictate no evident impact
of intracellular STAT3-levels by Cat G-treatment.
6.2.2
Impact of LPS, IL-6 and Cat G
The observed changes in cell morphology throughout the study goes hand in hand with the
[IL-6] results from ELISA. Supernatant [IL-6], being IL-6 secreted from cells following pro-
inﬂammatory stimulation, is seen to decrease signiﬁcantly and Cat G dose-dependently
following incubation with Cat G compared to non-treated cell samples. The positive ef-
fects of Cat G towards IL-6 elimination are thus clear They also verify the previously
observed tendencies with Cat G having a disrupting eﬀect towards intact IL-6, as de-
scribed throughout Chapter 3 as well as in previous research [23, 44, 45].
After LPS-stimulation, no onward stimulatory source originates from LPS as LPS is re-
moved from the media. But as [IL-6] is decreased, due to the catalytic cleavage by Cat
G, less pro-inﬂammatory signalling between cells is suggested to follow. Hence, the drop
in intercommunication between cells, deduced from the decreased cell elongation, may
therefore be reasoned by the decrease in pro-inﬂammatory IL-6 presence and signalling,
comparing LPS-only- with Cat G-treated cells. This may explain the equal mitigation
in cell elongation, thus less cell-to-cell signalling, and adaptation to a more round cell
morphology following Cat G-treatment. The cells are therefore seen to increasingly ac-
commodate to a less pro-inﬂammatory environment with less cell-to-cell communication
as a result of increasing elimination of pro-inﬂammatory IL-6 signalling. These consid-
erations are consistent with the well-known eﬀects of IL-6 preserving and promoting a
pro-inﬂammatory microenvironment [1, 2], previously described in Sections 2.1 and 2.2.
Whereas ELISA results of increasingly aﬀected [IL-6] by Cat G-treatment interplay with
cell morphology changes, the associated expression patterns of intracellular STAT3 as-
sessed by Western Blot are not as clear. As expected, expression of STAT3 is enhanced
signiﬁcantly upon LPS-stimulation which then prompts the observed increase in secreted
[IL-6], previously described in Section 5.2.1. After LPS-stimulation, as LPS is removed
from the incubation media ideally further LPS-induced stimulation can be disregarded
from this point on. However, the LPS-induced eﬀect on cells is likely not diminished
immediately and some continuous, downstream STAT3 expression must be expected as a
delayed eﬀect from LPS-stimulation. In any way, produced and continuously secreted IL-6
then further causes STAT3 expression, as known from Section 2.2. But as is evident from
the western blot, no decrease in STAT3 expression is observed for cell samples with lower
[IL-6] in the supernatant. The eﬀect of Cat G in decreasing extracellular [IL-6] is there-
fore not directly inﬂuential in lowering intracellular STAT3 expression. Hence, no direct
correlation between changed cell morphology, altered extracellular [IL-6] and expression
pattern of intracellular STAT3 can deduced based on the observed results. The western
blots do therefore not directly conﬁrm the trends of decreased inﬂammatory activity seen
from cell morphology images and ELISA.
Multiple explanations can explain this lack of decrease in intracellular STAT3 expression
98
==PAGE:119==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION & FUTURE OUTLOOK
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
from lowered extracellular [IL-6]. It may be suggested that since STAT3 is not exclusively
activated by IL-6, but also other cytokines such as TNF-α and IL-1β [123, 124, 125, 126].
These pro-inﬂammatory cytokines, including IL-6, may still be present in the extracellular
media or continue to be secreted, as cell expression of pro-inﬂammatory cytokines likely
persists for some time after LPS-stimuli removal, representing the natural immune system
in the eﬀort to ensure complete pathogen elimination. Expression of STAT3 would thus
continue despite the decreased [IL-6] following Cat G cleavage, explaining the observed,
maintained expression levels of STAT3 following Cat G-treatment.
Another possible explanation might be that the downregulation of STAT3 simply does
not occur fast enough within the investigated time-frame.
Although [IL-6] is notably
decreased following Cat G-cleavage, the decrease in concentration together with the per-
sistent presence and eﬀect of e.g. TNF-α and IL-1β might not be substantial enough for
STAT3 expression to be aﬀected and lowered within the experimental time frame. In other
words, the eﬀect of Cat G in decreasing is not appropriately eﬃcient and [IL-6] might not
be reduced suﬃciently for STAT3 to be aﬀected here as the STAT3-induction of TNF-α
and IL-1β likely also contributes to the complex pathway relationship. Additionally, LPS-
induced anti-inﬂammatory IL-10 is also seen to activate STAT3 [133, 134, 135]. This may
suggest that IL-10-mediated STAT3 activation also occurs here and that the persistent
activation of STAT3 by IL-10 is not aﬀected by the activity of Cat G, explaining the lack
of STAT3 expression decrease.
To further investigate the relation between IL-6 and STAT and the contribution hereto
from TNF-α, IL-1β and IL-10, concentrations of these cytokines could be determined
upon repeat of an experiment similar to the conducted. It is important to understand
this experiment stands alone and that further repetitions and optimisations are required
to reproduce the trends and to draw ﬁrmer conclusions.
In conclusion, Cat G is seen to have substantial, positive impact in cleaving and decreasing
cell-secreted [IL-6], causing signiﬁcantly reduced pro-inﬂammatory signalling between M1
LPS-stimulated macrophages. The inﬂuence of Cat G-treatment also extend to aﬀecting
the morphology of LPS-stimulated cells, as lower levels of IL-6 are generally seen to
correlate with cells presenting morphologies resembling the shape of non-inﬂammatory
stimulated cells. Despite intracellular STAT3 expression is initially not seen to diminish
after Cat G-treatment, the observed trends showcase the potential and highly promising
eﬀects of Cat G-administration to pro-inﬂammatory cells.
Treating cells with Cat G
can therefore be utilised in the eﬀort of eliminating the pro-tumourigenic impacts of
extracellular pro-inﬂammatory IL-6 signalling between cancer cells.
6.3
Prospects of successful NP development
From the work conducted here, it is evident that the development of nanobiocatalytic NPs
has been challenging and generally achieved with inadequate yields and insuﬃcient cat-
alytic activity. These observations are both clear from the techniques utilised to quantify
conjugation of Cat G onto NP-surfaces but also from the experiments conducted applying
the developed NPs, being SDS-PAGE and enzyme kinetics studies. From these studies it is
likewise clear that despite the minimal success in Cat G-conjugation, prominent catalytic
99
==PAGE:120==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION & FUTURE OUTLOOK
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
activity is still seen for the NPs, taken into account the poor success of Cat G NP-surface
immobilisation and general NP development. The limit in this project has therefore not
been the activity of the individual, prepared NPs but rather the unsuccessful preparation
and conjugation of Cat G to NP-surfaces that has shown itself to be the major challenge
and hindrance for the success of this project so far.
It is strongly believed that the modiﬁcations and alterations required for development
of a successful nanobiocatalytic particles are feasible and can be implemented to achieve
a highly functioning NP-Cat G system. Some changes to protocols have already been
proposed. To summarise, general approach modiﬁcations include focus on utilisation of
polymers with non-burried, reactive endgroups such as −NH2 rather than hydrazide-based
polymers. Additionally, application of the approach of EDC-NHS-based linking and Cat
G-conjugation can be utilised further, as the latter has shown some applicability in de-
veloping somewhat functional NPs up until now. An approach of minimising reaction
volumes is equally suggested in the eﬀort to increase reaction and development yields. As
small linked NP-volumes around 5-15 µl have been utilised for the coupling of Cat G, it is
likely that reaction yields could be heightened by lowering overall reactions volumes from
mililiter to microﬂuidic levels in order to enhance the likelihood of reaction and coupling
occurrence. Hence, multiple approaches to optimising the steps of NP-Cat G conjugation
and overall NP development exist with numerous aspects of the process to be tweaked.
The successful development of a highly potent Cat G-conjugated NP-system with strong
capability towards IL-6 elimination is therefore undoubtedly achievable.
With the experiment involving LPS-stimulated macrophages and subsequent treatment of
these with Cat G, the prospects of Cat G-administration are clearly seen. By the delivery
of Cat G to the IL-6 secreting cells, cleavage and indisputable decrease in IL-6 levels
are evident. It is proposed, that even superior results and eﬀects would have been seen
following administration of Cat G-conjugated NPs, had the development of the NP-Cat
G system been conducted with a higher degree of success. This is substantiated by the
fact that following NP-surface enzyme immobilisation, an increased enzyme density is
typically achieved. Thus, by administration of a small quantity of successfully developed
NP-Cat G particles, the impact of the highly potent NP-system would exceed the eﬀects
of extracellular cleavage of IL-6 seen for administration of free Cat G. In addition, with
the delivery of NP-coupled Cat G, rather than free Cat G, a series of advantages related
to the aspects of NP delivery are obtained. These include among others enhanced enzyme
density within a small volume, prolonged circulation times in vivo together with decreased
enzymatic degradation and utilisation of the EPR eﬀect for tumour accumulation etc.,
all described in Section 2.8.1. Successful development of the NP-Cat G system, to higher
extents that what has been achieved during this work, is therefore critical for delivery of a
sustainable treatment option in the shape of a highly potent polymeric nanobiocatalytic
system with persistent activity towards degradation and ablation of pro-inﬂammatory,
pro-tumourigenic and metastasis-inducing IL-6.
100
==PAGE:121==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION & FUTURE OUTLOOK
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
6.4
Future outlook
The development of a nanobiocatalytic system for ablation of cancer inﬂammatory sig-
nalling comes with great potential, not only in vitro but in particular in vivo. But whereas
the developed NP system in vitro only interacts with the co-incubated IL-6, the story is
diﬀerent in vivo. Here, delivery of a NP-system with catalytic speciﬁcity towards IL-6
would interact with IL-6 systemically and not limited to pro-tumourigenic IL-6 in the
TME. Systemic eﬀects of IL-6 cleavage would thus have an extensive, negative eﬀect on
the natural immune system, as IL-6 is a key factor in providing a natural immune re-
sponse, described in Section 2.2, aspects that are also seen for systemically administrated
mAbs under development in clinics, covered in Section 2.3. Furhtermore, due to the broad
speciﬁcity of Cat G, the NP-system would likely have speciﬁcity towards other targets
than IL-6 as well. Systemic eﬀects of the NP system would thus entail comprehensive
side-eﬀects.
Figure 6.1: Schematic overview of the fully developed nanobiocatalytic NP-
Cat G system encapsulated by a PEGylated polymeric capping. In vivo the
encapsulation layer will undergo MMP-cleaving, exposing the NP-Cat G com-
plex for cleaving of IL-6 in the TME.
To deal with the issue of toxicity as a result of systemic and side eﬀects, the fully developed
NP-system for in vivo administration will include the incorporation of an MMP-cleavable
PEGylated polymeric encapsulation layer. This layer will cap the Cat G-conjugated poly-
meric core and upon passive targeting of the tumour site based on exploitation of the EPR
eﬀect accessing the TME. As the capping will be composed PEG-polymer monomers, the
NP will possess stealth-like characteristics and decreased susceptibility to uptake and
clearance by MØs, DCs and PMNs [14]. In the TME the polymeric capping will un-
dergo tumour MMP cleaving, exposing the NP-Cat G complex, based on the increased
expression and activity of MMPs in tumours [26, 27, 28]. An MMP cleavable encapsula-
tion surrounding the NP system thus prohibits systemic eﬀects while allowing for passive
targeting of NPS to the tumour site and thus tumour speciﬁc activity. The process of
disintegration of the polymer encapsulation by MMP activity and exposure of the under-
lying catalytic NP-system is schematically illustrated in Figure 6.1.
An additional step to the further NP system development is the integration of a speciﬁc
mAb, actively targeting IL-6. Either a mAb under development or already approved, see
Table A.1, could be implemented to further improve the speciﬁcity towards IL-6. The
combined system would thus allow for both a ’bait and prey’ approach, binding IL-6 by
mAbs and subsequently cleaving IL-6 by Cat G. The speciﬁc choice of mAb for application
would require deeper insight into the speciﬁcities of each mAb and greater understanding
101
==PAGE:122==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION & FUTURE OUTLOOK
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
of what is required for the mAb of choice in the NP system.
Furthermore, as the cores of polymeric NPs are susceptible to incorporation and load-
ing of additional, already developed anti-cancer therapeutics, the aspects of drug co-
administration are equally considered. As the targeted NP system here is focused towards
inhibiting cancer metastasis, by co-administration of an anti-cancer drug simultaneously
to nanobiocatalytic activity a highly eﬀective one-two punch concept could be targeted.
This concurrent drug delivery approach would incorporate and exploit the advantages,
impacts and eﬀectiveness of anti-tumour treatment while limiting the serious side-eﬀects,
characteristic for anti-cancer drugs.
The integration of both mAbs with speciﬁcity towards IL-6 and development of an MMP-
cleavable encapsulation layer provides great potential with high IL-6 speciﬁcity, passive
tumour targeting in addition to limited systemic side eﬀects and thus promising aspects
to the further development of an in vivo-eﬀective nanobiocatalytic system for tumour-
speciﬁc ablation of cancer inﬂammatory signalling.
102
==PAGE:123==
Chapter 7
Conclusion
IL-6 plays a major role as a pro-inﬂammatory cyotkine in promoting tumour metastasis
initiation and development [1, 2]. By activation of the JAK-STAT3 and NF-κB pathways,
IL-6 induces enhanced tumour cell proliferation, survival and invasion while promoting
EMT and angiogenesis, allowing for and increasing the odds of metastasis of tumour cells
[1, 2, 21]. The tumourigenic eﬀects of IL-6 and its role in cancer development are thus
clear.
To combat the pro-inﬂammatory and tumour-promoting eﬀects of IL-6, the goal has been
to develop a nanobiocatalytic system for cancer inﬂammatory signalling ablation. Suit-
able characteristics of biodegradable nanomaterials and traits of spherical NPs such as
increased circulation times compared to free-enzyme administration, utilisation of tumour
EPR eﬀect allowing passive targeting and possible surface conjugation have been exploited
in this eﬀort [14, 15]. The conjugation of the neurophil-derived serine protease Cat G to
NP-surfaces has been targeted due to the potent activity of Cat G towards cleaving IL-6
and thus eliminating downstream, intracellular signalling of IL-6 [23].
Cat G has here been shown to be highly useful in cleaving IL-6 in experimental setups.
By the use of SDS-PAGE and enzyme kinetics studies, freely administrated Cat G has
been proven to present exceptional catalytic ability and eﬀect towards IL-6 disruption in
vitro. Observations from SDS-PAGE indicate a time-dependent decrease in intact IL-6
following Cat G-incubation with eﬀects of Cat G towards IL-6 cleavage visible already
after 15 min. Concurrently, enzyme kinetics studies have shown signiﬁcant activity of Cat
G towards a speciﬁc IL-6-resembling colorimetric substrate allowing for determination of
enzyme kinetic parameters by use of Michaelis-Menten enzyme kinetics. In comparison to
literature the deduced parameters here generally exceed the previously reported, indicat-
ing higher speciﬁcity of the applied Cat G. On the other hand, the techniques of ELISA
and MALDI-TOF have been shown to be unsuitable in diﬀerentiating between cleaved
and intact IL-6 and detecting intact IL-6 in samples, respectively.
By use and nanoprecipitation of PLGA-based polymers with functional endgroups such
as −NH2 and −COOH, biodegradable polymeric cores have been prepared. Generally,
these developed, monodisperse polymeric NP cores exhibit diameters in the range of
70-130 nm and ζ of -25-55 mV. This nanometer and NP charge range is particularly
favourable, as it allows for exploiting the EPR eﬀect in tumours by passive targeting.
103
==PAGE:124==
CHAPTER 7. CONCLUSION
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
Although great batch-to-batch variation have been observed between prepared batches of
polymeric NP cores, the here ultimately optimised approach and procedures have yielded
reproducibility in developing stable cores applicable for enzyme conjugation with suitable
biophysicochemical characteristics and properties.
Whereas successful development and repetitive preparation of polymeric NP cores have
been achieved, ensuing bioconjugation of Cat G to NP-surfaces have posed a serious chal-
lenge. Despite applying multiple diﬀerent approaches utilising various crosslinkers and
mechanisms, little success and only minuscule yields have been achieved in coupling Cat
G to core NP-surfaces. Approaches of enzyme coupling using linkers such as SPDP, NHS-
PEG4-Mal and EDC-NHS-based linked have been exercised, but only by using extensive
reagent excess and reaction times, some successful NP-Cat G conjugation has been ob-
tained by application of EDC-NHS-based conjugation approach. With a maximum [Cat
G] coupled to NP-surfaces of 1.89 µg/ml, limited yield of succesfully developed nanobio-
catalytic NPs have been achieved.
Based on the established proof-of-concept for the applicability of SDS-PAGE and en-
zyme kinetics studies, nanobiocatalytic activity of the developed NP-system in compar-
ison to freely administered enzyme has been assessed. Even though limited NP-Cat G-
conjugation have been accomplished, developed catalytic NPs have demonstrated eﬀect in
cleaving IL-6, determined by SDS-PAGE, as well as catalytic eﬃciency in enzyme kinetic
studies. In comparison with the catalytic activity of free Cat G, developed NPs have
been seen to exhibit an unsatisfactory potency both in the time-dependent cleavage IL-6
and by determined enzyme kinetic parameters: Similar amounts of cleaved IL-6 by Cat
G-conjugated NPs have been observed after 3 h incubation as after incubation with free
Cat G for 15 min, whereas Vmax and kcat/KM measures have been drastically impaired
after NP-conjugation of Cat G. However, the observed activities are still particularly in-
teresting in relation to the limited success of Cat G-conjugations, since even NP-[Cat G]
at this low order of magnitude can exhibit a concrete and measurable catalytic activity,
in particular evident from preserved kcat measures between kinetics studies with free- and
NP-Cat G. Although NP-Cat G activity is still limited and the hypothesised development
of biocatalytic NPs has not been achieved to the desired extent, the observed trends pro-
vide a promising aspect to the further development of biocatalytic NPs, as a signiﬁcant
increase in activity must be anticipated following successful preparation of NPs, poten-
tially surpassing the activity seen for free Cat G.
Cat G has been proven to present a strong eﬀect on reducing levels of IL-6 secreted from
inﬂammatory cells. Due to the limited catalytic activity of developed NPs, free Cat G was
applied for impact investigation on a more complex in vitro system involving cells. Fol-
lowing free Cat G administration, the promising results of changed cellular morphologies
from stimulated and intercommunicating to inattentive cells, in addition to signiﬁcant de-
creases in extracellular levels of secreted of IL-6, showcase the would-be expected impact
of fully developed NP-Cat G treatment. In addition, the performance of a potent NP-Cat
G system would likely surpass the cellular eﬀects seen for free Cat G as conjugation of Cat
G onto NP-surfaces exploits the generically high surface-to-volume ratio of spherical NPs.
This would provide an exceptional increase in enzyme-density, while allowing enhanced
IL-6 attraction to NP surface, compared to administration and treatment utilising free
104
==PAGE:125==
CHAPTER 7. CONCLUSION
Jeppe Sejerø Holm
enzyme [66, 67].
It is clear that immobilisation of Cat G onto NP-surfaces, in addition to future aspects
of integration of an MMP-cleavable polymeric encapsulation, provides the basis for the
further development of a highly potent, tumour-targeting polymeric nanobiocatalytic sys-
tem with excellent enzymatic activity, irreversibly destructing pro-tumourigenic and pro-
inﬂammatory eﬀects of IL-6. The impending and successful development of such treat-
ment option is therefore with great potential in achieving the target of ablation of cancer
inﬂammatory signalling and obstruction of cancer metastasis, ultimately providing a key
polymeric nanobiocatalytic therapeutic in the ongoing battle against cancer.
105
